THE BED PAGE.
“The Outcast of the Islands ” is a story of the
Malay archipelago, 20 or 30 years ago. Peter
Willems, the chief character, is the most dramatic
figure Conrad has yet described or created. He
is a man proud of himself, and of his position as
confidential-clerk to an important Dutch trader
at Macassar. In a moment of intellectual weak-
ness, he helps himself to his employer’s money;
is found out, and contemptuously discharged.
His half-caste wife jeers him ; the thought of his
social fall is insupportable ; and he is on the point
of suicide when his first patron, Captain Lingard,
takes him away to Borneo. Lingard is a tender-
hearted old sea-dog, of immense local repute as
trader and navigator ; and to his pet trading-place,
a river so difficult of entrance that he has hitherto
defied competition, Willems is brought. When
Lingard leaves, exhorting patience, Willems
quarrels with Almayer, Lingard’s jealous
agent. His empty life so weakens white pride
that he is caught by intoxicating passion for a
Malay girl, Aissa. The Malays and Arabs work
upon this passion to induce Williams to betray
Lingard’s secret river-entrance ; and the old man,
returning with Willems’s wife and projects for his
rehabilitation, becomes Willems’s bitter enemy.
The sweetness is gone from Aissa, and Willems
sees his position with horror. He purposes flight
with his wife—who represents for him regained
manhood, self-respect, civilised status —but is
shot by the jealous Aissa. So the book ends.
“ Almayer’s Folly ” introduces some of the same
characters at a later day. The plot revolves round
Lingard’s agent, Almayer, whose stupidity and
selfishness helped to. drive Willems to his tragic
fate. Almayer also is Dutch, and he endures
Bornean exile for the sake of his half-Malay
daughter, the idol of his heart. She loves him,
but her instincts as woman and savage draw her
irresistibly to fly with Daln, a Malay prineelet.
Almayer dies miserably.
* * * *
If in these stories the author has had a definite
aim, it was to present a picture of the semi-savage
life of the Archipelago at the point of contact
with white races. He has succeeded splendidly.
But the sombre note is personal to Conrad. His
atmosphere of gloom never alters ; all his paths
tend to disaster. Even his language has a grave
and sighing cadence : the picture is black against
a background of grey. This is comment merely,
not complaint; for an author chooses what aspect
of his subject he will, and Conrad has chosen to
show what is probably the dominating aspect.
But, after all, one sees in life what one looks to
see. The melancholy in Conrad’s books is the
reflection of his own.
* * * *
Conrad, in fact, is very like Loti. He has eyes,
and can put his visions in words now pale, now
fiery ; but those visions are always less of in-
dividuals than of puppets in the life-drama—
strange figures joyous or sad, but most sad when
most joyous, seen dimly walking through the
mystery of the world ; tragic shadows for a
moment obscuring the stars. Conrad’s effects,
like Loti’s, are less those of fiction than of poetry.
The characters come and go in the thraldom of
circumstance, and behind them looms the curious
earth, the sinister sea, the profound and secret sky.
They fade, and of them all remains only the sense
of the burden of human fate, the impression of in-
communicable grief. Like Spinoza, like Rossetti,
like all the race of mystics, these men see life sub
specie eeternitatis, see through the temporary into
the continual, beyond the appearance into the
essence, past the trivial individual destiny to the
general and predestined doom. And their books
have the flavor of themselves, are vain attempts
to shape and color and place within others’ grasp
the dreams which themselves have hardly seen or
touched, which they never have succeeded in
seizing.
* * * *
Like all his artistic kindred, Conrad has keen
sense of the color, the shape, the visible beauty of
words. His sense of their sound is dull—or he
has not had time or inclination for the difficult-
delicious task of adding sound-impression to
color-impression. His sentences, indeed, swing
to a peculiar music ; but his words are mute. In
the interesting note attached to “ The Nigger of
N arcissus,” when published in The New Review;
Conrad declared that “ fiction— if it at all aspires
to be art—appeals to temperament. . . . The
artistic aim when expressing itself in written
words . . must strenuously aspire to the plas-
ticity of sculpture, to the color of painting, and
to the magic suggestiveness of music—which is the
art of arts. And it is only through complete, un-
swerving devotion to the perfect blending of form
and substance ; it is only through an unremitting,
never-discouraged care for the shape and ring of
sentences, that an approach pan be made to
plasticity, to color, and the light of magic sug-
gestiveness may be brought to play for an evanes-
cent instant over the commonplace surface of
words—of the old, old words, worn thin, defaced
by ages of careless usage.” And Conrad con-
tinues—
To snatch in a moment of courage, from the remorse-
less rush of lime, a passing phase of life, is only the begin-
ning of the task. The task approached in tenderness and
faith is to hold up iniquestioningly, without choice and
without fear, the rescued fragment before all ejes and in
the light of-a sincere mood. It is to show its vibration,
ita colour, its form ; and through its movement, its form,
and itfi colour, reveal the substance of its truth —disclose
its inspiring secret: the stress and passion within the
core of each convincing moment. In a single-minded
attempt of that kind, if one be deserving and fortunate,
one may perchance attain to such clearness of sincerity
that at last the presented vision of regret or pity, of
terror or mirth, shall awaken in the hearts of the be-
holders that feeling of unavoidable solidarity ; of the
solidarity in mysterious origin, in toil, in joy, in hope, in
uncertain fate, which binds men to each other and all
mankind to the visible world.
To arrest, for the space of a breath, the hands busy
about the work of the earth, and compel men entranced
by the sight of distant goals to glance for a moment at
the surrounding vision of form and colour, of sunshine
and shadows ; to make them pause for a look, for a sigh,
for a smile—such is the aim, difficult and evanescent, and
reserved only for a very few to achieve. But sometimes,
by the deserving and the fortunate, even that task is
accomplished. And when it is accomplished—behold !
all the truth of life is there: a moment of vision, a sigh,
a smile—and the return to an eternal rest.
That is Conrad’s theory of fiction ; and the pass-
age eloquently represents his style. He swims in
a flood of harmonious sentences, whose waves of
words are blown higher and higher by the need
which his temperament feels of making its impres-
sions clear to itself, of transferring them intact
in form and weight and hue to the temperament
of his readers. “The Nigger of the Narcissus”
is a later and deliberate effort to make the theory
valid. It is a sea-dithyramb, a phantasmagorio
vision of strange vague sea-figures moved across a
lighted circle into blackness. Certainly the
author has realised his intention. The reader
sees the crew of the Narcissus as Conrad’s
memory sees them, through a glamor of fantastic
wizardry—“a crew of shades” who “pass and
make a sign, in a shadowy hail.” No book gives
a better impression of certain phases of the sea
and sea-life as viewed through an individual tem-
perament. And, after all, the book remains de-
tached, episodic—a section of the universe cut
cleanly out of its surroundings, varnished, framed,
and hung on the wall of literature. Mr. Conrad’s
dogma has played him false. What he has accom-
plished is a tour de force and nothing more. What
he should have accomplished, to reach the highest
plane of art, was not to bring a bit of universe
to the reader, but to take the reader to the uni-
verse. You may not see any more of the universe ;
but at least you do not see the picture-frame and
the limits of the wall.
* • • •
There are two stories in Conrad’s last book,
“Tales of Unrest,” which show the asked-for diffe-
rence in method. One of them, “ The Return,”
is spoilt by that accumulating of words which is
Conrad’s besetting sin. (He is not intellectually
profound, and his words, apart from their intrinsic
effect, have, as a rule, only an emotion to justify
them —not a thought. Often Conrad labors his
emotion too long.) The other, “The Idiots,” is
•ne of the best short-stories extant; and, though
only a short-story, it makes a more definite, a
more abiding, and a more artistio impres-
sion than the six-times-as-long “Nigger of
the Narcissus. Only the briefest abstract
can be given. The actors are French peasants.
The first scene shows the impression made on a
tourist by four idiot children met by the roadside.
Later he learns their tragedy. Coring Jean-Pierre,
returning from his military service, found the old
people very much aged and the farm neglected.
His peasant’s devotion to the land impelled him
to marry at once, that fresh hands might come to
till and care for it. Twins were born—they were
both idiots. The blow was heavy. When a third
child came the parents watched it closely. That
child, like the other two, never smiled, never
stretched its hands to the mother, never spoke,
Jean-Pierre pondered dully, with fire at his heart
of clods. His wife’s mother persuaded him, the
atheist, to go to mass. A year or so afterwards a
fourth child was horn. That child turned out an
idiot too. Jean-Pierre is desperate. His wife’s
mother, sitting one autumn evening, is confronted
by her daughter : “I have killed him.” To her
“No! It’s impossible! Why?” the daughter
speaks—
“ Theere’s no mercy in heaven—no justice. No i. . .
I did not know. ... Do you think I have no heart? Do
you think I have never heard people jeering at me,
pitying me, wondering at me? Do you know how some
of them were calling me ? The mother of idiots-that
was my nickname ! And my children never would know
me, never speak to me. They would know nothing ;
neither men—nor God. Haven’t I prayed ! But the
Mother of God herself would not hear me. A mother !
. . . Who is accursed—l, or the man who is dead? Eh?
Tell me. I took care of myself. Do you think I would
defy the anger of God and have my house full of those
things—that are worse than animals who know the hand
that feeds them ? Who blasphemed in the night at the
very church door? Was it I? ... I only wept and
prayed for mercy . . . and I feel the curse at every
moment of the day—l S"e it round me from morning to
night . . . I've got to keep them alive—to take care of
my misfortune and shame. And he would come. I
begged him and H-aven for mercy. . . No ! . . . Then
we shall see. . . . He caine this evening. I thought to
myself: ‘Ah! again 1’ . . . I had my long scissors. I
heard him shouting. ... I saw him near. ... I must
—must I? . . . Then take! . . . And I struck him in
the throat above the breast bone. ... I never iieard
him even sigh. ... I left him standing. ... It was a
minute ago. llovv did I come here ? ”
Horror climaxes thus ; the rest tells of the wife’s
death, half-suicidal, by falling in the sea. The
fragment has the force of Aeschylus.
B.C. : Justification of the charge that Prof.
Morris’s “ Austral Dictionary ” is utterly misleading to
aliens comes in Literature of March 19. “ What, for
instance,” asks the critic, “ would the ordinary news-
paper reader in London make of such a head-line as
‘Latest about the Cretan Corroboree,’ which appeared in
the Sydney Herald during the Cretan troubles in the
spring of last year?” Presumably Morris meant Melb.
Evg. Herald ; but in his airy, inaccurate way he put the
“ Herald ” merely—and Grandmother gets the blame !
Again: “The sundowner, whose very name suggests
twilight, is the tramp or plunderer of the west, while the
‘ Kanaka,’ or South Sea Islander, is the accomplished
thief [and of the west!]. The latter adores women, and is
benevolently inclined to ohildren [as Q. sugar-town police-
records show], but these excusable weaknesses do not
hinder him in his habit of pilfering, and his native quick-
ness of intelligence, sharpened in the pursuit of his ill-
gotten gains, renders valuable assistance to enterprising
Europeans opening up the country.” Of larrikin . . .
“it is neither more nor less than the word * larking ’
pronounced in broad lush ‘fashion.’” And so on.
Poor Australia!
Re ‘ Never-Never,’ Chaa. J. Turner writes from
Apis Creek, C.Q. : —George Hobler, once well-known on
the Hunter Itiver, N.S. W., took up the Weewaa station,
on the Namoi, in the early forties. His eldest son (my
cousin) told me that he considered Never-Never an
aboriginal phrase meaning the far interior, because when-
ever lie questioned the blacks on his father’s station re-
garding the interior they referred to it as ‘ Niver Niver.’
But ‘O’B.’ writes: Major De Winton, a one-
time lieutenant in Wynyard Square Barracks, Sydney,
whose name, ‘ Winton,’ was given to the bush settlement
in Queensland’s Never Never, still haunts the military
clubs in London, and has been publishing his remini-
scences in an English magazine. According to him, low
pubs, in Chatham (Eng.) 50 or 60 years ago were known
among soldiers as ‘ Never’(? Never Never), because they
never closed—were kept open day and night. Pubs, in
Australian Never Never similarly defy licensing laws.
Query: Is the Australian Never Never simply the land
where onoe the shanties never shut ?
“ Carrying your swag,” “ humping bluey,”
“.carrying Matilda,” “ pushing the knot ” any others?
And derivation of last?
Scotty : What is the derivation of “So long” as
an adieu? Dr. Burns, of New Orleans (in ’7O when
first it came out) claimed “ Salaam ” as the origin. In
the hush the general expressions now are “Be good!”
and “ Hooray ! ”
7x7: Between the lower waters of the Namoi
and the Castlereagb, the word “ brushing ” is used in the
sense of “ narked,” or “ out of temper.” Tradition says
usage originated in a oertain cocky, when angry, shamb-
ling with his trouser legs brushing, whioh makes the word
all the more funny when applied to a petticoat-wearer.
“ Brushing” is peculiar to the locality mentioned, and is
not understood elsewhere. In New England, for example,
“narked” is the word for the same idea. Slang varies
with locality. On the N.S. W. plains, say from Liverpool
to Bourke, the word “alright” is used by bushmen as
an adjective ; and it means a lot, too. Butin some towns
the word is changed into “ all-cigarnette,” and in others
into “all cusli” (? derivations). In many places the
salutation “good-day” or “good-night” is simply
“Hooray! ” —day or night time is all the same to the
bushman. I haven’t seen noted the common expression,
“ fair into my tomahawk,” meaning “ it i” 1 suits me.”
B.E. : Is the local preferetice for participles
new or Australian ? Heard a Sydney observe to a
stone-throwing companion, “ You didn't shot it far
enough;” to which the reply, “I can shot it as far as
you!” [“Shot” is common Australee.J Also, our
slavey yesterday in a back-fence confidence: “ He needn’t
think he’s going to left me.”

The Australian Slanguage.
’Tis the everyday Australian
Has a language of his own,
Has a language, or a slanguage,
Which can simply stand alone ;
And “ a dickon pitch to kid us”
Is a synonym for “ liei”
And to “ nark it ” means to stop it,
And to “ nit it ” means to fly.
And a bosom friend’s a “cobber,”
And a horse a “ prad ” or “ moke,’*
While a casual acquaintance
Is a “joker ” or a “ bloke.”
And his lady-love’s his “ donah ”
Or his “ clinah ” or his “ tart ”
Or his “ little bit o’ muslin,"
As it used to be his “ bart.”
And his naming of the coinage
Is a mystery to some,
With his “quid” and “half-a-easer"
And his “ deener ” and his ‘‘ scrum ” I
And a “tin-back ” is a party
Who’s remarkable for luck,
And his food is called his “ tucker"
Or his “ panem ” or his “ chuck."
A policeman is a “ johnny ”
Or a “ copman ” or a “ trap,”
And a thing obtained on credit
Is invariably “strap.”
A conviction’s known as “ trouble,"
And a gaol is called a “jug,”
And a sharper is a “ spieler,”
And a simpleton’s a “ tug.”
If he hits a man in fighting,
That is what he calls a “plug,”
If he borrows money of you
He will say he “ bit your lug."
And to “ shake it ” is to steal it,
And to “strike it ” is to beg,
And a jest is “ poking borao ” ..
And the jester “ pulls your leg."
Things are “ cronk ” when they go wrongly
In the language of the “ push,”
But when things go as he wants ’em
He declares it is “ all cush.”
When he’s bright he’s “ got a napper, : "
But he’s “ ratty ” when he’s daft,
And when looking for employment
He is “ out o’ blooming graft.”
And his clothes he call 9 his “ clobber *•
Or his “ togs,” but what of that
When a “ castor ” or a “ kady ”
Is the name Jhe gives his hat!
And our undiluted English
Is a fad to which we cling,
But the great Australian slanguage
Is a truly awful thing !
W.T. GOODGB in Orange Leader.
Vagabond Verses.
I have had enough of women, and enough of love,
But the land waits, and the sea waits, and day and night
is enough:
Give me a long white road, and the grey wide path of the
sea,
And the wind’s will and the bird’s will, and the heartache
still in me.
Why should I seek out sorrow, and give gold for strife?
I have loved much and wept much, hut tears and love are
not life ;
The grass calls to my heart, and the foam to my blood
cries up, 7/’
And the sun shines and the road shines, and the wine’s in
the cup.
I have had enough of wisdom, and enough of mirth,
For the way’s one and the end’s one, and it’s soon to the
ends of the earth ;
And it’s then good : night and to bed, and if heels or heart
ache,
Well, it’s sound sleep and long sleep, and sleep too deep
to wake.
ARTHOR SYMONS.
Where forlorn sunsets flare and fade
Oh desolate seajand lonely sand,
Out of the silence and the shade
What is the voice of strange command
Calling you still, as friend calls friend
With love that cannot brook delay,
To rise and follow the ways that wend
Over the hills and far away ?
Hark in the city, street on street,
A roaring reach of death and life,
Of vortices that clash and fleet
And ruin in appointed strife,
Hark to it calling, calling clear,
Calling until you cannot stay
From dearer things than your own most dear
Over the hills and far away.
Out of the sound of the ebb-and-flow,
Out of the sight of lamp and star,
It calls you where the good winds blow,
And the unchanging meadows are:
From faded hopes and hopes agleam,
It oalls you, calls you night and day
Beyond the dark into the dream
Over the hills and far away.
W. M RENLEI.
I am fevered with the sunset,
I am fretful with the bay,
For the wander-thirst is on me
And my soul is in Cathay,
There’s a schooner in the offing,
With her topsails shot with (ire,
And ray heart has gone aboard her
For the Islands of Desire.
I must forth again to-morrow 1
With the sunset I must be
Hull-down on the trail of rapture
In the wonder of the sea.
CARMAN or HOVEV.

Failing Manhood.
When your manhood begins
to go you will twitch and show
signs of premature weakness.
Following are some of the symp-
toms :
Do your faoil nerves twitch ?
Are you all run down ?
Spots before the eyes?
Do the muscles twitch ?
Have you pains in small of back ?
Have you pains in head ?
Have you shooting pains?
Do you feel fatigued?
Are you weary ?
Does your head swim ?
Do your knees knock?
Does your hand tremble?
Nervous Twitchings.
A nervous man easily gets all
of a tremble, gets fits of weak-
ness, fits of twitchings. Here are
some of his symptoms :
Do your eyes twitch?
Do your hands tremble ?
Do you feel shaky ?
Can you walk without halting ?
Are you melancholy?
Do you have ringing in the ears ?
Is your blood poor ?
Are your eyes weak?
Are your eyes bleary ?
Does your head swim?
Nervous Debility-
This affliction destroys ambi-
tion, organic strength, energy,
and hope. Following are some
of the symptoms :
Do you feel weak?
Have you cold feet?
Have you backache ?
Do you shun society ?
Are you losing flesh ?
Do you sleep poorly?
Are you low-spirited?
Are your eyes sunken ?
Do you have hot flushes ?
Is your memory impaired ?
Have you no manly vigor?
Have you no vital energy?
Do you have sick headaches?
Is there nausea after eating ?
Do your hands or feet sweat?
Has'the brightness left your eyes?
Do you feel unfit to marry?

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Grey Day.
The still grey day is ending
In hollow and on height,
The lighthouse seaward sending
White rays of steady light :
A little cloud is leading
A great cloud west by north ;
Woe waits on ships unheeding
That blindly venture forth.
All day the sea, dull-heaving,
Moaned low like one who ail '.
While spectre-hands were weaving
A veil o'er distant sails.
All day with drooping feather
And wings devoid of gleam,
The sea-birds grouped together
Forbore to wheel and scream.
Salt-arms and river-reaches
Were glazed and leaden-hued,
And, haunting sodden beaches,
Went grey-haired Solitude.
The dead leaves in the forest
Sank earthward all aswoon,
The green marsh-frogs that chorus d
Had ta’en a sadder tune.
Lost loves and sins long hidden,
Through some unguarded gate,
Entered the soul unbidden
And made men desolate.
And fears beset the fearless,
And laughs were stayed to sigh,
And eyes long dry and tearless
Grew moist, and none knew why.
Gleamed red the covered ember
Beneath its ashen grey,
And some said, “ I remember,”
And some, “ ’Twas such a day ! ”
And all were lonely-hearted,
Sight innard-set and blurred,
At touch or tone they started
And groped for fitting word.
Down-cast, in weeds, went Nature,
Stilling man’s mirth and song ;
And mourning through every creature
A grave and ancient wrong.
Light fades on hill and hollow,
Night falls, and close behind,
Storm-rage and seawrath follow
With wild cries on the wind.
RODERIC QUINN.
Nunc Dimittis.
Lord, let me go before this sweetness closes,
Before my quickened heart is dust again,
One morning hour be mine among the roses,
Heryoung arms round me, her young lips astrain;
But ere I mar her pleasure with my pain,
Lord, let me go !
Lord, let me go ! My heart is old and jaded,
I cannot cleanse it of its early stain ;
The glory of to-day will soon be faded, _
Her lips will cling, her arms enclose, in vain j
Before I mar her pleasure with my pain,
Lord, let me go !
f |1»» m T> a kvr
THE PAGAN.
Satana.
She draws all men to serve her, and her lure
Is her pulsating, human loveliness:
The beauty of her bosom’s rippling lines,
The passion pleading in her eyes, the pure
Soft contour of her cheek, her dainty dress,
With all the rich aroma of her warm,
Lush womanhood perfumed, her supple form
Curving and swaying like a living flower,
Aflush with life and youth. These are the
signs
By which she queens the hearts of men, the
power
By which she makes her sovereignty secure !
But though her red lips mock me of their wine,
And that low laugh of hers fills me with fire,
As, spent with loving, in her scorn I lie ;
Yet some day she will come to me and twine
Her slender arms about me ; and desire
Will plead in those eyes that were all disdain,
And break her bosom with a sob of pain,
And her hot lips will lavish all their store
Of wanton kisses on me—then shall I
Remember all her queenly coldness, or
With kisses make her breathing beauty mine ?
T. TO F.
The Rose out of Reach.
A red rose grew on a southward wall,
There was never a rose on the tree so tall;
Though roses twined at iny lingering feet
Roses and roses, scented sweet,
And roses bent to my love-lit eyes,
Roses flaming in wanton guise,
And roses swung at my shoulder height,
Damask and crimson and golden and white,
With a curse for all and a frown for each
I longed for the rose beyond my reach.
The gold sun shone in the summer days,
The wee buds opened a hundred ways ;
Winds of the morning whispering sweet,
Tossed the blown roses down at my feet,
Dainty petals for lover’s tread,
Ruby and ivory—brown and dead !
But morning to nooning, noon to night,
One rose only glowed in my sight—
Silently, all too rapt for speech,
I worshipped the rose beyond my reach.
I stormed her tower on the southward wall,
To drop fatigued from the bastions tall,
Thorns made sport of me, red as the rose,
A hundred wounds ran biood at their blows,
The soft little roses red and white
Changed to the bitterest foes in spite,
Scourged my face with their stinging wands,
Mocked my toil and my bleeding hands
Till I learned at last what they strove to teach
The great red rose was beyond my reach.
And so I watched in the autumn days,
“ Summer is dead,” so I mused, a-gaze,
“ The cold mists creep when the night is nigh,
Day after day the roses die,
Storms of winter will gather soon
Frosts will follow the coining moon,
Here if I wait where the blooms are cast
My love will drop to my arms at last.”
But wild winds laden with death for each
Blew the red petals beyond my reach.
WILL H. OGILVIE.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
'Correspondence” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But ice
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

Westralia and Maor Hand.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is now
£l, with 2s. 2d. added for postage. England £l, with
6s. 6d. added for postage.

prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.

SATURDAY, JUNE 4, 1898.
The Case for the Bill.
On Friday, June 3, New South Wales, Vic-
toria and Tasmania have to decide whether
they will or willnotgive the requisite majority
for the acceptance of the Federal Constitu-
tion. South Australia follows on Saturday,
June 4, and Westralia is due at some as yet
unfixed date. Under these circumstances,
The Bulletin takes this opportunity to once
more sum up the Case for the Bill :
(1) It is agreed on all hands that Australia
wants Federation. Even the men who scoffed
at any kind of union when the Enabling Bill
was under discussion now allege that they are,
and always have been, true Federationists, and
are not against the movement itself, but only
against the present scheme. Their past career
certainly belies them, but they say so.
(2) There is no possible guarantee that,
if Australia loses this chance of union,
it will get another. No one knows what
there is in the future. The confident as-
surance of Heydon and Want and Reid
and Hiogins and Ebenezer Ward and
others, that there will be plenty more chances,
and so the loss of this one doesn’t matter, goes
for nothing. Heydon isn’t the Deity, nor
anything resembling Him ; neither is Want, or
Reid, or Higgins.
(3) If Australia rejects this Bill, and does get
another, the next one may be worse instead of
I letter. Heydon, Want and co. say it will be
better, but, again, neither of these gentlemen is
the Omniscient.
(4) Anyhow, the nation which won’t have
any Constitution till it gets a perfect one, will
never have any at all. The best it can hope for
is to get something better than it has, and the
Federal Bill provides that. If the State Con-
stitutions (which are simply awful) were pub-
licly discussed, and carefully overhauled by the
people, they would probably all be rejected by
popular vote. If the public fully understood
the Constitutions it has got, the benefits of the
one olfered to it would become more apparent.
(5) The Federal Constitution provides for the
payment of a sufficient salary to every men) rer
of each House. At present, in New South
Wales, Victoria, Queensland, and Westralia,
Council members are unpaid, therefore on y
men not dependent on their labor for a liveli-
hood can sit, or, if they do sit, can be con-
stantly present to look after the public interests.
One-half the Legislative machine is thus in the
hands of the Rich. In Westralia the Assembly
is also unpaid, and in Tasmania payment is
only nominal, therefore in these two provinces
the Rich practically boss both Houses. The
Federal Constitution gives the Democracy its
first chance to run the whole Legislature.
(6) It abolishes all property qualifications for
members, whereas in Victoria only Rich Land-
lords are eligible to sit in the Council.
(7) It makes both Houses elective on the
same franchise. No plural voting is allowed ;
there is no property qualification of any kind,
and NO PERSON, MAN OR WOMAN,
WHO HAS A VOTE FOR THE ASSEM-
BLY OF HIS OWN STATE CAN BE
DEBARRED FROM HAVING A VOTE
FOR BOTH HOUSES OF THE COMMON-
WEALTH PARLIAMENT. At present the
Victorian, South Australian, Westralian, and
Tasmanian Councils are elected by property-
holders or leaseholders ; the Victorian Assem-
bly is largely bossed by the plural property
vote ; and the people of New South Wales have
no voice whatever in the election of one-half
(the Council) of their Parliament.
(8) Both Houses of the Federal Parliament
can be dissolved and sent to the people, when
they come in collision with the popular will.
At present when the Victorian, Tasmanian,
S. A. or Westralian senator opposes the popular
will the only thing is to wait for various periods
up to nine years, till his term expires. In New
South Wales and Queensland the only remedy
is to kill him, or wait till he dies—generally of
old age.
(9) Therefore the proposed Federal Parlia-
liament will be the only one where both Houses
will be accessible to the people, where they
will be elected solely by the people, and where
they will be responsible to the people.
* * • *
(10) The Federal Constitution provides a
remedy for deadlocks. If the Council refuses
to accept a measure passed by the Assembly,
and after a joint dissolution and re-election it
still refuses, the two Houses sit together, and a
three-fifths majority of the members present
can carry the Bill. This is not a perfect
remedy, still it is a remedy, and as the two
more populous provinces (New South Wales
and Victoria) possess more than the three-fifths
majority, the power ultimately rests with the
bulk of the people. Even a partial re-
medy for deadlocks is better than none.
In New South Wales and Queensland
the only remedy for Council obstruc-
tion is to make a whole lot of new Coun-
cillors (if the Governor permits), or wait till
the old ones die. In the other provinces
the only remedy is to wait many years till the
majority of the Councillors go out by the slow
effluxion of time. And, when they do go out,
the people in general are no better off; the
small gang of propertied electors send the old
crowd back again.
N.B.—The above is the Constitution which
the bitter Tories of Australia—the nominee
Councillors and the men who have been
elected on a narrow property suffrage—ask
the people to reject on the ground that it
isn’t Democratic enough.
* * * *
(11) The Lower House of the Federal Legis-
lature is elected practically on a population
basis. In the Senate, the provinces have six
members each, regardless of population. This
is the system adopted by the Federal Govern-
ments of Switzerland, the United States,
Brazil, Chili, Mexico, Argentina and Venezuela.
It has worked well in all these places, and has
never been regarded as undemocratic. Ger-
many carries the system so much further than
it is proposed to do in Australia that the small-
est German state has 44 tinies as much repre-
sentation (in proportion to population) as the
largest. In Canada, too, the smallest state has
times the proportionate representation of
the largest. Also there are in the Convention-
Bill Constitution the following safeguards:—
(a) The House which represents the majority
of the people has the power of the purse.
(b) If the Lower House refuses to agree to a
measure passed by the Senate the matter
ends there. But if the Senate refuses to
assent to a measure passed b}' the Lower
House the latter can demand a dissolution
of both Houses, and, if the deadlock still
continues, a joint sitting. Therefore, the
House of Representatives, which represents
the majority of people, has a veto on the
one which only represents the majority of
States ; the latter has no veto on the
popular House.
(c) The proposed Constitution gives the
democracies of Tasmania, South Australia,
and Westralia a little more than their fair
share of power for the time, and until
population becomes more equally dis-
tributed. But the democracies of New
South Wales and Victoria will have a
larger share of power than they have now.
At present they run one-half the Govern-
ment, and the Rich Men of their respective
Councils run the other half. A large share
of power is taken from the Rich Men of
New South Wales and Victoria, and a part
of it is transferred by the principle of
equal representation in the Senate, to the
democracies of the three smaller provinces.
(12) The three Democratic provinces of New
South Wales, Victoria, and South Australia
(under the proviso that every person who has a
vote for the Lower House of his own State
shall have a vote for both Federal Houses, and
that no plural vote shall be allowed) will be in
a position to return a vast Democratic majority
in both Federal Houses from the very first.
Any alterations in the Federal franchise will be
made by that Democratic majority. But the
provisos that there shall be no plural vote and
that every person who has a vote for the Lower
House of his own State shall have a vote for
both Federal Houses, are embedded in the Con-
stitution.
(13) The Constitution may be altered by the
vote of a majority of both Houses, a majority
of the people, and a majority of the States,
The Democratic provinces of New South Wales.
Victoria, and South Australia command all
three majorities.
N.B.—The Fat Men of New South Wales
in particular demand that this Constitution
should be rejected because it isn’t sufficiently
Democratic.
* * * *
(14) The most populous province (N.S.W.)
hasn’t enough people to make one city like
London, Paris, Berlin, New York, Vienna,
Pekin, or Canton, and it only keeps ahead of
Philadelphia, Chicago, Tokio, and St. Peters-
burg by a close shave. Compared with the
area to be protected, the strongest of the Seven
Provinces doesn’t amount to much; the weakest
is barely a row of pins. By amalgamating the
separate military departments and creating a
national army, Federation will make the best
of what defences we have.
(15) It will combine five post and telegraph
departments ; make postal rates uniform ; save
the cost of four out of five Postmasters-General,
and greatly reduce working expenses.
(16) It will save the cost of four out of five
Agents-General.
(17) It will create a uniform tariff outside the
Commonwealth, and absolute Freetrade inside.
All the costly and troublesome paraphernalia
of border-customs and customs-officers will be
swept away. By making trade absolutely free
over an entire continent (for the ultimate adhe-
sion of Queensland is inevitable, and the special
arrangement with Westralia lasts only five
years), it will be the greatest Freetrade move-
ment which the world has seen this century.
The Freetrader who is against Federation under
such circumstances is an anomaly.
(18) The Commonwealth Bill gives power to
the Federation to take over the State debts.
And, as the Federal Government’s credit will
be better than that of any individual State, the
Commonwealth will be able to consolidate the
debts at reduced interest, and thus save, pro-
bably, ,£1,000,000 a year. This will be an im-
mense reduction of the public burdens. And
against this the whole cost of the Federal
Government is reckoned, by the N.S.W. Finan-
cial Committee, at .£380,000.
mm* *
(19) The Federal Constitution leaves the
fiscal problem absolutely open, and the ques-
tion of Freetrade or Protection is left to the
people to decide—all the allegations about
“ Bkaddon’s Blot” to the contrary notwith-
standing. Braddon’s famous amendment pro-
vides that one-fourth or any less proportion of
the customs and excise revenue may be re-
tained by the Federal Government towards its
own expenses, and three-fourths must be re-
turned to the States. This leaves the Federal
Government absolutely free to pay its expenses
out of direct taxation, or out of customs and
excise, or partly by one and partly by the other.
(20) The Constitution does not, unfortu-
nately, provide definitely for the taking over of
the railways by the Commonwealth. But it
gives the Commonwealth power to take them
over with the States’ consent. It is proposed
in some quarters that Federation should be
postponed until the federalising of the railways
is arranged. That also means waiting till the
States consent. And there is no reason for
waiting. The federated States will consent
just as soon and just as easily as the unfederated
States would.
(21) The Constitution Bill provides Aus-
tralia, for the first time, with what is practi-
cally a final Court of Appeal of its own.
Hitherto the final Court of Appeal has been in
England, 16,000 miles away, and justice has
been hideously slow and remote in consequence.
(22) It leaves the fixing of the Federal capital
to the Federal Parliament—the Parliament from
which the property-vote is absolutely excluded.
But the Federal capital must be severed from
the State which now owns it, and made into a
separate Federal Territory, therefore it will be
neither in New South Wales, Victoria, South
Australia, Tasmania, or Westralia. It will
simply be a piece of territory cut out from one
of these States, and no separate State will
monopolise the advantages to be derived from
its possession.
(23) Whatever is bad in the Constitution the
people can amend or repair in. the manner
described in Paragraph 13. At present, the
people of New South Wales can’t repair their
Constitution without the consent of the Coun-
cil, which they didn’t make and can’t remove.
Also, the people of the other four federating
provinces can’t repair their Constitutions with-
out the assent of the propertied class which
runs their respective Councils.
* • * *
(24) Under the proposed Federal Constitution
the people of all the provinces will be taxed on
the same scale. All the complicated processes
by which Tories all over Australia have en-
deavored to prove that their particular province
will pay an undue share of the cost can’t alter
this simple and obvious fact.
(25) Federation is the only thing which can
prevent the ultimate dismemberment of New
South Wales. While a great part of the South
is nearer Melbourne than Sydney, and a great
part of the West is nearer Adelaide than
Sydney, and while provincial tariffs shut these
districts off from their nearest market, and
provincial railway management refuses them
nleans of communication with it, they will
always tend to leave New South Wales and
unite with their more conveniently - situated
neighbors. Nothing can make a district
belong naturally to Sydney if another big
city or advantageous port is 100 or 200
miles nearer. If provincialism lasts, these
districts will secede from New South Wales
when they are strong enough to assert
themselves. Federation will remove the tariff
barrier now, and the railway barrier later on,
and destroy all motive for secession.
(26) If New South Wales wrecks this Federal
movement as it did that of ’9l (both of which
were started by its special invitation) it will,
probably, be a long time before the other pro-
vinces listen to further invitations from the
same quarter. »
(27) If another Convention is held, with
Queensland in it, then, instead of three Demo-
cratic and two Tory sets of representatives to
draft the Constitution, there will be three Tory
Governments represented. There will be no
reliable Democratic majority as there was at
the last Convention, and the allegedly
rapacious small provinces will have more
votes, and the next scheme will, probably, be
less liberal than the present one.
The Bulletin presents these reasons to the
electors for their consideration. If anybody
has a better collection to present in the anti-
Billious interest, that collection has, so far.
been very carefully concealed.
For Australia!
It is decided, seemingly, that patriotism is a
sentiment, a tradition, one of those curious
hereditary instincts which humanity, thank
goodness ! is now fast outgrowing. Quite
obviously, patriotism does not pay. It leads an
otherwise sensible individual to peril his liberty
or his life in quarrels which, at best, can only
remotely concern him. And when a man has
only one life, his plain duty to himself is to
make the most of it—to satisfy his tastes and
appetites, to eat and drink and be merry, so
that when death comes all too quickly he can at
least say, Well, I have lived and loved; there
was nothing better—and so, Cood-night! That
a man with health, and friends, and money, and
a bounding joy in mere air and the sun, should
fling all away for a bit of rag on a pole and an
insane shibboleth—as, for example, thousands of
well-to-do Southerners did in America in ’6l -’65
—is clearly the height of human foolishness.
And patriotism is not even in accord with
ethics. Often the so-called patriot fights for a
cause which, as far as unbiassed intelligence
can see, is morally doubtful or morally rotten.
Besides, the mere fact of a man being born
upon some arbitrarily-delimited portion of
earth-surface gives no reason why he should
count other such accidental babies brothers, and
rank as actual or potential enemies the people
of equal personal merit born outside his precious
pale. If love of country be an exalted emotion,
love of humanity is still more exalted. How
mean, how narrow, looks “ Our country, right
or wrong ! ” beside Tom Paine’s grand utter-
ance, “ The world is my country, and to do
good is my religion 1 ” And yet
* * * *
Human nature is human nature still. It may
be outgrowing patriotism, but it has not out-
grown it. Were our intelligences higher or
our emotions purer, as we merge the individual
in the general, the parochial in the national, so
we might be content to merge the national
in the human. But men cannot transcend
themselves. And, as the nineteenth century
closes, the worship of humanity, so lofty in
theory, still seems to the bulk of humanity a
pale and bloodless thing in practice. That
may be a blind and foolish instinct which im-
pels a man to love best his own child, not
another’s ; to love best his own country, not a
stranger’s. Yet we trust in it and follow it,
and—bad cause or good—we are seldom led
astray. For, somehow, to the comjnon intelli-
gence of humanity, the man who gives his
life for a bad cause still seems to have more
elements of the sublime in him than the man who
gives convincing reasons to prove he ought
not to fight. Everybody knows that to shed
blood is no proof of doctrine ; but the man
who sheds his own blood somehow seems to rise
superior to doctrine. He does not really do so,
of course, and it is irrational of human nature
to be even momentarily hoodwinked into dream-
ing that he does. But apparently human nature
cannot help itself : it is built that stupid way.
And it is ardently to be hoped that, whenever
they are called in defence of their country,
rightly or wrongly, logically or illogically,
Australians will fight. At present they are
only asked to vote for their country. That is
not much, but it is a great deal.
* • » »
It is true that Australian patriotism is no
whit more justifiable than patriotism in general.
Australia is beautiful and terrible —there are
other lands as beautiful and terrible. Air as
sweet swims under other skies as fair ; grander
heights frown elsewhere upon deserts still more
desolate than ours. There is no reason why a
man of open mind should choose to dwell here
rather than among the storied scenes of elder
countries, why a man of romantic fancy should
be thrilled and inspired by our spaces and soli-
tudes rather than by the rivers and mountains
of America or Europe. From a cosmopolitan
standpoint, Australia can appear uninviting,
even unlovely. And it may be that united
Australia will act no worthier or braver part
than Australia disunited. The same passions,,
the same prejudices, will govern after union as
before. Ultimate loss there cannot be; but
present profit may be slow of realisation. And
yet
» » * *
Australia is our own country, and the pride
in united Australia will be the safeguard of
our own country. In the streets and lanes of
cities there is little room for the spell of father-
land to work ; but who is there—horn in the
bush, having eaten and slept in the bush, in
whose boyish blood bush scents and sights and
sounds have mingled—who has never thrilled
with passionate exultation that, come what
may, he is part of Australia and she of him ;
that his life has been fed at her generous
sources ; that his spirit is impregnated and
colored by her spirit; that the destiny of him-
self and his children is inextricably involved
with the destiny of this hostile, mysterious,
magnificent island? Who that has ridden
solitary in the bush through the hot day-
silence, or upon a silent night-long path—or,
camped bv some creek-side, has listened to the
strange whisperings of the she-oaks when the
ashes fell from the last pipe, and the starred
blackness closed round like a cloak—or,
wakening at the dawn-streak on a western
plain, has shivered for one half-conscious
moment with a sense of individuality lost in
the interminably-surrounding spaces—has not
felt that this country of unique emotions is a
country which a man would live for, which
a man could die for? And who is there-
having voyaged faraway ; having grown weary
with the ‘clamor of foreign tongues, and sick
for the sight of a friendly face in foreign cities ;


and having at last turned homeward with glad-
ness—who has not breathed with delight Aus-
tralian air before Australian shores were
sighted ; whose pace upon the deck has not
quickened with longing for Home ; who has
not at last been overjoyed, landing at the
western port, to see once more the familiar
flowers and trees, and smell once more the
sweet odour of Australian earth ? Other coun-
tries are as good as Australia; but others can
never be our own. And were the plain benefits
of present union to be lessened a thousandfold,
and the outweighed hindrances to be increased
as much, the glory and pride in assured nation-
hood would still make union infinitely prefer-
able to discord which is a national danger and
may at any moment lead to national disaster.
• * * •
There are many men in this country who do
not belong to the country, whose hearts swing
to a foreign rhythm, whose blood is of necessity
fired by foreign aims and ideals. It is natural
that these should look to their own individual
welfare, heedless or scornful of patriotic dreams
to which they must remain always strangers.
They have their own flags, and ours is to them
an unmeaning strip of cloth ; they have exulta-
tions and enthusiasms begotten far away, and
ours for them may well seem to have no concern
or justification. There are men born in this
country whose minds have narrowed to the
width of the city or the province which bred
them, so that they are blind to the grandeur of
the broadening horizon, and callous to the leap-
ing of the babe in the womb of nationhood. And
there are men who have so thriven by the main-
tenance of feuds and barriers that, no matter at
what cost to the community, they would destroy
any union which took one iota from the tithe
of private profits which disunion enables them
to levy. The appeal of the present is to Aus-
tralians, not to aliens. The appeal is to Aus-
tralians, not to the men of a parish or men of a
province. The appeal is to Australians, not to
calculating self-seekers. And it is to Aus-
tralians who are worthy of their opportunity
and of their country that the challenge comes
to stand up for the promise of a nation, for the
indissolubility of a race—for Australia.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Awful Burden.
The Bulletin once had some respect for
Premier Reid as a rather able manufacturer of
inaccuracy ; now it only regards him as a coarse
and clumsy and loud-mouthed Munchausen of
a very unconvincing order. In his Newcastle
speech last week Reid first told his audience a
lumbering tale about how the Commonwealth
would have to raise £7,000,000 a year in cus-
toms and excise, and, of this, N.S.W. would
have to pay £3,010,000, so that her payments
in customs alone would be £2 per inhabitant
yearly. And he added that he was “ bound in
honesty” to mention that this was “a larger
burden of taxation through the Customs House
than has ever been known in the history of the
world.” Whereat it is alleged there were
“cheers.” Reid’s honesty, as usual, consisted
in a hard and unscrupulous telling of the thing
that didn’t exist, the tale being delivered with a
snufflesome air of truth. By way of evidence,
the following are a few returns of the amount
per head per annum collected as customs duties
in various places in the years set forth :
New South Wales: (1886) £2 Is. 9d.; (1892) £2 3s. 7d.
per head per annum.
Victoria: From 1887 to 1891 the customs taxation
varied from £2 Is. 7d. to £2 10s. lOd. per head per
annum.
Queensland : From 1887 to 1890 the customs varied
from £3 2s. sd. to £3 13s. lOd. per head per annum.
Since. 1890 it has never been below £2 Bs.
South Australia : (1890) £2 3s. 2d. ; (1891) £2 3s. lid.
Tasmania: From 1887 to 1892 the lowest figure was
£2 Is. Bd. per head; the highest, £2 10s. lid. Is still
above £2.
Maoriland : Since 1886 the lowest customs revenue has
been £2 2s. lid. per head per annum ; the highest,
£2 11s. 6d.
Westralia : Lowest customs revenue in last 11 years,
£3 13s. 4d. per head per annum ; highest about £7 10s.
Present amount, over £5 per head per annum.
The Seven Provinces combined paid (in 1887) £2 3s. Id.
per head in customs duties ; (1888) £2 6s. 9d. per head ;
(1889) £2 6s. 6d.; (1890) £2 4s. lOd. ; (1891) £2 4s. Bd. ;
(1892) £2 4s. Id. per head.
The moral of which figures (all taken from CoGH-
lan’s “ Seven Colonies ”) is that the alleged cus-
toms tax of £2 per head per annum, if the exigen-
cies of the Commonwealth should require it, is
not a crushing impost, but one which most of
the Seven Provinces endure constantly without
noticing the burden. Also, it isn’t a larger
burden of Customs taxation “than has ever
been known in the history of the world,” but
an affair of the commonest occurrence. Further,
Premier Reid, despite his sham aspect of
moderation, is merely one of the ordinary
crowd of slanderers who are deceiving the
people with bogus statements and bogus
horrors in the interests of Provincialism. And
when the same politician tells the people about
the other alleged evils and dangers of the
Federal scheme they had better measure his
reliability by the above stupendous misrepre-
sentation and judge the rest of his facts ac-
cordingly. Reid’s “ honesty ”is calculated to
make any decent person feel sick.

Heydon’s Muddled Tale.
L. F. Heydon at Narrabri (N.S.W.), as re-
ported in Sydney Telegraph
Mr. Heydon said as far as the Bill was concerned the
opponents might leave off working further, as it was
dead and buried already. The Premier was against it,
and also two-thirds of the Assembly and three-fourths of
the Council. The colony at heart was all against the
Bill. The Convent ionists got a start through the plaus-
ible reaping-in of Mayors. But a reaction and a revulsion
had set in. The finance commission’s report was its
death-blow. He had been a Democrat all his life. (!) That
immense body, the Labor-party, were strong and patriotic
against it. They were the bone and sinew of the country,
and were heart and soul against it. It threatened dis-
aster and loss to New South Wales. Everybody was in
favor of a wise Federation. It was easy to do it in a
better form. New South Wales was doing better than
the other colonies. Therefore, it was unwise to take
partners who were doing badly. New South Wales had a
land revenue of £2,000,000 last year, which more than
paid the interest on her debt. New South Wales grew
and sold more than the others combined.
If N.S.W. is all against the Bill, then The Bul-
letin assumes that Heydon wouldn’t mind re-
pealing the 80,000 minimum and letting the
Federalists and anti-Federalists meet on the
same level, so that the majority would decide.
For it is a miserably mean and cowardly thing
to insist on 80,000 start over an enemy who is
beaten already. But when Heydon, about the
hardest Tory bigotan N.S.W., tells the public
that he is a Democrat and always has been one,
people are apt to lose faith in all his state-
ments. The wretchedly illogical old gentleman
further says that it is “ unwise ” to federate
with the other provinces—and that he wants to
federate. Which of these two varieties of the
illogical old gentleman is the public to believe?
And when L. F. Heydon declares that he is in
favor of a w'ise Federation, the statement looks
very ridiculous, seeing that he was one of
the little band of provincial bigots who
deluged Sydney with anti-Federal literature
before the Convention was elected and before
the Bill existed. Further, N.S. W. didn’t have
<£2,000,000 of land revenue last year by a good
way, and, if it had, that sum wouldn’t have
paid its interest bill by fully £260,000 ; also,
the N.S. W. Statistician flatly contradicts the
inflated statement that N.S.W. grows and sells
more than the other provinces combined. And
this unspeakably ignorant person, whose figures
are always wrong (and always wrong in his
own favor) professes to teach and enlighten the
people !

The Fast Records of the Anti-Billious.
All the leaders of the Anti-Billious push are
alike in one thing —they all constantly assure
the people that they are true Federationists
and it is only the alleged injustice of the present
Bill that they object to. Obviously they can’t
well say anything else. If they said that they
were against all forms of Federation, and that
if a perfect scheme framed by the archangel
Gabriel came before them they would curse
just the same, then their criticisms would lose
weight. Yet almost every prominent member
of the Anti-Billious mob ivas more or less
against Federation before the present Bill was
in existence. In every province except S.A.,
when the Federal Enabling Bill was first intro-
duced, the semi-liostile crowd began operations
by requiring that the Federal party should not
only show a majority before anything could be
done, but should also poll a large minimum
vote. In N.S.W., Neild, Hughes, Lyne and
others were in favour of putting the minimum
vote which could ensure acceptance as high as
100,000, so these politicians, long before there
was any Bill or any details to object to, had a
great yearning to give Disunion a very long start
over Union. When the work of drafting the
measure had only commenced, and no one
really knew what kind of measure it would be,
the N.S. W. Legislature raised the minimum
from 50,000 to 80,000. Levien, M.L.A.,
asked for an impossible minimum of one-lmlf
the electors—about 150,000 affirmative votes.
Neild demanded 100,000. Crick, Dick,
Haynes, Hogue, Lyne, M‘Gowen, Neild and
a lot more of the anti-Billious voted for the
second reading of the Amendment Bill with
the proviso in it that one-half (fully 150,000)
of the voters of N.S.W. must say “ Yes”
before Federation could be accomplished, even
though there were not 100 votes among the
“Noes.” Almost every present anti-Billite in
the Assembly voted to make union more diffi-
cult and disunion more easy by, at least, raising
the minimum from 50,000 to 80,000. In the
Council, Salomons’ speech on the Amending
Bill was a long, bitter curse at Federation in
general, and a regret that the new mini-
mum wasn’t higher than 80,000. Attorney-
General Want supported Salomons through-
out. Heydon was part of an anti-Federal
league which deluged Sydney with unreliable
anti-Federal literature before the Convention
was elected, and urged the people to remain
disunited. All these individuals now obtain
a hearing from the people on the assertion that
they are true and genuine Federationists ; that,
as such, they wouldn’t oppose the Bill but for
its inherent defects; and that, being true
Federalists, they are unbiassed critics—not
provincial bigots who object to union of any
kind. And they all assure the people that if
will only be beguiled into dropping the
bone that is here they—the above mentioned
individuals —will toil their hardest to get them
the shadow by-and-bye. The Bulletin, judg-
ing the anti-Billious from their past records, is
of opinion that the same crowd will object just
as much to any Federation scheme that can be
invented, and that if the country is persuaded
to drop the bone now for the sake of the
shadow which the anti-Billious gentlemen pro-
mise to provide by-and-bye, it had better bring
its bed along with it, for it will have a long
while to wait.
4.
Equal Representation.
Germany has been so often quoted by the
N.S.W. anti-Bill crowd as a strong case against
the proposal to give the Federated States equal
representation in the Senate that The Bulletin
is moved to point out that this particular piece
of evidence, like most anti-Billious logic, is a
sinful falsehood, and that the argument is
really the other way about. The one point on
which it presents any shadow of truth is that
it is proposed in Australia to give every State,
large or small, the same number of senators,
and this is not done in Germany. Yet, all the
same, in Germany the small States are much
more over-represented than they will be in
Australia. The two extreme cases in the Aus-
tralian Federal Senate will be N.S.W. and
Westralia, and by giving them six members
each in one House (the one which has least
authority) the Westralian voter will have (in
one House only) considerably more representa-
tion than the Cornstalk. In the other House
things are about right, and when Westralia
grows up even the disproportion in the Senate
will disappear. In the German Bundsrath,
Prussia, with. 32,000,000 people, has 17 mem-
bers ; Schaumberg-Lippe, with 41,000, has one
member. Therefore, one elector of Schaumberg-
Lippe has 44 times his proper share of repre-
sentation —instead of eight times, as in the
worst Australian anomaly. And the little
German principality has no room to grow, so
the tremendous anomaly is permanent, not
merely temporary, in Germany, a mass of
small States have 21 votes in the Bundsrath
for less than 5,000,000 people, while Prussia
has only 17 votes for 32,000,000. The small
and medium States, all put together, have not
much more than half Prussia’s population, and
they have much more than three times as many
votes. Switzerland gives two members to each
canton, or one to each half-canton, large or small.
Thus Uri, with 17,000, and Zug, with 23,000
people, when compared to Berne, which has
just the same representation for 541,000, are
fully 2£ times as awful a discrepancy as the
worst discrepancy in Australia. The old Swiss
Constitution-builders were so ignorant, for
want of Neild and Heydon to help them, that
1-| million people have four votes, and 1£
millions have 18, in the State Council. The
United States, Mexico, Chili, Argentina, Brazil,
Venezuela—in fact, every Republican Govern-
ment which calls itself a Federation—allows
equal representation to each State in its Upper
House, regardless of either population or size.
The statement that the system is a new and
rash experiment is so utterly false that the sys-
tem of equal State representation in the Federal
Upper House is practically universal. And,
instead of being a failure, it is the only Federal
system which has been tried for many years
with success. Absolutely the only Federation
which can be quoted as an argument on the
anti-Billious side of the case is Canada, and
even Canada is a better argument in favor of
the proposed Australian system than against it.
There Quebec and Ontario are equally repre-
sented with 24 senators, and the difference in
their population is much greater than that be-
tween N.S.W. and Victoria. And the differ-
ence between the senatorial representation of
the largest and the smallest State is so great
that one voter in British Columbia has
three times the voting power of an elector
in Ontario In fact, of all the Federalists
in the world, the anti-Billious of Australia is
the only one who asks that representation
should go according to population in both
Houses, or who asks anything even dimly
resembling it ; and the bargain that N.S.W.
and Victoria have made with the smaller pro-
vinces is not only absolutely fair according to
the practice of every existing Federation, hut
is a great deal more favorable than that which
the large provinces have secured from the small
ones in either Germany, Switzerland, the
United States, Mexico, Chili, Argentina, Brazil,
or Venezuela.

Branker’s Bunkum.
The Bulletin has always wondered how such
an utterly hopeless muddler as old Sunday
Brunker ever came to be a Minister, ’even in
the very ordinary Ministry of George Reid.
It wonders more than ever now after reading
Brunker’s reasons for opposing Federation.
The following is carefully scissored out from
the report (in Sydney Telegraph of May 30)
of the stupid old man’s outpouring at Minmi ;
said outpouring being the result of three
months’ deep thought :
Mr. Coghlan’s letter to the Premier the other day
showed that the customs and excise of the colony would
have to be increased to 27 per cent, to support this Feder-
ation. (Applause.)
A voice : “ Keep groins'; good lad.”
Mr. Brunker : That sum was fixed by one in whose
figures he believed and had always believed. Twenty-
seven per cent, increase meant to a man with a family
receiving only £lOO a year that his additional taxation
would amount to £27 a year. How many of them could
bear that? (Exclamations of surprise.)
Brunker actually doesn’t know the difference
between charging a 27 per cent, duty on im-
ports (which they do in Tasmania to-day with-
out noticing the burden) and taking ,£27 per
cent, of everybody’s income ! Probably he
thinks, too, that the same tariff will require
everybody to have 27 children and spend 2s. 7d.
in drink. A 27 percent, tariff in N.S.W. is
estimated to produce ,£3,000,000 a year, or
possibly less ; and 27 per cent, charged on every-
body’s income would yield about £16,500,000 a
year ; that’s all the difference. Brunker only
alleges that N.S.W. is to pay millions a
year more than anybody ever proposed. More-
over, it isn’t a question of paying 27 per cent,
more, but 27 per cent, altogether, which makes
Brunker’s mistake 1 J millions larger still. And,
having mistaken a 27 per cent, tariff on imports
for 27 per cent, income tax, the innocent old
cow-merchant says sadly that he is afraid many
families in N.S.W. will be utterly ruined.
Another lovely item from the same speech :
He was in favor of questions upon which deadlocks oc-
curred, after the failure of the joint sitting of the two
Houses to settle them by an absolute majority, being
submitted to a referendum. (Applause.)
Poor pious Bunkum believes that in case of
deadlock the two Houses should sib together and
decide by a bare majority. And if the deadlock
continues after that then the holy ox-dealer,
who has evidently got his patter off by heart
without understanding a word of it, thinks the
referendum should be resorted to. Bub if the
two Houses sit together, and a majority decides
the question, and the Speaker has the usual
casting-vote in case of a tie, how can the dead-
lock continue? The Bulletin doesnotsuspect
Brunker of being a wilful perjurer, but re-
garded as a godly and well-meaning fool, he lays
over every other politician this paper ever
heard of. Even Shambles Patterson, of Mel-
bourne, was a Solomon by comparison. But
when the pitiable old man goes round doing
his little best to blight the prospects of the
Australian nation by telling such tremendous
untruths, even if he only does it out of sheer
stupidity, he grows into a Dublic calamity.

London Times on Federation and the axe-
grinders :
Opposition to the Bill, however much it may be theore-
tically tempered by declarations of sympathy with the
principle of Federation, is for all practical purposes
opposition to Federation. Every trouble has been taken
in repeated and prolonged debate to hammer into shape
some form of federal compromise that may be accepted
by all parties favorable to Federation in all the Australian
colonies. Those who now refuse to accept the Bill can
cherish no reasonable hope that another and a better Bill
will be offered for their consideration. The choice which
lies before them is this Bill or no Federation. Those who
disapprove of the Bill may consider that no Federation is
the better of the two alternatives, but there has been no
catch vote and no exceptional circumstance attending
either the election of delegates or the debates of the
three successive sessions of the Convention which might
serve to justify the supposition that, if the work were all
to be done over again, there would be any greater likeli-
hood of producing a Bill absolutely satisfactory in all
its details. Compromise is the soul of corporate
action. The compromise which one man objects to now
might be changed for a gospel which another man would
object to then, but if the Commonwealth were to be re-
cast a hundred times, compromise would still remain
essential to the acceptance of a scheme which Is to affect
the affairs of six States and upwards of three millions of
persons. The best efforts of Australian statesmanship
have been devoted to the production of a Commonwealth
Bill. The result, we are told, is defective. Unquestion-
ably it is defective. Every Commonwealth Bill will be
defective. The cure is to be found in the power given to
the Federal Parliament to remedy defects, but, as matters
stand, there is no escape from the conclusion that the
man who refuses to give a Federal vote until he is pre-
sented with a scheme entirely satisfactory to him is an
anti-Federationist in adtion, whatever he may please to
call himself in theory.
And no solider gospel was ever preached.
The question of Leg. Council reform grows very
cold in N.S.W. At Bathurst Premier Reid
assured the public that he much admired the
present Council, and fully appreciated its great
services, and that he intended to preserve the
nominee principle and make no violent or revo-
lutionary changes. When the proposed Coun-
cil reform is such a mild little thing as this,
N.S.W.’s Fat Man might as well have done it
any time in the last three years by shoving
through a little Bill of six clauses ; the chances
are that the Council wouldn’t object in the least.
Anyhow, the announcement that next election
is to be fought on this spectral little question is
too thin. If the Federal Bill passes, the N.S.W.
election will be a fight between the Billious,
who want to give effect to the popular vote,
and the anti-Billious, who want to throw the
Referendum overboard, and disfranchise the
People bodily by rejecting the measure. In
that case, Barton will probably run one cr' wd,
and Want and Lyne the other, and wobbler
Reid won’t be needed. And, if the Federal
Bill doesn’t pass, the fiscal issue will rage once
more, only the Protectionists will probably
throw their incapable Lyne overboard and have
Barton for their leader.
One of the most glaring of Premier Reid’s
many glaring hypocrisies is his complaint that
this Federal scheme will probably result in fix-
ing a Protectionist tariff on the Commonwealth.
When Reid, the boss politician of a province
that is nearly half Protectionist in sentiment,
asked five Protectionist provinces to federate
with N.S.W., he knew that there would almost
certainly be a strong Protectionist majority in
the combined Legislature. Also, he knows that
there will be the same comparative certainty in
any Federation which can possibly be formed
out of such materials. Therefore, if he was
going to raise this difficulty, he should
never have started the movement ; and when
he raises it now he ought to explain that the
same difficulty will be there next time, and
that when he raises it he is objecting, not to
the present Bill, but to Federation in general.
The alleged Federationists who point out that
this scheme may mean an increase of some of
the N.S.W. postal rates to bring them in line
with those of the other provinces (unless the
rates of the other provinces are reduced to
bring them in line with those of N.S.W.) also
fail to mention that every Federation involves
the same difficulty. Most of the objections
advanced by the alleged Federationists who are
doing their best in the cause of disunion are
objections, not to the scheme now on hand, but
to every scheme that can be advanced either now
or hereafter.

Premier Reid, in his Newcastle speech last
week, said that if “ the bonus of Jd. per ton
per mile on coal now paid by the Victorian
Government ” was continued after Federation
was established “it would be an absolute
breach of the contract of Federation.” (The
Telegraph, a week ago, told its usual lie by
saying that the bonus was £d., instead of Jd.,
but that is a mere side-issue.) What Premier
Reid carefully avoided mentioning was that
the said bonus was granted for two years only,
and expires next October. Also, he made no
mention of the fact that while Victoria has, for
two years, indirectly subsidised its land coal
traffic to the extent of per ton per mile
(though even then it only carries coal at the
same rate as in N.S.W.), N.S.W. has, for about
a dozen years, indirectly subsidised its sea coal
traffic to the extent of 3d. per ton. It did this
by abolishing, at the expense of the general
revenue, the wharfage rates at the two
great coal ports of Newcastle and Wol-
longong. Strangely enough the Telegraph,
which curses so bitterly about the Victorian
subsidy, defends the N. S. Wales one.
Also, it states, on no evidence at all,
that the Victorian temporary subsidy will be
kept up, after Federation is established, while
the N.S.W. permanent one will be abolished.
And, after cursing all bounties, direct and in-
direct, with a terrific curse, it calls upon the
Cornstalk public, in its leading article of 26th
May, to vote against Federation lest it should
lead to the abolition of this bigN.S. W. bounty.
But the paper which alleges, for the sake of
making a case against Federation, that 100
half-pennies make 16s. Bd. is capable of just
about anything.

Justice Hood’s discharge of Hobby, the biga-
mous hubby, on the ground that he was drunk
on the occasion of the Kinsman matrimonial
performance, once more illustrates the fact that,
though the letter of the law insists that drun-
kenness does not palliate any offence, offences
are and should be often palliated by it.
Judges almost invariably instruct juries to dis-
regard the plea of drunkenness. When the
plea is ,pressed they cite the old dictum th fe
when a man gets drunk he takes the risks of
breaking laws, and as getting drunk is a
voluntary act the after facts are all to be con-
sidered as voluntarily perpetrated deeds. The
dictum and the inferences from it are constantly
breaking down under the tests of everyday
experiences. Men get drunk involuntarily,
and men who get drunk voluntarily tumble
into crimes outside the range of vices to
which they knew themselves liable ; yet
judges re assert the old dictum and oblige
juries to reject the plea of drunkenness.
While judges like Justice Hood give effect
to common sense and natural law grave
evils may be avoided; but why should it
be left to the discretion of a judge to give or
withhold such effect ? Why should not drun-
kenness, as a state of mind, go, like insanity,
among other facts of the case to the jury ?

Lord Rosebery’s mother —the 79-year-old
Duchess of Cleveland —has just returned from
a tour in India, during which, amongst other
youthful escapades, she made the ascent of one
of the Himalayas.
Melbourne has had a celestial visitation
s.s. Bucephalus, from India, dumped at Port
Melbourne 50 dusky Orientals, nearly all young,
and most of them closely resembling Ranp s other
brother. These mournful-eyed Asiatic fellow-
subjects of ours imagined they were coining to
work for princely wages on Australian sugar -
plantations, but found themselves stranded in
Melb. without 18d. amongst them. Charity will
have to cover their multitude of shins it they
cannot make a honest living, as so many new-
chuin Eastern niggers do in Melbourne, retailing
vermilion photographs and unchartered cigars to
the innocent and confiding people Who polish the
Bourke-street verandah-posts between Russell ana
Swanston streets. The Sikhs say there are 40
more on the water, which suggests anot
deplorable effect of the Ranji boom in Australia.
