Town Hall Newbury’s Saturday “ Pops.”
The “Squire of Dames,” which was put
up for an undeservedly short season at Sydney
Criterion last week, is, next to “ The Middleman,”
the piece in which Cartwright shows best, so far
as Sydney has yet had a chance to judge. The
management which gave this pretty and effective
comedy only three nights at the end of the season,
while “'Moths” was allowed to haul its draggled
wings through a whole week of damp and melan-
choly, deserves to be presented with the large
stale pussy-cat of public opprobrium. The
“ Squire of Dames ” is a steal from the French,
and has all the French brightness and crispness of
dialogue. Also, it has a novel motive, as compared
with the average comedy. The cynical hero,
being rich and otherwise unemployed, passes his
weary life away helping lovely woman out of the
sort of difficulties which lovely woman is always
falling into. He warns young married ladies who
are about to “ fall,” and they mostly take his
warnings kindly. He recovers compromising
letters, and wrestles with the gay deceiver to
make him give up the photograph, and when the
deceiver has got into the house at a compromising
hour he gets him out again. Girl-wives who flirt
with the beautifully-moustached beguiler are re-
monstrated with at their own residences, and
where a maid has fallen in love with an unworthy
object the object is hauled off effectually, free of
charge.
* * * *
The “ Squire,” in short, is a pleasant and
novel busybody, and his part isa difficult one to play.
Obviously, the tired, cynical deputy-Providence
who spends his life doing good by hauling off the
object of a lady's misplaced affections needs an
infinity of tact ; even the work of telling the lady
that he knows about her misplaced affections is
full of difficulty, and the business of recovering
the compromising letters without a fight, and a
black eye, and a disgracefully wrecked ear, and
the loss of one coat-tail, has much trouble in it.
Cartwright did the part well, though now and
then this scribe had a vague idea that
the lady ought to be remonstrated with
more gently and less like a kindly and
well-meant ton of bricks. There was a general
brightening-up of the company during the brief
season of the “Squire.” Miss Noni Rickards
made her first real success as a small girl in a short
frock and a broken heart. Miss Gethyn Darragh
did another of those bright, perky American girls
who seem to come naturally to her, and spoke her
part through her adorable little nose in a charm-
ing fashion. Miss Lamb seemed to have got rid
of some of her stiffness. The tremendous Story
Gofton struck out a rather novel line as a learned
professor with a lost memory, and looked less like
his own huge self than usual, and Thursby took
well as he always does. The Cartwright season,
which looks as if it was beginning to pay good
dividends at the last moment, is supposed to end
this week with a brief attack of the “ Idler.”
»* * *
Bland Holt continued his “ Life of
Pleasure” into a fourth week after all, and the
new drama doesn’t go up till this (Saturday) even-
ing. Curiously enough, no Sydney manager seems
to have thought of putting up a new piece on
Friday, though it is a holiday, and the night of
the great national and patriotic drunk when the
engineer will get down and scrape the remains of
Reid and Lyne and Hevdon off the wheel. This
seems a serious oversight somehow. “Straight
from the Heart” is the rather clumsy name of
Bland’s next venture ; it is a perfectly new drama
so far as Sydney is concerned, and it is guaran-
teed, so far as the staging goes, to be as gorgeous
as a bird of Paradise.
** * *
At Sydney Lyceum the “Orphan Heiress”
will be bathed in woe and dragged through a
tangled wilderness of tears till further notice.
Probably it is the dark and premeditated shoving
of the heroine into the den of lions that is the
secret of such success as has attend' d this haggard
literary shriek. There is a terrible moment during
which it seems just possible that Miss Bensusan’s
shapely limbs will be partaken of as light refresh-
ment by the animals, so the gallery gets lots of
thrill for its 6d. As the bush reporter always says
in his account of the banquet given at the agri-
cultural show where the alderman’s hog gets first
prize: “ The board groaned beneath the good
things provided.” The good things on the board
in this case are Miss Bensusan herself, raw and
without gravy, and any hasty kind of lion would
eat her with her clothes on at first sight. This
easting of the heroine to the lions in the nine-
teenth century is somewhat novel, and if
Diver, as a preliminary, were to buy a
second-hand rack cheap, and dislocate her
in the scullery, her shrieks of agony being
smothered with the dishcloth or the hall mat, it
would be a further novelty. The drama could
hardly be made more grotesque than it is in any
case, so that matter needn’t be considered, and a
play in which the bad character gallops his horse
on the head of the long-lost parent just when she
is about to find her child, after 17 years of search,
is so harrowing that a little more harrow wouldn t
matter. The joke about the pork-trotter, which
brightened up the gloom somewhat, begins to look
a trifle wan ; the fierce sun of criticism has shone
on the trotter, and diminished its freshness some-
what. All the same, the pig’s foot in question
does its best to frivol pleasantly in the dark places
of the drama, and this paper is grateful to it
accordingly.
** * *
The Early Christian hasn’t yet announced
his departure from Her Majesty’s, and as business
is satisfactory he will probably continue to be a
bright spot in the pagan world for some tune
longer. The public likes him ; he has a.ll the
merits of going to church without the desolation.
All the same, this paper sometimes wonders
whether that Early Christian was a paying in-
vestment. The speculation leads nowhere ; there
is nothing so absolutely unsatisfactory or so end-
less as the attempt to think out what might have
been if the thing had been that wasn t, or if the
thing that was hadn’t been at all. And whether
the world would have been better or worse to-day
if there had never been any Early Christian is a
huge mystery. Anyhow, these Early people
kept alive a gospel which is essentially one
of poverty and humility and non-resistance,
and all the rich and arrogant and hard-
fighting nations profess it to this day.
The only nations in this world which don’t fight
are a few which never heard of the gospel of
non-resistance. Therefore, this writer’s theology
is mixed, and he regards Williamson’s imported
martyr as a pleasant spectacle which is evidently
intended to convey a great moral, only the moral
has apparently been drinking and has come home
with its hat in a very confused attitude over its
left eye.
*m * *
The Tivoli is to be adorned for a short
season by John Coleman, he of the inimitable
Scarecrow Dance, prior to that bright particular
humorist’s departure for the United States. When
he departs it is to be hoped he will have a safe
journey, and won’t be captured cn route by any
serious-minded Spaniard who wouldn’t be able to
appreciate him. Miss Peggy Pryde has so far
grown on the Sydney public that she is now voted
one of the brightest variety ladies the city has yet
seen, and George Wood, the somewhat different
comedian, is too well-known to require recommen-
dation. As for Miss Marian Ainsworth, the feet
of that agile danseuse grasp the public with both
hands and speak for themselves with a loud voice
that requires no bush ; and The Bulletin can
only trust that they will lend an ear to these few
words of appreciation and accept them with a
smile, and that the laurel which this paper re-
spectfully places on their hair may never fade.
A polished, conscientious actor of Titheradge’s
class is valuable even in a poor melodrama, and
when he talks platitudes to the doleful occupant
of a garden-seat the dialogue is almost impressive.
“ Shall We Forgive Her ” gets better treatment
in many places than it deserves. Hans Phillips
is well placed, Charles Holloway plays effectively,
and Miss Ida Gresham (as the heroine), Willard,
Miss Leighford, the Gourlays, and Geo. Chalmers
help the agony along. As for Johanna, she is re-
presented by Miss Duggan in a way that delights
The Bulletin, and commands its heartfelt
blessing.
“ Dr. Bill ” is putting in a second week at
Melb. Bijou, and a generation which knew not
the “ kangaroo dance ” in the long ago seems to
have taken kindly to its revival. “ Dr. Bill,” on
the present occasion, is more of a farce than a
comedy ; the humor is laid on with a trowel, and
the points are hammered into the audience’s
understanding just as far as they will go. On the
opening night the show looked sick and weary
towards the finish, but since then there have not
been any solemn pauses, and the prompter isn't
called upon to find the missing word in a loud
tone of voice. Also the dude person who lifted
his feet extravagantly at the outset is not such a
high stepper as he was, and W. J. Montgomery,
in the name part, begins to get the better of his
Shakespearian tendencies. Farcical comedy being
more or less asked for in Melbourne at this par-
ticular moment, Miss Myra Kemble and co. will
try “ The Alhambra Girl ” when “Dr. Bill” has
served his turn. Probably on Saturday.
** * *
Wilson Barrett terminates his hardwork-
ing career at the Princess’s to-night (Thursday)
with a last grand final farewell production of
“ Virginius,” and after whittling his unhappy
offspring for the sake of his family pride, the mas-
sive Roman will doubtless make a speech. On
Friday the Firm’s theatre will be closed for
rehearsal of the new-coming show, also in order,
perhaps, to give Barrett and co. a chance to get
elsewhere before the end of the week.
** * *
Saturday brings Australia’s well-remem-
bered Pattie Brown back to the Princess’s, where,
by the way, she made one of her very early marks
in “ Harbour Lights.” Miss Pattie, in herself, is
rather a national event, and, if W. and M. had
merely advertised her to walk across the stage
“ by arrangement with Mr. Rickards,” she would
find a lot of people waiting outside the early door
for the joy of seeing her smile once again. She
will appear, however, in conjunction with sundry
new people from London, and a main body of
Australian co., and Barrie’s bright “ Little
Minister,” dramatised from his verra Scawtish
novel of the same title, will give the season a big
Caledonian send off. Pattie Browne, supported by
Pillars of the Auld Licht Kirk o’ Thrums, should
fill a long-felt want.
*# * *
The new management at the Alexandra
made a fairly promising commencement on Satur-
day, and if the company, whose very weakest
point is wardrobe, can keep up a good supply of
five-act horror the big house may continue to flash
an electric-light around the district for the next
few weeks. “ The Ladder of Life ” is a crimson
narrative of a bad favorite son dumping all his
misdeeds upon the head of his younger brother.
The suffering relative has to endure three acts of
unjust suspicion ere the forged papers, and the
murdered body of the girl who went astray, are
debited to the real offender. Wilfred Hayes
undertook the suffering in a capable manner, and
the performance generally went a little higher
than the average Haleck standard.
*# m *
The first-class fooling of the Poluskis, and
the Kelli no Family gambols, and some new
Sisters, and a great improvement in the weather,
account for a furious rush of business to Melb.
Opera House just now. Also, the concert-boom
gives no indication of going wrong. Wednesday
afternoon musicals at the Athenaeum are a suo-
cess, and Newbury’s Saturday-night Pops at the
Town Hall go as vigorously as ever. A new con-
cert co., imported by Lemmone, will take up the
running when the “ Pop ” season closes.

From Wellington, M.L. :
Burward Lely is here giving concerts in conjunction
with an excellent little Polish violiniste. Full houses
nightly, and Smythe’s face fairly beams. The Lely is
certainly a most, fetching tenor, and would be an even
more fetching performer if he were not so terribly exact.
He will give you the name of the song he is going to
sing, but before he sings he will tell you when it was
written, who wrote it, who the author was, who he or she
married, what and where the children are, who they
married, if they married a duke, what happened to the
duke, &c., then he will turn his lengthy figure round to
Mrs. Lely at the piano, and the song starts. Dunvard
reminds me of Stanley, who never came to climaxes until
he had made it clear to the audience that such an event
occurred when a solitary bunch of bananas was fortu-
nately found 92 miles from Squibsklski, in latitude 88,
on the night of the 15th day of June, 1887, at 9.30 p.m.,
Greenwich time. Amy Sherwin is also here with quite a
large co., but is not doing too well. Lely got in first.
How can Wellington in the dance season stand two con-
cert cos., with H.M.S. Royal Arthur in port as well?
“ Shall We Forgive Her? ” the long-winded fiction
That filled Melbourne Royal on Saturday night,
Has struck, with the weight of its dull leaden
diction,
The soul of this ballad and flattened it quite.
The plot comes to pass in the commonplace
manner
Of dramas which picture impossible life,
And nothing is new save a gruesome Johanna.
Who raises objections to Oliver’s wife.
The husband’s a fool, and his girl is too frightened
To trot out the truth when he’s looking for lies ;
Johanna, her countenance spectrally whitened,
Awaits the disturbance that’s bound to arise.
For the wife has a Past which she wishes to cover
With hunks of repentance and slabs of regret;
In the prologue she sinned, and her drunken old
lover
Turns up—and Johanna makeS trouble, you bet!
The Past, after all, wouldn’t very much matter
If the culprit owned up to her early disgrace,
But the drama proceeds, and the characters
chatter,
Whilst gruesome Johanna, with chalk on her
face,
Stands hugging her elbows—a virgin unpleasant,
Who lurks in the foreground and hatches the
plot ;
She doesn’t say much, but, whenever she’s present,
You feel that Johanna is bossing the lot.
The husband gets suddenly stricken with blind-
ness
Through hearing the Past which Johanna has
found :
His wife wanders off to lament his unkindness,
And leaves Miss Johanna to hover around.
Then Reverend Titheradge pleads for the sinner
(She looks very pretty, in spite of her shame),
The drama grows steadily thinner and thinner,
But ghastly Johanna goes on all the same.
And e’en when the husband recovers his vision,
And falls on the neck of his penitent Grace,
Johanna is there, with a smile of derision
And fresh coats of chalk on her treacherous face.
Johanna remains whilst the curtain is falling,
Alone on the hearth-rug, suppressing her rage—
As an uncoffined corpse she is simply appalling :
Her equal has never been seen on the stage.
The story is stupid, and clumsily tearful,
But Virtue at last gets the better of Vice ;
The cast isn’t bad, and the scenery’s cheerful,
And seats can be bought at a popular price.
So the gallery youth, seeking fun for his “ tan-
ner,”
Likewise the stalls and the circle below,
May pardon a drama that suffers Johanna
To shine like a spectre surrounded by woe.
At Poverty Point.
Signor Steffani has recently unloosed a small flight of
singing-birds on the Sydney public. Florence Schmidt,
at present gathering sheaves of golden opinions and
pocketfuls of golden coin in her native Bananaland, is
probably the greatest of these warblers, hut she is fol-
lowed ciosely'by Hetty Holroyd, noted already for the
frozen purity of her voice, and piquant Florence Mack,
whose concert a couple of weeks ago, in the Centennial
Hall, was a decided success. This little lady’s voice is by
no means a strikingly powerful organ, but it is perie-
tratingly sweet and flexible, and in fioriture reminds one
of those sparkling intricate plaits and twists of spun-
glass presented to glass-blowers’ patrons. In the face
she hears a striking resemblance to Letty Lind, and
she likewise possesses a little of that much-adored dancer’s
verve and snap ; in short, she seems designed by nature
to shine in Australia’s national amusement, comic-opera.
When Wilson Barrett did his “one-night-only” per-
formance of “Otbello” in Melbourne, on Wednesday
evening last week, he “passioned” with much frenzy,
and gave rise to a ludicrous situation. Having stifled
Desdemona in a business-like fashion, he came down
stage, being first supposed to draw the tapestry together,
leaving the smothered Miss Jeffries comfortably corpsed
on the bed. But in his enthusiasm he did not quite close
the arras, and, while he was elocuting in the foreground,
half the audience were tittering at the comic sight of
the beauteous corpse in the rear, sitting up and arranging
her draperies to picturesque advantage. Having seen
that the curves and lines of her ample form were correctly
displayed by the fall of her white robes, Maud Desdemona
lay down arid went dead again, while Barrett continued
his riot.
Wilson Barrett’s co., or some of them, went to see
Titheradge act, at Melb. Royal, the other night, and
thought nothing, absolutely nothing, of his alleged his-
trionic talent. Which was exactly what the Barrett co,
might have been expected to think. They saw a finished
artist in an unreal, melo-dramatio character, and were
struck only by the fact that ho didn’t perform on barn-
storming principles.
One of the Poluski Bros., of Rickards’co.—the broad
comedian brother, with the baggy pants—has been in the
business for 37 years. He was a four-year-old when he
started.
Pattie Browne is nowadays stout in moderation, but
her face and figure are very little older, for stage pur-
poses, than when she went away.

old Cinderella Adelaide 1
Prompter: “Nearly ten years ago, when well in,

the play-books. Harwood never displayed any of the
admiration of Shakespeare so much affected by Thespians.
He admired a good dinner.”

At “ Othello,” t’other evening, the boudoir-scene
evoked a mournful wail of frustrated hopes. After being
“ unpinned ”by Emilia —a perfunctory proceeding withal
—Desdemona, in a lavish r obe-de-nuit, approaches her
couch. Just on the edge of expectancy, however, the
lady carefully shut off the audience’s delicate interest
with a screen. Apropos, I should think much skill is
required to get gracefully into bed.
“Latest Melba pars, to hand state that the Australian
nightingale and her . j. drew 30,000d015. for three per-
formances in ’Frisco. At this rate, the further informa-
tion that “ Melba intends to visit Australia next year, but

A leading article in a recent Argus spoke of Friar
Lawrence as a character in “ Hamlet.” The editor, seem-
ingly, has joined the Society for being Very Tired of
Shakespeare and didn’t trouble to note the curious mis-
take. Curious because everybody associates the Friar
who married Romeo and Juliet with the cell where he
performed the ceremony. And there is no cell in “ Ham-
let” except for the ardent playgoer who expects to get
“ new readings ” aud “ more light ” from Wilson Barrett’s
appearance in the name part.
Carrie Moore jumped into Ada Reeve’s part in the
“ French Maid ” in Adelaide, and made a real success.
Carrie is a little Melb. girl of 16, and has only been with
W. and M. two years.
Ada Reeve and Bert Gilbert have both abandoned
cycling. In Adelaide the fair Ada was upset and got a
sprained ankle, which kept her out of “ The French
Maid ” for a week. Gilbert, in the middle of the follow-
ing week, collided with a cab, and was unable to sing his
songs. Same day Carrie Moore, who was playing the
Maid, vice Reeve, was thrown out of a dog-cart, and
nearly broke her arm. For full details, apply to Pete
Hughes, whose language is too unorthodox for the holy
city, anyway.
The Kickapoo Co., (’Murka) is sending a vaudeville and
minstrel show to this country, via Hawaii. Australia
awaits with anxiety the first parcel of Kickapoo goods.
To Australia.
Australians, riding knee to knee,
Meet these betrayers man to man,
Show all the carping crew that we
Strike home to-day in freedom’s van 1
What matters it if kith and kin
All prosper ’neath our nationhood ;
Has our own courage grown so thin
That we need fear our flesh and blood ?
Comrades ! sole heirs by pride of place
Of all the land, of all the sea,
Ours is the task to lead our race
On to the glory that will be.
Remember, those who bid us dwell
Cooped in a mean provincial den,
Would rather reign as lords in hell
Than serve in Heaven ’neath nobler men.
Here, with our feet at Union’s gates
And eager hands on freedom’s rungs,
Dare true men tempt the fickle fates
At the false call of craven tongues?
“ No ! ” rings our answer from the West;
A hundred years have proved that we
Were right to trust that what is best
For all, for one must better he.
Vanguard of progress, onward ride
O'er these provincial, sordid ranks,
Strike home for union deep and wide,
A world awaits to give you thanks !
KENNETH MACKAY.

The WILDCAT
COLUMN
The Westralian Government has borrowed
£500,000 at 3i per cent, from the A.M.P. Society to go on
with. The loan is for three years, to give the credit of
W.A. time to improve in Britain, it being practically
admitted that the record spendthrift among the Seven
Provinces couldn’t raise a loan there now except on terms
that would be next door to ruinous. But, unless the
Federal Government takes over the Westralian debt
before 1901, the chances are that its state three years
hence will be a vast amount worse than it is now. Ten-
ders have been called for the stupendous Coolgardie
water scheme, which will cost two or three millions, and
there are signs of a deficit of £250,000, or so, and, in one
wav arid another, the present loan will only keep Wes-
tralia off the London market for a few months. Wes-
tralia should have made arrangements to borrow the
whole A.M.P. Society in one act. Or else it should have
fixed that 3J per cent, loan for 20 years, with the proviso
that, in case of Westralia joining an Australian Federa-
tion, the Federal Government should have the right to pay
it off at a year’s notice.
* * » *
The Insurance and Banking Record, in its
report on the Colonial Mutual Life Insurance Society,
estimates that the interest realised on the funds last year
was only £4 os. lOd. per cent., which agrees very closely
with this paper’s statement that the interest-rate was
about £4 Is. And as the Colonial Mutual directors, at
the annual meeting, said not a word about the dubious
position of the society, but, instead, congratulated each
other enthusiastically on its progress and soundness, and
the sagacity and foresight of the people who brought it to
its present state, The Bulletin again draws attention to
the figures. Two important features in the safety of an
insurance office are the rate of interest earned upon its
funds now and the rate which it is estimated will be
earned in the future. Any office can be solvent on paper
if it estimates future interest-earnings high enough, but
any well-managed institution always assumes its future
interest-earnings a good deal lower than the present ones,
in order that there may be a safe margin in case of a
sudden drop in rates. Therefore the following figures,
taken from the latest report in the Insurance and Bank-
ing Record, are interesting :
Earned* Mar Sin
earned in future
A.M.P. Society .. .. £4 18 10 £3 10 0 £lB 10
City Mutual .. .. 581 400 181
Maoriland Govt. Dept. 4 19 0 3 12 6 166
Mutual Life Association 418 7 4 0 0 18 7
National Mutual .. 417 2 4 0 0 17 2
Aus. Widows’ Fund .. 415 7 4 0 0 15 7
Colonial Mutual.. 4 0 10 4 0 0 10
The Mutual Life Association, considering its margin of
18s. 7d. per cant, insufficient, is reducing its assumed
future rates of interest. The Colonial Mutual is appar-
ently quite satisfied with its margin of lOd.
The trouble with the Colonial Mutual is just
this : It is highly probable that its interest earnings
would not be so much lower than those of every other
large office if it were not that a lot of its assets are really
old boom losses for which the management never had the
courage to make provision. To bring the interest earn-
ings of the Colonial Mutual on a level with those of the
Widows’ Fund, which is itself below the average, it
would be necessary to write-off about £382,000 of the
alleged funds as lost. To get on the same level of solvency
as the Maoriland Government Department it would be
necessary to write off about £445,000 —over one-fifth of
the entire assets —and also to make an additional reserve
of about £BO,OOO in order to reduce the assumed future
interest rate to the M.L. Department’s level. To get to
the A.M.P. Society’s level would cost about as much. But
if the Colonial Mutual management wants the public to
believe that it has no serious losses unprovided for, and
that it merely earns so much less interest than other
societies owing to some cause that is unexplained (a thing
that is very hard of belief), then it obviously requires to
reduce its estimates of future interest to about £3 6s. in
order to have the same margin of safety as the next insti-
tution on the list—and that would necessitate an extra
reserve of about £160,000. To get the same margin of
safety as the A.M.P. it would require to reduce its esti-
mates from £4 to about £2 12s. per cent., which would
necessitate an additional reserve of something awful.
Whichever way it is looked at the Colonial Mutual seems
in a bad situation.
There is no legal standard of solvency whatso-
ever as regards life assurance societies in any of the Aus-
tralias. There is no independent valuation of the assets,
and (except in the one case of the M.L. Government
Department) no State supervision whatever. And the
fact that the alleged supervision by the great, scattered,
utterly-disunited body of shareholders and by the press is
a dismal farce, is sufficiently evidenced by the fact that
the Colonial Mutual holds its annual meeting year after
year, and the officials congratulate each other on the
remarkable progress and soundness of the institu-
tion, and no questions are asked. Probably there is very
little chance of effective State supervision from the seven
disunited Governments, but the Federal administration
should be able to do something. Meanwhile there should
still be time to put the Colonial Mutual on a sound basis
if the board will only gather up its courage in both hands
and move vigorously in the matter. But, apparently, the
size of the trouble scares it.
• * * •
A quaint little life-office, whose very existence
is a secret to most people, is the Australasian Insurance Co.
It is a proprietary concern with a capital of £25,000, and
another co., the Australian Alliance, seems to be the only
shareholder. It has a premium income of about £l4OO a
year, and it hangs out in an obscure fashion somewhere in
Melbourne. The fact that the premium income is about
£7O a year more than it was six years ago seems to suggest
that it does a little new business now and then. The total
sum insured under all its policies is £38,936 2s. 9d., and,
as the capital and reserves amount to £44,425, it could
pay up promptly to the last farthing, even if every one of
its policyholders dropped down dead to-day (Thursday).
And even the A.M.P. or the “three American giants
couldn’t do that.
• * * *
Standard Mutual Building Society (Melb.),
which is doing pretty well for a building society these
times, made a profit of £476 for the half-year ended
January, 189 S. This is equal to about 19/5 per cent, per
annum on capital—a figure which very few societies reach
nowadays. The Standard is laboriously paying off its
deposits at the rate of an occasional shilling in the £, and
in the last six years it has reduced the load from £120,000
to rather unde'r £57,000. It has paid no dividends for six
years and its earnings are now about one-fifth what they
were ’in ’92 ; yet it is a very respectable survivor, and
appears to have some prospects before it-though not the
sort of thing that would have been called prospects ten
years ago.

The N.S.W. wheat harvest of 1898, from 992,484
acres of land, is estimated at 10£ million bushels. And
the ill-luck of Victoria in the matter of last year’s drought
may be gathered from the fact that its crop is estimated
at 10£ million bushels also, from 1,638,019 acres. In ’97
N.S. YV. averaged over 10 bushels to the acre while Victoria
reaped less than 4i. In ’9B N.S.W. has over 10£ bushels
to Victoria’s 6&. How Victorian agriculture has lived at
all through the three drought years which have struck it
in succession is a mystery.

The Insurance and Banking Record gives a
list of 24 Australasian fire and marine insurance cos.
which have disappeared within the last ten- years. Four
small ones went broke ; seven were bought up by other
Australasian cos., and Bull annexed the remaining 13.
* • • *
St, James Building Society (there is no luck in
building societies that are named after saints) held a
meeting in Melbourne lately, and announced that a loss
of £10,071 had been made during the half-year through
the sale of properties below the absurd paper-value put
on them in the books. The balance-sheet now frankly
admits that of the £215,000 capital about £130,000 has
gone in depreciation. And the chairman gently broke
the news to the meeting that, though the remaining
liabilities are only about £40,000, and the assets on paper
represent £121,000, leaving an apparent surplus of £BO,OOO
for shareholders, these latter are never likely to get any-
thing. The alleged £121,000 of assets are simply proper-
ties that stand in the books at the old impossible prices of
the hoom, and the only present hope is that they may
realise enough to pay the £40,000 liabilities. So St.
James may be considered extinct, and another £215,000
of Australia’s alleged wealth evaporates.
* * * *
B.H. Block 10 has apparently settled down to
an average profit of just over £20,000 per half-year ; the
figures for the last three periods have been £23,340,
£23,634, and (for the half-year just ended) £20,546. In
the same time there has been treated at the mill 41,519
tons, 55,132 tons, and 53,866 tons of ore. The ore goes
into two general classes —-one concentrates and siimes,
the other “ tailings.” The former include, approximately,
80 per cent, of the lead and 50 per cent, of the silver,
with about 2 per cent, of the zinc ; the “ tailings ” carry
practically all of the rest, and average 28 per cent, zinc,
12oz. silver, and 7£ per cent. lead. Yet the co., despair-
ing of profitable local treatment, has just sold 100,000
tons of “ tailings ” for ss. per ton on the mine ; they are
to go to Europe. At present quotations the metal in
these “ tailings ” is worth about £7 15s. per ton (of
“ tailings ”) ; so that the buyer has a pretty big margin
to work on. The proceeds from the sale fairly belong,
of course, to all the half-yearly periods during
which the concentrating mill has been running ;
at the present rate of production ss. per ton is equal to
£8250 per half-year. Last half-year’s profit was equal to
7s. 7£d. per ton of ore, or £1 Bs. 6d. per ton of concen-
trates and slimes. The co. received on the average 17s.
lid. per ton less for its concentrates than during the pre-
vious half-year (that is, about 3s. 4d. per ton less for its
ore) ; but, owing to economies in the mine, the profit in
this last half-year was only Is. Old. per ton of ore less
than it was during the preceding half. And as the man-
agement expects that the mill will do still better work
(and if it doesn’t some stiff remarks will be called for),
and as the mine has an abundance of ore of the same
grade as has been dealt with lately, though some of it is
harder, prospects are pretty good. Everything, of course,
depends upon the price of the metals ; assuming that a
trifle less than current quotations continue to rule, and
that the “tailings” can be disposed of, the shilling
monthly div. ought, as before said, to be just possible.
Seeing that there is, theoretically, over £7 worth
of metal in every ton of Block 10 “ tailings,” and that
onlv ss. per ton is got for it, and seeing that £20,000 per
half-year can be paid in divs. from tip other part of the
ore, and seeing, further, that exparlSients in the treat-
ment of this “tailings” material are proceeding in a
hundred places, it is a matter for serious consideration
whether the co.’s interests lie in the direction of imme-
diate sale. Certainly 1250 tons of it are being produced
every week, and storage room is of some account.
* • * *
B.H. Block 14 ’s profits have been tumbling
seriously. The course in the last three half-years has
been—£2l,69B, £17,763, £14,150. But this is partly due
to a reduction of the carbonate ore handled, and partly
to a falling-off in the ore-buying business. The mine’s
hope is in the lead market. On the last half-year’s
figures, it would take a fall of 4 l-sd. per oz. of silver to
counterbalance a rise of £1 per ton for lead ; and, as
in two years a fall of 3jd. per oz. for silver
has been accompanied by a rise of £2 15s. per ton for
lead, it has been argued that the mine is pretty safe.
But, though a persistent fall in silver would certainly
strengthen the lead market, anything beyond about £l5
for lead would open up scores of lead-mines pure and
simple, now discarded. There has been, the general
manager savs, a further slight improvement in the re-
covery of the metals (that is, rather less has gone away in
the “ tailings”) ; but whether this improvement counter-
balances the admitted change of the ore to a harder
character, involving greater expense both for mining and
milling, it is impossible to say. Block 14 will probably
suffer more from this change of character than any
mines except the South and North. The latter doesn’t
count, and the writer has come to the conclusion that it
never will until we know a much better way of handling
sulphides than we do to-day.
The B.H. Junction is the Awful Example.
Just when the Melb. people were beginning to know
something about mines and mine management, the con-
trol of this show went to Adelaide ; and when presently
a new run of rich ore was found it was obvious that the
South Australians were the right people. But if worse
use could have been made of this new development Thu
Bulletin is anxious to have particulars of the plan. Just
at present, it isn’t glad to remember that it said about 22
times that a day of reckoning was coming ; it is only sad
because it remembers that, while the ore was being dragged
out by the roots, the shareholders, fattening on dividends,
weren’t taking any warning. Fifteen months ago, the co.
having been too busy pulling out the ore to fill up the
holes it was making, the mine took the responsibility on
its own shoulders and closed up ; the only satisfactory
feature of the proceeding was that it didn’t make a grave
(as it easily might have done) for 50 or 100 workmen. In
the two half-years that preceded this collapse, the profits
amounted to £70,749 ; since then, in the same period, no
profits whatever have been made ; but, instead, a balance
of liquid assets over liabilities, amounting to £27,232, has
been wiped out of existence, and an excess of liabilities
over assets, amounting to £18,141, has been put in its
place. True, some machinery has been bought; but those
purchases do not alter the fact that, though 67,800 tons of
sulphides have been sold, not enough has been realised in
the 12 months since the collapse to pay current working-
expenses. It would hardly be a fair thing, perhaps, to
remember that, when the collapse occurred,, the manage-
ment pooh-poohed it as quite a little thing in fact, rather
a good thing, so far as it went. But, as the general
manager has just now again told the shareholders that he
stands by the opinion he then expressed (that “ the ore
would be raised much cheaper and to greater advantage ),
Tiie Bulletin wants to know, in view of the figures quoted,
what sort of a humorist he is ?

Lake George copper-mine, N.S.W. Manager
Trewenack taking a rest after putting the new smelting
system into shape; Manager Collins, formerly of Went-
worth mines, in charge. Stated now that the cost oi
working and treating a ton of ore has been brought to a
shade under 17s. a ton, and that only 3 per cent, of coke
is used in smelting. The ore will need to be verj low
grade not to pay now. What a prospect there is for the
opening up of other copper ore-bodies in the country it
this can really be done with a mine handicapped by 20
miles road-haulage !

Victory Forest Reefs—another N.S.W. mine in

Cobar Gold Mines (old F<prt Bourlce), N.S.W.:
Reconstruction determined on in London —a new co. to
be formed, capital £200,000, all shares to carry a liability
of 55., with a certainty of calling up 4s. Amongst tne
estimates of necessary expenditure is £7500, for sinting
and equipping a shaft 400 ft. deep. Writer would much
like to have the contract, at the money. Also, ,‘° r
a cyanide plant. A contract has been made with the
Johannesburg Consolidated Investment Co.—already inte-
rested to the extent of 26,000 shares —to have a call on
unapplied-for shares up to 30,000. The mine has again
been thoroughly sampled since Mr. Buckland has been
acting-manager. The average is quite as high as the
average ore-values given by Janin, Callahan, and others
who sampled it two years ago. The tailings from the
100-head American modern stamp-mill, which was ex-
pected to leave them too poor for cyanide, average 4dwt-
lgr., and a quantity of these have been put through the
small cyanide plant, some distance away, at a cost under
2dwt The chairman said that ex-manager O Gorman
frankly admitted his error in thinking he could recover
practically all the gold by amalgamation, and that the Uig
cyanide plant should have been erected in the first in-
stance. His confidence in the mine remained unshaken.
He offered to put his own money in the mine and retain the
management, but, when told that an entire change was
essential, he acquiesced and tendered his resignation.
The chairman also remarked that “the remuneration of
the new directors will be materially reduced from what it
has been.” They take it away with one hand and give it
back threefold with the other. Six directors, whose
names are given, are to have £l5O a year each —£50 extra
for the chairman. That makes £950. “ The directors
shall also in each year in which 10 per cent, has been paid
on the ordinary share-capital receive such asum, not exceed-
ing £2OOO, as shall amount to 5 per cent, of the net profits
of that year.” If it pays anything, this mine will pay more
than 10 per cent., in which case these directors, who have
never seen the mine, will draw £2950 yearly. That is more
money than received by all the directors of all the other
mines of Cobar and for 100 miles around —and one is mak-
ing a profit of over £lOO,OOO a year ! The mining manager
gets £lO a week ; the directors will draw over £5O a week.
This is a barefaced whatdyecallit, which surely will not be
endured in silence by the Australian shareholders.

Madame Berry West (Vic.) has made a good
showing since it struck the lead a few years ago. Gold,
£229,152; divs., £40,187, and many more in sight. A
cheaply-worked mine. It crushed during the last half-
year at a profit 3579 tons of tailings from the old Madame
Berry shaft which yielded ldwt. llgrs. to the ton. The
directors walked off—the grasping rascals !—with a total
of £3O in fees. 'The mining-manager, greedy man, once
accepted a bonus of £25, but that was a long time ago. If
Bull investors would like to run mines a little cheaper let
them send a commission to Ballarat. It would pay them.

The latest re Westralia : “ Perth. This place
is absolutely dead—no business doing, everybody over-
stocked, and small merchants either compromising or
assigning. Coolgardie. —Mining dying a natural death,
nearly all shut down ; saloons have reduced drinks to 9d.
and 6d. No mine giving divs. except Burbank’s.
Machinery being sold at any price. Kalgoorlie.— Livelier
than usual, owing chiefly to the alluvial controversy.
After all the trouble some of the shafts sunk on the Ivan-
hoe Venture lease have been abandoned. An attack made
on Lane re the treatment of Gt. Boulder tailings by the
Konnemann process. A slander action will probably'
come out of it, anyhow. Kanowna. —This is the liveliest
place I have seen for a long time. About 10,000 persons,
and about 7500 employed digging for alluvial. A much
higher proportion than usual are on payable gold, some of
the claims good enough for fortunes. Some three miles
away a rush has broken out on the Golden Valley leases ;
three out of seven shafts bottomed are on good gold.
There is a big prospect for this place, and hotels and
stores are going up in all directions. The finding of these
leads is, at this juncture, a most fortunate thing for the
country.” A few yields-by-crushing from Kanowna
cement claims : Kelly and party, 98 tons, 6690 z.; Travena,
176 tons, 5250 z. ; O’Connor, 200 tons, 5250 z. ; Evans and
party, 49 tons, 105 oz. ; Macdonald and party, 21 tons,
750 z.

The Londonderry mine (Coolgardie) has a strong-
room at the 200 ft. level for the storage of rich rock. This
was broken into last week, and specimens to value of £5OO
removed, and the theft so cleverly done that it was not
noticed for some days. Anyhow, it is satisfactory to
know that there is still gold in the Plunderderry rich
enough to steal.

Up to the end of April the mines at Kalgoorlie
produced 16 tons 16cwt. of gold. Five produced over a
ton each, while the Great Boulder turned out about a
third of the total.

A peculiarity in Croydon returns is that the
yield of Rogers’ No. 1 Golden Gate is published quarterly
instead of monthly. Result, monthly return of field’s out-
put always incomplete. The No. 1 being one of the
largest producers, the returns should be given regularly.
If not, why ?

The Croydon outside camps show signs of re-
covery. Here, scratching is now giving place to legiti-
mate development. There are several fortunes in store
(writes a Bulletin correspondent) for the experienced
capitalist who will take up some of the low-grade quarries
at the Mountain Maid and Tabletop, ana work them
systematically. If on the Towers, these quarries would
have paid divs. years ago.

The half-yearly report of Mt. Lyell, followed
by the Argus strictures thereon, caused that property to
slump upwards of a million in value, from which it has
since partially recovered. More was paid in divs. than the
mine earned,"and there was a slight falling-off in the value
of all metal contents, but the great trouble seems to be the
impossibility of working the big show without taking and
smelting that part of the lode the value of which is only
14s. per ton. Again Tiif. Bulletin wants to direct atten-
tion to the fact that while the production of Mt. Lyell is
double that of the Great Cobar copper-mine, the profits
are a long way short of double the amount. Not hard to
guess what this means when additional Cobar smelters
get to work. Some of the recent copper consignments
from the Great Cohar contained over 7oz. gold to the ton.
The increase in gold is, no doubt, partly made up by
smelting ore from the Peak mine. If wholly thus
accounted for, then from 4oz. to over 7oz. on such a big
output proves that the Cobar tributors have now a
mighty fine gold-mine in the Peak. Melbourne has at
last taken a strong interest in the development of the
Cobar-Chesney—otherwise, there is no interest in Cobar
stocks taken bv anybody.

Administration and depreciation are estimated ; latter
item is probably considerably under likely expenditure,
and the former was nearer 2s. per ton during past term ;
then again the practice of debiting mine prepa-ratory
works accounts with a portion of the working costs is a
bad one. This item figures as an asset of £9400, and
represents a charge of Is. 3d. per ton on all ore treated
to date. Profit and loss account is a proper place
for this alleged asset. When new smelter plant is in
operation the co. will treat at least 250,010 tons
per annum. This output would pay 14s. per share per
annum on basis 55s. ore, or 10s. 7d. per share on 51s. 7d.
ore—equal respectively on shares of £10 to 7 per cent, and
5J per cent, per annum, and would, in something under
eight years, mine, tram, smelt, and otherwise deal with
the whole of the pay-ore in the mine. In other words,
the investor assures himself of his 7 per cent, or 5J per
cent, as the case may be for the period named ; hut if he
accepts literally the following violent remarks of Sticht
he stands a better chance of becoming a celestial so-
journer than of getting hack his original £10 per share,
plus legitimate interest: —“ With regard to the value of
ore below No. 4 level the testimony of present exploration
is confusing. Much of the ore is known to be low grade.
. . . For the present, estimates of value of the ore body,
as a whole, must draw the line at No. k level, even more
firmly than estimates of the quantity only." Let it be
understood that the co. has done a very considerable
amount of work below the No. 4 level, both at the inter-
mediate and No. 5 levels, and the sickening significance
of the above is apparent.—Jay.

No provision is made in the Mt. Lyell balance-
sheet for depreciation, and, in fact, mine and plant
figure as a better asset by some £55,000 than they did
six months ago. Why ?—in face of Sticht’s trenchant
remarks All the plant used in the reduction of
the company’s ore is in first-class order, and, considering
local conditions, some of which have a grave bearing on
wear and tear of apparatus, has done a satisfactory
amount of work during the half-year.”

Since March, 1897, the Mt. Lyell Co. has raised
£153,093 by the issue of new shares, and now proposes to
raise another £150,000 by the same means.
. » * *
The Mr. Lvell people frankly admit that the
copper contents of their ore have fallen. They had been
led by Dr. Peters to expect an average of 41 per cent, ;
the present manager estimates an average of 31 per cent.
On that basis the profit would be about 19s. a ton. As
the co. is not in a position to .find a 4s. quarterly div., and
provide funds for the erection of extra furnaces, 25,000
new shares are to be issued at a premium of £3. This will
provide for an output of 1000 tons a day, which, at 19s. is
£950 profit. Averages of all mines fall with tonnage, and,
unfortunately, in this case the grade at the start was so
low that it cannot stand either a fall in average or in the
metal-market.

Stated that Queensland Govt, definitely de-
clined the proposal to run a railway to Cloncurry, because
the scheme embodied the long lease of land along the
route. What a tail our cat is growing ! The unspeakable
Papuan land-grab took place months ago, and not a
move was made against it by that or any other Govt, till
public indignation had been awakened per newspapers to
the gigantic nature of the steal. Writer is not yet able to
give the names and number of politicians wbo have a bit
up their sleeves in this filthy business, which may, and
apparently should, end in the degradation of more than
one highly-placed personage. Also, remembering the
beautifully philanthropic and patriotic speeches they
made, it iiow begins to look as if Lowles and Vine were
wolves in sheep’s clothing. Patriots !!

EitzGibbon’s Board has given occasion for
another outbreak re the encouragement of local coal-mines
in Vic. The Metropolitan Board has accepted an outside
tender for 1500 tons of coal in preference to a local offer
asking 2d. a ton more. The Board has a bigger excuse
than the City Council had when it did likewise, since the
C.C. only saved ltd. per ton on its deal. Fitz used the
usual fat argument: “Must guard the people’s interests.”
The people’s interests!
The flotation of the Bright District and Smoko
Reef mine, Vic., is attempted, and accepting the statements
made as anywhere near the mark, it seems to offer a
reasonably fair run for one’s money.

Sydney s brace of newly-made knights
jumped at the chance of exhibiting their brand-
new labels at the recent Guvmentouse meeting re

* * * »
The Royal Hotel, George-street, Sydney,
where a few people met last week to compile
felicitations to some newly-made K.B.’s, is the
hotel in which the first meeting was held to pass a
resolution denunciatory of W. C. Wentworth’s
bill in favor of establishing a colonial aristocracy
by means of titular distinctions. It was at this
latter meeting, 65 years ago, that Deniehy,
M‘Encroe, Parkes, Flood, and others, emerged
into Australian political history.
* • * •
Dear Bulletin. — Re Australian liners.
Once upon a time, an Australian liner was wrecked
in mid-ocean, on a desert island, in broad daylight.
All the saloon passengers were saved, and got
ashore with only the loss of their luggage. There
were plenty of provisions saved from the wreck,
and a fine water-supply was found on the island.
Yet, owing to the fact that none of the passengers
were possessed of evening-dress clothes, and a
first-saloon passenger on a liner cannot dine with-
out evening-dress, the whole of that well-bred
ship’s company slowly died of starvation, and
their skeletons are there to-day to prove my asser-
tion, This story points a moral and airs a griev-
ance. I went on board a liner to get to London,
and laid myself out to have a good loaf, in pyjamas,
flannels and slippers. How was I deluded ! I
found that, though I escaped choir-practice,
■prayer-meetings in the saloon, the services on
Sunday, card tournaments, sports, balls and
dances, yet one thing I could not escape, and that
was dressing for dinner. I soon ran out of linen
and patience. Since then, I always travel second-
class, where you can go around all day with a
flannel coat and trousers, where shirts need not
be changed three times a day, where people’s souls
are not considered so much worth saving as for’ard,
and where, if wrecked as above, etiquette would
not run so far as starvation. Wanted, a ship run
on strictly atheistic principles, where “ God Save
the Queen ” would not be played more than twice
a day, and where a man would be allowed, in the
tropics, to dine in a loin-cloth, if he felt so inclined.
—Locust.
* * * •
Lyttelton (M.L.) Times is very wroth with
Admiral Pearson for adhering to strict etiquette
in not advising the local dignitaries of his ap-
proaching arrival in the Royal Arthur: “It was
the custom of Admiral Bridge—who did not, by
the way, make an ostentatious use of the title of
‘excellency’— to advise the local authorities by
telegraph of the time when he expected to be in
any particular harbor. That was perhaps not
strict etiquette, but it was ordinary politeness and
sound common-sense—two things that are closely
allied. And, if the new Admiral did the correct
thing in coming into Lyttelton harbor un-
announced, and waiting in solemn state the visits
of local officials, the Mayor of Lyttelton recipro-
cated by calmly ignoring the existence of the
Admiral and the Royal Arthur. No doubt he had
read in his paper that the flagship had arrived ;
but, having no official intimation of the fact, he
simply waited developments. He is probably
still waiting.” What a tail our cat’s got!

Railway Commissioner Mathieson is moving
the Vic. Govt, to the end that he may be per-
mitted to run a pub. in connection with inland
trains. He desires to attach a car that will have
all the advantages of a bar, peroxided beauty in-
cluded, The Bulletin presumes, so that the
Department may scoop much profit by the way,
and travel may enlarge the passenger’s head as
well as his mind.

Dear B. —Apropos the typical Australian
divorce-court husband who “just stepped out”
and never returned, I know a case in which a
wife, after placing his dinner before her lord and
master, took down her bonnet from one nail and
a jug from another, as per usual, saying, “ I’ll go
and get your beer.” Then she “just stepped
°u fc never returned.— D.E.N. P.S. —It
should be mentioned that the night was dark, and
the cyclist had no lights, and there was a lorry in
it, too. The husband “stepped out” in time to
hear her say, “ Damn you and your beer ! ” before
she died in the hospital.
Tommy Ah Noh was an English-edu-
cated, Australian-born Chow, who tookun-
to himself a white wife, in blissful uncon-
sciousness (or placid disregard) of the
fact that the lady had been a temporary
bride on several previous occasions. One
night, however, Tommy, having come
home unexpectedly, vvas roused from his
slumbering Maria’s side by a tapping at
the window and a gentle call for “Maliah !
' Maliah ! ” Tommy went outside, and
there found his compatriot, Ah Hoo —
with the result that the long-clawed
Lothario, after being badly knocked, was
kicked on*, of the front gate. Next, of
course, came the proceedings for assault,
crossed by a summons for trespass. Ah
Hoo s lawyer, proceeding, as he thought,
to serve up Ah Noh upon toast, inquired
if he, Tommy, was aware that his wife
had “ lovers ” before her marriage, and
that, in fact, she had been at one time
living with this very Ah Hoo? Where-
upon Tommy, in good English : “No,
sir ; I did not know it ; and I don’t con-
sider the question a fair one to ask. You
are a married man*yourself. How would
you like to have things your wife did be-
fore you married her spoken about in
Court ? ” “ Soilence in the Coorrt! ”

Papa Pinnock, P.M., of Brisbane, is an
interesting antique. Three weeks ago a
man was charged with having slightly bruised a
policeman’s lip. Two months without the option.
Later on the master of a steamer was summoned
before the same amiable old magistrate for allow-
ing his vessel to be loaded so as to submerge in
salt-water the centre of the disc indicating the
load-line. Fined forty skillings ! This in the face
of recent shipping disasters.

before a Northern magistrate. Casus belli: Busi-

to a friendly medico, and suggested they should
go partners in the biz. Whereupon Aisculapius

When Gov. Buxton paid his first visit to
Morphettville (S. A.) racecourse last year he blun-

dially shook him by the hand, much to his sur-
prise. The other day H.E. greatly disgusted the
old man by passing him with a stare. The Gover-
nor has now been struck off the veteran’s visiting--
list.

Fourteen iron-jawed ladies met in Melb.
lately to denounce the Federal Constitution Bill!
and everything and everybody connected with it..
One furious oratress, after casually remarking that
“Simon Fraser lived on the damnation of the ■■
country,” made a wildly dishevelled appeal to her
audience. “ Let us,” she cried, “go out into the
streets and bring in women who will show men >
how to vote on this question.” Not a single
woman present saw the shrieking humor of this
suggestion. Neither, of course, did the lady who
thus proposed to gather in the Flossies and:
Totties.

At a Yic. artists’ “smoke,” the other
Saturday night, little Dr. Springthorpe adminis-
tered a nauseous pill to some of the budding
geniuses present. He told them Painting had ;
thus far lagged behind Poetry and Music in this
land of artistic temperament, and although a
scornful Michael Angelo roared “ Bunkum !” '
from the refreshment-bar, the audience was mostly
with the doctor when he said that “ the color
scheme was so delicate that it became weak, even i
melancholy.” The fact is, Victorian Art—he.,.
the Art that plays the name-part—is sadly lack-
ing in stummick. The country has raised several!
buxom singers, and some absolutely fat poets ;
(look at Bedford, for instance), but the meaty,
painters—where are they? M‘Gubbin is plump;
enough, but he is really a by nature ■
who has not followed up his. ; top-note as a pro-
fession.

man.'’ The other turned a dim, mirthless, par-

racecourse, Vic., the other day. In a locker under

there five or six years.

Melb. storyette. An aged couple lived for
years in genteel poverty. The husband died. The
landlord stepped in and seized, among other
things, some hundreds of old newspapers the old
couple had slept on for years. These were sold
to the butcher. To the butcher ! Therefore it is
pleasing to know that the wrapping of meat in
any kind of printed paper has now been prohibited
by law in Melb. In other cities the complete tale
of the ’orrid murder arrives round the steak as
usual.
* • * «
The remarkable state of preservation in
which Mrs. “insurgent” Dwyer’s remains were
recently discovered in Sydney, after so many
years of interment, need not be so very surprising
in view of the fact that “mummified,” as applied
to a living person, is often—especially in a dry
climate like Australia’s—something more than a
mere figure of speech. Old men and old women—
more notably the latter—attain often in life to
such a desiccated condition that, as far as the
exterior parts are concerned, there is practically
nothing left for post-mortem decay. They’re pretty
well dust all the time.
Remarkable thing in connection with the
skeleton recently found in a cave at Woollahra,
Sydney, was that the denudation of flesh should
be so complete in so short a time. The Govern-
ment Pathologist declared that, but for the
abundance of proof that the owner of the bones
had been alive less than six months before, he
would have unhesitatingly fixed the date of death
at least several years back. The proximity of a
red-ants’ colony to the place where the bones were
found, is the probable explanation of the anomaly.

The late Dr. Badham’s proposal to teach
youths and girls in the backbloeks by means of
correspondence is again receiving favour from the
heads of Sydney University. Big-hearted Charles
Badham held that every student who could not
afford to go to the University was entitled to have
the University go to him. He chafed at beiug the
professor of a privileged few.

The great Australian holiday nuisance :
PARKES, Thursday.—Much indignation was expressed
this morning when it became known that a public holiday
had been proclaimed, this making three this week. About
a dozen applicants for land had come in to lodge applica-
tions for the Cobang blocks, and were prevented from
doing so. One of the applicants came from Gundagai,
and will, therefore, have to stay a whole week to transact
his business.
Public holidays seem to be conceded in N.S. W. on
lottery lines—any sort of gamble to while away
the tedium of a Cabinet meeting.
She was the winsome daughter of a
publican, and when the local revenue-officer
invaded the premises in search of irregulari-
ties, the damsel cheerfully ottered to un
lock the cellar door for him. The couple
traversed a darksome underground pas-
sage, the official leading, but when he
stopped at the cellar door and turned he
was astonished to find the young lady’s
hair down and her attire seriously disar-
ranged, as if she had been passing through
a severe struggle. Before the officer
had time to grasp the situation the dam-
sel’s arms were around him, and she hissed
into his ear—“ Clear out at once! or I’ll
scream out that you're assaulting me—and
where will you be then?” The official
saw he was “ on toast’’—handed back the
key, and departed, musing on the guile-
lessness of woman.
* » * •
To get on in Melb. (or anywhere
else) an honest man needs an immensity
of cheek. The champion cool hand turned
up, the other day, in answer to an advt. re
a lost purse. The loser herself opened the
door, and to her the visitor handed the mis-
sing article with the remark : “ You adver-
tised 10s. reward, madam, but as there was
.£5 in the purse I didn’t consider 10s.
enough reward, so I just took a quid for
my trouble. Good-day! You’ll find the
£4 all right.” And before the astonished
woman could recover her breath, he was
gone.
* * * *
He the liquor-question in Victoria.
Apart from the Sunday-trading trouble,
the police are also beaten by the country
shanty-keeper, who openly trades under
their noses, knowing that an excise officer
only can secure a conviction against him.
The one-eyed Act, if the exciseman sheets
a charge home on the shanty-man, rubs
it in hotly—first offence, fine of <£2s;
second conviction, <£so ; and third offence,
unlimited fine and two years’ quod.
There are two sides to every question.
The comic man who jokes about the toughness of

* * * •
A novel funeral took place t’other day in
a S. Queensland town, all the mourners (six of
them) being women. Things went all right until
the undertaker (who was the only male present)
had to lower the coffin into the grave—a task to
which he proved unequal. On requesting the
assistance of the ladies in the matter, a wild,
infuriated feminine shriek arose, and the mourners
departed indignant, leaving the dear defunct
delicately poised on the edge of the yawning
sepulchre. Always be sure there’s a man-mourner
present at your interment. You never know what
may happen.

The Anglican Synod at Adelaide declares
there is nothing m cremation opposed to any
principle of religion. Even churches move. Civili-
sation’s progress is retarded by the labor of
dragging them up hill, but they move.
One time their creed the priests excused
For burning men who still had breath ;
To-dav the creed has grown confused,
And burning’s Jawful—after death !
* * * *
The “metaphysical healer” is doing a
roaring business in Adelaide. Even a well-known
medico has climbed down, and is now under the
treatment of the man with the oily hair and
drooping eyes, who lays his hands on, prays, and
sings hymns in his own unapproachable style.
* * * •
The upper-classes section of the Kalgoorlie
public-school was, the other day, being “shown
off ” before the minister’s wife and the chief
inspector, and was making a sorry exhibition of
itself in the matter of Westralian geography.
Then, upon a suggestion, the inspector asked how
many of the youngsters—about TOO of them —were
Westralian born. Not a single hand was held up,
and on reference it was found that every child
present was a tothersider.
* » * *
The city of Rockhampton is a whale on
hygiene—a fact that perhaps accounts for the low
death-rate in that sultry city. Rockhampton’s
latest departure is having its milk chemically
tested—six per cent, of butter to be the standard
of good milk. Some of the milk purveyed ran
down to p.c. of oleaginous matter. Wonderful
how the test improved the quality. Milk that
assayed 2 p.c. on Monday gave place to milk
assaying 5 p.c. on the Tuesday, and higher still
on the Wednesday. Other cities might take a
lesson.

Judge Hodges re a recent Melb. Supreme
Court case :
However, moral or immoral, honest or dishonest, it is
the duty of the court to administer the law even for the
benefit of a knave.
The fool goes to the law, hoping he will get jus-
tice, and the cute rogue goes to the justice, hoping
he will get law.
* * * *
The health of cattle in Bananaland is a
matter of infinitely more importance than the
health of human beings. Hence the appointment
of tick-inspectors, the drawing of quarantine
lines, etc. At the same time, there is no attempt
made to secure the establishment of publio abat-
toirs or the inspection of slaughter-houses.

“Be that Mt. Gambier hotel-gaol yarn,”
writes a Bulletin correspondent. “In the fifties,
Melb. gaol was known as Wintle’s hotel (Wintle
was Governor). His eldest son told the writer that
Wintle the elder received a circular from an Eng-
lish hardware firm, soliciting an order, and at the
same time saying that their traveller ‘ had often
patronised the hotel and spoke favorably of it.’ ”

Temperance-lecturess Harrison Lee got in
a good one the other night about the threepenny
claiming to be better than the half-crown, because,
while the latter went to theatres and racecourses,
and such wickedness, the 3d.-bit was principally
given to going to church. But alas for the holy
threepenny’s immoral relations with the long-
beer !
* * * *
When the Presbyterian Church, in Vic.,
lately admitted six ladies to the new order of
deaconesses, the officiating parson said : “ We, as
ministers of the pastorate, receive you with pecu-
liar gratification ... in the exalted hope that in
you we welcome the forerunners of a long line of
faithful and pious women.” Ah! but it’s a long
line that has no turning. Old Nick can afford to
wait for the coining of deaconesses who will not
be peculiarly faithful and pious.

A Perth citizen writes re the tragrant city
of the Six Families :
As if tiie cost of living and house-rents were not suffi-
cient burden on the poor devils who pay ta'xes, the
Perth municipal rates have steadily risen to 6s. in the
P. For some years there has been an increasing agita-
tion for better sanitary arrangements in Perth, but the
t’othersiders who are the clamorers are so neatly ex-
cluded from the franchise that (hey cannot do more than
cry aloud without having the slightest chance of redress.
The councillors are elected on a very restricted muni-
cipal roll, and the ratepayers represented are mostly of
Westralian origin, bred, born and reared amidst the
filth of ignorant meanness. They have done without
any sanitary or underground sewerage system. Why,
therefore, this idiotic and wholly unwarrantable attack
on their old-established germs ? A morning journal in
Perth has been publishing a series of articles on the sani-
tary state of that city -by an impertinent t’othersider,
one Horrocks, M.D., and the council has been casting
about for some means to make this meddling fellow sit
up. In his concluding remarks, Horrocks expressed his
indebtedness to Dr. O’Connor for information supplied.
The unfortunate O’Connor is the city Health Inspector,
and the wrath of the councillors knew no bounds. A
special meeting was summoned and the indignant citizens
blackguarded O’Connor to their heart’s content. “This
man was their officer and had no right to tell stories out
of doors. His views were for their private ears. They paid
him,” &c. Then a patriotic sand-groper complained
with tears in his voice that any man should have told
such lies about his beloved city. “Perth is one of the
healthiest cities in the world." (Death-rate at 23 per
1000 annually against 13 per 1000 in Sydney.) Another
declared, “It is the sanatorium of Australia." No men-
tion was made of the fact that in Perth alone, with its
population of about 24,000, there were 1400 cases of ty-
phoid in 1897 ! Casually a councillor named Sweet
elicited the fact that a month before Dr. O’Connor had
supplied the council with his opinions on the sanitary
condition and requirements of Perth, but the Mayor,
Premier Forrest’s little brother Alec., had kept back the
treasonable documents as calculated to discredit the City
of Typhoid. No one suggested that Perth should be
cleaned and drained ; all the rage was about the fact that
someone had commented on its unspeakable filthiness.
Very soon people will give up using street names in Perth,
and visitors will be directed by so many smells to the
right or left, as the case may be.
* « * *
A peculiar case came before Mr. Justice
Cooper in Brisbane the other day. A young half-
caste named Poingdestre, son of an aboriginal
woman called Lucy, of Normanton, died a short
time ago. Deceased was insured in the A.M.P.
for <£soo. He made a will shortly before his death
leaving everything to a Normanton storeman,
Welsh, by whom he had been brought up and
educated. Welsh was to dispose of everything for
the benefit of Poingdestre’s mother and sister.
But as deceased was only 17j years old at time of
death, the will was consequently invalid, and the
matter passed into the hands of the Curator of
Intestate Estates. Judge Cooper finally decided
that the estate belonged to the Crown, but recom-
mended that the Curator’s suggestion be adopted
— viz., that an annual payment of .£5O be made to
Welsh to he divided between mother and sister of
deceased.

Some definitions :
Marriage : The final paroxysm of a madness that
destroys two fools.
Love : Paralysis of the intellectual faculties which com-
pare.
Courtship : The attempt to make a girl think you are
as good a catch as you think you are.
Life : The thing that makes you jump when your toe is
trodden on.
Death : That which enables you to bear insults with
patience.
Laziness : The successful control of the passion for
work.
—Kati-Kati, M.L.

A lawyer may be a good Christian, but it is
most untilial conduct.
A good “ action ” is its own reward.
When a man has an enemy he goes to law, and
makes two more.
Many an ass covers his ears with horse-hair.
A law-suit generally wears well.
Possession is nine points of the lawyer.
