A WOMAN'S LETT ER
Sydney, May 31, 1898.
Mr Dear Myee—
The Birthday races at Rand wick were frowned
upon by as bleak a wind as blows, and as ugly a
one. Nothing slays beauty with such deadly
rapidity as our west winds in winter. Under
their blighting influence the bluest eyes in the
world turn sandy-green, the shiniest, finest hair is
hard and coarse enough to stuff a horse-hair suite,
peaches-and-cream are transformed into boiled
gooseberries, and the only bright spot in a femi-
nine armory is the gleaming edge of the temper.
And the grand-stand of a racecourse has a
supremacy over all things that are evil when a
biting wind blows.
Those whose overcoats and jackets buttoned
tightest were the happiest people present. In
Sure frocking, the smartest dress on the lawn was
. Irs. G-. B. Simpson’s black moire antique and
jewelled belt. But the smartest dresses had a
forlorn appearance beside a homely cape or jacket.
I saw nothing very remarkable in the way of
overcoating. Our climate does not lure us
sablewards with the recklessness of a lower
temperature, and Australian wraps are permitted
to be bargains, duplicates, and all the rest of those
indiscretions that are shunned by other self-
respecting garments. I spent a good deal of
admiration on a vice-regal overcoat with a velvet
collar and Ouida-esque lines such as no woman can
resist. Then there was the droll overcoat of a well-
known haberdasher, shorter by a yard and a half
than the coat underneath. One of the few sable
capes present was worn by Miss Gotthelf ; another
belonged to Mrs. Gadsden, who resembled her
well-known portrait as elegantly as ever, in a sable
wrap with tails.
The latest thing m portraits is Tom Roberts’
study of the latest thing in knights, James Fairfax.
I went round to Mr. Roberts’ studio one day last
week to see the picture, and judged it to be as
subtle, faithful, and characteristic of the painter
as most of this artist’s portraits are. Streams
of visitors have been in and out through the
studio these last two weeks, and the audience is
sometimes three deep round the speaking likeness.
But there was a peculiarly fascinating baby pre-
sent at his father’s studio the day I went, and I
confess I can tell you more about the baby, who
was a little Tom Roberts, than about the knight
on the easel.
From one of those little papers that tell you all
about Shakespeare and the musical glasses for a
penny, I learn an interesting fact about the Prin-
cess of Wales. An article on eyes illustrates its
matter with photographs of orbs, taken away from
the rest of the face, and planted down in the
letter-press, with eyelids open beyond all chance
of a wink. Among others, the Princess of Wales
has been defrauded of her optics. The statement
that hers are among the most beautiful eyes in
Europe is followed by the statement that each eye
looks a way of its own. Briefly, that her eyes are
crosst d. I don’t call this a counter statement,
because the article explains later that there is no
beauty in the world like the piquancy of a cross-
eye. In the words of the magazine, to be crossed
is the “crowning beauty of a woman’s eyes.”
Surely these little magazines have some effect on
the rise of butter.
Hetty Holroyd’s concert attracted an unusually
large house to the Town Hall last Saturday. Miss
Holroyd is the third soprano whom Mr. Stetfani
has launched successfully on the public within one
year. Florence Schmidt gave her first concert
last June, and is now known all over Australia.
She still continues a triumphal tour through her
native Queensland, and sails for Europe in July or
August. Florence Mack achieved an artistic
success at her concert a few weeks ago, and will
probably appear in light opera shortly. And now
comes the youthful Hetty, with a brilliant organ
that must make a way for itself when her girlish
nervousness wears off. Her fluent and delicate
rendering of Qui la Voce covered the platform
with one of the biggest floral exhibitions I have
seen.
It seems as if the tide of the Cartwright Com-
pany has turned at last. The revival of a “ Mar-
riage of Convenience ” drew a crowded house last
week, and on Saturday night an over-crowded
dress-circle witnessed the first production in Syd-
ney of the “Squire of Dames.” Miss Mor'ley
made a re-appearance in the “ Marriage ” revival.
You will be glad to hear that the scars on her
pleasing face are not noticeable from the house,
and are quickly paling away in the light of day.
There was nothing new in the performance, but
the company played with a vivacity that grows as
the house grows. I particularly admired Miss
Stewart, as Marton, and any lady who wishes to
train up a maid in the way a discreet maid should
go is advised to take lessons from the diplomatic
soubrette in question.
Wit and repartee flash as bountifully over the
“Squire of Dames ” as limelight on a melodrama.
On the whole, the company are admirably suited,
and Noni Rickards, as a little schoolgirl, is a
huge improvement on Noni as anybody else. All
the ladies wear their best frocks, but the American
heiress shows a weakness for simple white effects
that is hardly justified by the orthodox American
heiress’ wardrobe. Miss Lamb is a charming
grass-widow in her black dinner-gown, with
jetted bodice and front of jetted net hanging
apart from the train. I noticed on Saturday that
her indifference to make-up has begun to yield,
and that the audience put an extra amount of
warmth into her reception, and sent a few flowers
her way.
Miss Stewart’s gowns are always worthy of
notice. In fact, it is a long time since we have
seen such smart and thoughtful frocks as this co.
has been revealing in every play. A subdued
but telling note is struck by Miss Stewart’s grey
crepe de chine over golden-yellow silk, with fleet-
ing and effective suggestions of golden under-
flounces. This lady owns one of those peculiar
figures that are the pride of London just now.
Not so long ago to have big hips was considered
fatal to grace. I once heard a lady deplore to her
dressmaker this failing in her physique. “ I have
such horrible hips,” she said. “ ’lps you call
them ; I call them ’umps /” was the dressmaker’s
withering comment. But modistes know better
than that to-day. Hips must bespoken of with the
utmost respect, for they are going to occupy a lot
of room in the fashions to be. Already the pannier
shows signs of a revival, and where the pannier
goes the hip follows.
A few years ago there was a wild storm in a
Sydney tea-cup because the Admiral didn’t go
into dinner before somebody who wasn’t as big as
an Admiral, in an Admiral’s opinion. An out-
sider might have seen reason in the furious raging
of that storm, if the one who went in first had
begun his dinner before the one who went in
second. But as there was no privilege of that
kind attached to the first place, the storm merely
revived memories of school-girl squabbles. And
now a thunder-cloud has broken over Maori-
land, and again an Admiral’s anxiety for the
courtesy due to him is at the bottom of the dis-
turbance.
It seems that Admiral Pearson and the Royal
Arthur anchored in Lyttelton harbor, and
were not called upon by the Mayor and Har-
bour Board until a whole day had elapsed. The
Lyttelton folk explained afterwards that they
had not been told an Admiral was coming. And
though they appear to have hastened out to the
Royal Arthur as soon as they understood it was
there, the Admiral’s dignity was “ riz.” He
left word he had gone for a sail, and was not
present to receive them. Then followed a stiff
refusal of all invitations, and, next morning, the
Royal Arthur got up before day-break and de-
parted, leaving behind it the impression that it
was not angry, but sorry—for Lyttelton. The
entertaining little episode has one reassuring
aspect. If our brave naval men fight so fiercely
over the etiquette of afternoon calls, surely they
will protect their country from such uninvited
guests as Spanish sailors with bearded lips and
clasp-knives.
What a pity that an organist has to play with
his back turned. For fully five minutes before
the vice-regal party arrived at the University
Chancellor’s reception, M. Wiegand played
“ God Save ” in his most patriotic style,
but vice-royalty actually entered the Great Hall
amidst a most depressing silence, despite the
agonising efforts of the vice-chancellor, the regis-
trar, and the beadle to secure repetition of the
hytnn. The most striking feature was, without
doubt, the new robes of Chancellor MacLaurin—if
all is not gold that glitters, certainly the greater
part was in this case. The aim and object of
opera-cloaks, too, seems to be that of originality—
Oriental draperies, tartans, chiffons, completely
ousting the orthodox cashmere and feather trim-
ming—in fact, one professor’s wife was strongly
suspected of having rushed off to the shivoo, at the
bidding of an impatient husband, before com-
pleting her toilet, so strongly did her outer gar-
ment resemble a dressing-jacket.
Culchaw was suitably in evidence, and when
the guests had finished wrestling with the Greek
motto at the top of the programmes, they turned
their attention to the “ objects of interest ” (other-
wise than animate) displayed round the walls.
These included such interesting trifles as “Dis-
articulated skull,” “ Casts of snakes death
adder,” and “Bernhardt’s drawing-desk, with
Zeiss’ improved camera lucida,” to say nothing
of a “Rocking microtome.”
The University Ball was a cheery hop this year,
with lots of youths and maidens and not too many
grave presences. Society sent more representa-
tives than usual, and seemed to find the jovial
atmosphere of the undergrad a pleasant change
from the frigid temperature of our public balls.
The genial union of animal spirits and culture
might melt the reserve of the most thickly en-
crusted matron. I noticed pretty Miss MePhill-
amy in white, embroidered o’er with pallid tur-
quoises, but most of the gowning suggested
that the people had come to have a good time
rather than show off their clothes.
“New life has crept into the Tivoli with Peggy
Pryde, the vivacious daughter of poor Jenny Hill,
whose vital spark has flickered right away. Peggy
is an indefatigable little person, with an immense
reserve of animation, and a personal appearance
that reminds you slightly of Ada Willoughby. In
ten minutes or so I saw her change her dress and
impersonation four times, and her dash and liveli-
ness redoubled themselves with every change. In
evening dress she sang a song that was half funny
and half sad. She disappeared for a minute
and came back in coster attire to whisk off a de-
scription of a coster wedding. Skipped out of
sight again, and returned in another costume with
a rattling song and story about a visit to Paris.
Her frisky smartness never flags for a minute, and
I felt sorry for the large dark gentleman who
came after her on the programme.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Toorak is almost in tears. One of its bachelor
idols has fallen from his pedestal. A certain
talented college-lecturer—he of the forget-me-not
blue eyes, angel voice and dimple chin—has
betrothed himself to a lady somewhat older than
his beauteous self. The lady (who will bring
money into the firm) has always been a rampant
advocate of platonic affection, hence Toorak feels
aggrieved, for it appears that the platonic theory
lias been used for some time as the herring on this
trail of true love. The girls thought he was at
least ‘ free from danger ’ in that direction.
“ Mrs. Premier Turner has lost and found her
earrings, all in one week. When she lost them,
Premier George informed the police that they had
been stolen. The detectives laughed. In a few
days, Mrs. T. found her barbaric ornaments,
which had simply been ‘ swept up with the dust.’
The fact that the room had been swept out mean-
while was forgotten. The incident is a mild repe-
tition of the loss of Mrs. Freeman Thomas’s jewels
on board ship, when it was found that the lady
had washed them down the sink when cleaning
them. But it is hard that the Melbourne thieves
should be suspected by these careless personages
of things they have not done. It must hurt their
feelings. It is a reflection upon their enterprise.
“ A sacred concert —‘Stabat Mater ’ and a few
extras —at Dr. Strong’s church (Melb.) the other
Tuesday night introduced a fetching little organ
solo, composed and played by W. E. Nott. If the
aforesaid ‘Romance et Berceuse’ (why this
foreign language?) was a strictly original Austra-
lian work, Nott may be expected to do something
brilliant in due course. Which is more than can
be said, so far, for Miss Adele Fawcett, who ranks
among Melbourne’s best contralto aspirants, and
seems now more than ever incapable of letting
herself go. One can appreciate the artistic singer
with no voice worth mentioning, or even the fine
voice that doesn’t know how to sing, but the
fairly cultivated vocalist who fears to put force
into her good notes is irritating.
“What with flannelette clothing and celluloid
hair-ornaments, the woman of to-day is as com-
bustible as a kerosene-kindler. A local victim,
overseeing the baking of her boy’s birthday-cake,
comi>lained off and on to the attendant Abigail of
a strong whiff of singeing, never suspecting that
her own underwear \Vas a-smoulder. At last the
cdor grow so pronounced that she thought the
house must be on fire, and rushed out of the
detached kitchen to inspect. Once in the air, her
skirts broke into a blaze, and flared up round her
head, where a ‘ tortoise-shell ’ circlet and dagger
pin were soon flashing and spluttering like a fiery
crown. Before the human torch was extinguished
the premises took fire in two or three places—
though, unlike its hapless mistress, the house was
ultimately saved.
“ A side-issue of the tragedy was the unlooked-
for taciturnity of deceased’s six-year-old son. At
school when the fatality happened, he was de-
tained there, at the victim’s wish, until after the
funeral, so as to spare him sad memories of his
mother. When he did go home he asked no ques-
tion as to his parent’s unexplained absence, nor
then nor afterwards expressed, even in the most
casual way. any consciousness of a change in the
domestic regime.
Wilson Barrett’s Othello, nice enough as colored
persons go, is rather spoilt, spectacularly speaking,
by the low cut of his corsage at the back. There’s
something too dowagerish in the rear view of those
bare shoulders of his, and the well-cushioned spine
which parts their plumpness, to suit one’s fore-
gone conception of the swarthy, sinewy Moor.
“Travellers’ tales testify that Jonahs aboard
the grounded ocean-liner deliberately intensified
their hereditary harmfulness by chainpagning
saloon-diners, officers and all, just because of a
birthday. Soured second-classers, who shared all
subsequent dangers and none of the preliminary
fun, declare that a clerical passenger, who left his
fellow-passengers to their vinous revels and went
upstairs primming his lips disapprovingly, spied
breakers ahead the minute he got on deck. The
poor castaways had a horrid time on the rescuing
vessel, where they overcrowded the rightful occu-
pants, and added so much to the work that the
stewards told them to wait on themselves. And
picture the feelings of that bride-elect doomed to
meet her fiance in London with ‘onebootoffand the
other on,’ like ‘ Diddle-diddle Dumpling, my son
John,’ while the liveliest Parisian trousseau and
ever so much jewellery lay at the bottom of the
Red Sea.
“The important question, ‘Shall we forgive
her?’ was asked for the first time at the Royal on
Saturday night re heroine Ida Gresham, haunted
by a stormy past in Queensland under the fist
of blackguard Holloway— ‘ engineer ’by name,
drunken navvy by nature. Ida herself, explain-
ing her presence in the drought-stricken, cut-
throat railway camp, says that when her‘people
at home died ’ (en masse, presumably), she crossed
the seas to former lover Holloway’s frowsy side,
in ignorance of his subsequent degradation.
“ But, considering that her ‘most filthy bargain
shows not the slightest trace of ‘ better days,’ it’s
strange how so high-toned a young person as
literary Ida can at any time have set her affec-
tions on hulking, ungrammatical, grimy Garth.
Anyway, she kept her neck out of the noose of a
legal marriage; and, after a term of unwedded
squalor, barely escaped with her life and returned
to England and mission-work under the wing of
Parson Titheradge. In no time the pretty
penitent re-entered matrimony—holy this time—
along with Hubby Willard who, while uneasily
curious as to wifie’s unexplained Bananaland
exile, smothers his suspicions till after the honey-
moon, when he takes to raking up the sore subject
with little satisfaction to himself and less to the
questionee. In the end, the skeleton is
unearthed through the exertions of spinster
“Joanna’’ (Eugenie Duggan) who helps Ida
Gresham with the house-keeping and nurses
a soft spot for Ida’s husband. The latter, by
the way, goes blind with the shock of wifie’s dis-
grace and drives his soiled dove out of the ark to
earn a fat living as sub-editress of an influential
woman’s journal. Parson Titheradge is winsome
from the start, when he appears at the railway-
camp all in pure white, in the midst of a water
famine with all the navvies ready to do murder for
a drink. Later on, as an English vicar, he pleads
for the prodigal to her unforgiving husband
(cherished in his blindness by Saint Joanna) de-
claring that, were the penitent free from to-
morrow, he would tender her his own clerical
hand. A woman might have done worse, perhaps,
than get a divorce from stiff-necked Oliver, on the
grounds of “Joanna,” and take his reverence at
his word.
“ In the Bananaland ‘ gulch ’-exile, Ida Gresham
flits about her unhallowed bower in grey home-
spun and red bandana turban. Later on, as model
English wife and mother, she affects pale yellow
muslin with black baby ribbons, chaste green
waist-belt, and posy of violets. However, it’s not
till she’s driven forth to grass-widowhood that she
indulges in grey velvet Russian-blouse dress, and
sweet silvery bonnet plumed with white. Thus
arrayed, she flops heavily and boo-hoos aggres-
sively on the spot where her baby’s chest would be
if, as inferred, the innocent actually occupied its
stage - cradle. That wise (if spiteful) virgin,
‘Joanna’ (Miss Duggan), in white cuffed and
collared black frock and plastered hair, represents
an obsolete type of ‘saint.’ The present-day
‘ unco guid ’ are often sentimentally soft-headed
in their dealings with sinners. A while back, a
local dowager took into her house a pretty young
‘brand,’ to try the effect of ‘home influence,’
though, at the advice of ‘ brand ’ authorities, she
kept her shut up as much as possible in the best
bedroom with nice books and fancy-work, until it
should be seen how the penitent shaped. Neat
frocks and trim house-shoes were supplied to the
prisoner to ‘restore her self-respect.’ She pleaded
for red stockings. Protectress hemmed and hesi-
tated, but gave in when she heard that ‘poor
Papa,’ in the ‘dear old days,’ liked to call the
‘brand’ his ‘Polly Red-stockings.’ Soon the
kindly dame noticed that hawkers, carriers, &c.,
took to loitering ever so long on the front veran-
dah before they rang the bell. Light broke upon
her when, coming in suddenly at her own gate,
she saw the postman staring open-mouthed at a
pair of saucy, scarlet ankles and black shoes dang-
ling out of the guest-chamber window. Bursting
in at a side door, the shocked philanthropist sur-
prised the penitent giggling hard behind the
sheltering pages of ‘ Home Heroines,’ while she
reclined comfortably on the bed drawn up close to
the casement, and twinkled her trim imp-
ishly on the sill.
“ Most women sported their new winter things
at Marshall-Hall’s recent orchestral concert.
The purple, heliotrope, lilac, and lavender family
is evidently to be the color-connection of the
coming season, vice the green-clan deposed.
“Things are looking up in the Cabbage Garden.
The first cauliflower-cargo of the season left for
Sydney and the North a couple of Saturdays ago.
Forsome time we have been importing kitchen-stuff
from N.S.W. —a record state of affairs. Methinks
that the heathen Chinee, as well as his white
brother in greengrocery, has been making his
modest pile out of the ‘recent drought.’ Tup-
pence per bunch for welly ni callot is a little stiff
for that humble root. As for cauliflowers and
tomatoes, we put ’em on the table for ornament
now. Lately we unfortunate, drought-stricken
Melbournites have had to elevate the despised
dried onion into an edible —boiled with white
sauce it isn’t nearly as repellent as its smell. A
girl of my acquaintance ate one for dinner lately,
and all her friends knew it before night. With
green-stuff at a premium, we copy Sam Weller—
‘ eats our biled mutton without capers and doA’t
care about horse-radish if we can get beef.’
“ A haughty person from ’Ome has been deco-
rating sassiety for a couple of years or so with a
single eye-glass, a paralysing mannah, and, in uni-
form, a positively killing swagger. And he has a
brother who sings. A Salon girl, making up a pro-
gramme for an afternoon-tea shivoo, asked him if his
brother would give his name as a performer. But
he shook his auburn head doubtfully, and replied,
‘ My brothah only sings in public when he quite
knows who his audience are. I don’t think my
brothah would sing before the Austral Salon.’
Paralysing speeches of this sort have gained for
the lofty one the name of ‘ The Shilling Shockah.’
“Lord Kilmorey—‘Kilmurray’ is the ’kreot
sound, and please don’t forget it—has just done a
deal in house property—sold a crib at Richmond
(Eng.) for ,£22,000 to the authorities, who have
turned the mansion into an Industrial School with
30 boys. The boys have the free run of the grounds,
and one man to five boys is the proportion of the
teachers. A London paper remarks frankly that
it looks like a job—but newspapers have a coarse,
underbred way of putting things at times. You
must remember Lord K. at Randwick some
time ago, a well - preserved aristocrat in the
train of a charming mummer and her ‘ Momma.’
The ladies are again in Sydney, to which attrac-
tive spot the erstwhile Lord Newry intends to
return in a few weeks.

Coolgardie beat Kalgoorlie in the recent
Leg. Council election. It takes two or three years
to get on a Westralian roll; so the older town,
which supported its Mayor, A. G. Jenkins, had a
long start of the newer one, which ran J. W.
Kirwan—mostly on the strength of the fact that
it was he who proposed the “ deputation of 10,000
that waited for John Forrest on a hot day outside
a pub.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Dumas, the new member of S.A. Assembly
for Mt. Barker, is, like Speaker Jenkin Coles, a
native of Sydney. He is a brother-in-law of
Russell Barton, who once sat in N.S.W.
Assembly.
N. : “It has not been the slightest use,
even now, trying to raise a Gladstone upheaval
of sympathy on this continent. Sala writes that,
lecturing in Melb., his mention of Gladstone
hardly got a hand. ”
Mrs. Maybrick, the husband-poisoner, is
in luck—that is, if it is possible for a person
under life sentence to be in luck. When a cousin
of hers died in Maoriland, the other day, he left
her £4400 to accumulate till she can get out to re-
oeive it.
Premier Reid’s appointment of R. M. Sly,
LL.D., to an N.S.W. Acting Supreme Court
Judgeship, intensifies the growing feeling that in
scattering Acting-Judgeships among the non-
political bar he is despoiling political barristers of
an old privilege.
A knighthood is almost as good as a
baronetcy for the native-born Australian if the
well-ventilated theory of a certain Sydney scien-
tist be true. The theory is that the native-born
have not sufficient stamina to carry on the race
for more than three generations at most.
While Gladstone’s father was a monarch
amid the Liverpool slavers, Cook, the vinous
tragedian, appeared there one night drunk on the
stage. Audience hissed. Cooke staggered to the
footlights, and blurted out: “ Men of Liverpool!
Every brick in your accursed city is cemented
with the blood of a slave ! ”
“ Skin-shivering stories anent the death of
Mrs. Oscar Wilde, are being whispered about,”
writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent.
“The most-to-be-pitied woman on earth, Mrs.
Oscar Wilde, was a granddaughter of the late
Q.C. Horatio Lloyd, and lived for the last two
years with her two boys at Genoa.”
King Kayser, of the great Bischoff tin-
mine, Tas., has resigned, but it is said that he will
aot as consulting-engineer. He was Boss with a
big Bin Waratah. A stranger, looking for work,
arrived by the train one day, and meeting Kayser
enquired the whereabouts of the Bischoff mine.
“lam de Bischoff,” replied the big man; and he
was, too !
THE DEAN.
Bananaland Govt, appoints some queer
characters to the position of tick-inspectors. One
of these gentry, a gentleman of highly-scientifio
tendencies, paid a visit to a blacks’-camp imme-
diately on his appointment, subjected the niggers
to a microscopical examination, and wanted to
dip them in a composition of his own invention.
He is still at large.
The strongly-treated three-column portrait
which accompanied Sydney D.T.’s Gladstone
obituary was drawn by young Harold Ashton,
son of Julian R. Ashton. Australasian dailies
have failed dismally so far with their illustrations,
and the local draughtsman with sufficient breadth
of style to overcome the difficulties of the rotary
press will be able to make big cheques.
The David Syme Queensbuthdy leader was
all that could be expected of a man who has de-
clined a knighthood with thanks, and who may
yet be a baronet, although it did refer queerly to
the “giddy” heights of the throne, and talk of
her-gracious hearkening to the plaudits and joy-
bells that herald in her eightieth birthday with an
eye moistened by recollections of the past.
Gov. Hampden : “ There is no royal road
to old-age pensions any more than there was a
royal road to wealth.” Isn’t there? At any rate,
there is a Fitz-royal road to both, and the present
Dukes of Grafton, Richmond, and St. Albans—
descendants of illegitimate sons of Nell Gwjmne
and other mistresses of Charles 11.-are living
witnesses of the fact. The British Pension-list is
another witness.
Kennerley, over whose will there has been
such a legal flare at Hobart, was a cheerful old
man of hospitable nature, and also, at one time, a
political figure of importance in the Isle of Grim
Remembrances. While on a trip to Europe, he
visited Paris, and was never afterwards tired
of narrating his experiences. So fond was he of
describing his trips to St. Cloud, Fontainbleau,
and elsewhere, in what he called a “ Charibang,”
that he came in time to be known as “ Old Chari-
bang.”

Pressure is being brought to bear to make
insolvent a very prominent Australian politician,
whose affairs have been becoming more and more
involved for years past. The success of the pro-
ceedings would involve the collapse of a Ministry,
and greatly affect politics generally. The crisis
has been expected for months, and cannot be post-
poned much longer.
Dear B.—One of Gladstone’s philological
niceties. A relative of mine, who, late in the
70 s, was a guest at the G.O.M. ’s literary break-
fasts in Harley-street, London, returned from one
of them saying that Gladstone’s most startling
utterance that morning - had been, “ There is
really no such word as ‘ reliable ’ in the English
language ; ‘ trustworthy ’ is the correct word to
use.”— Chapel Place.
All four Sydney dailies combined to kill
John Gilbert, R.A., a second time last week,
although the famous illustrator died in October
last. The Evening News even devoted a leade-
rette to him. Next day the D.T. admitted its
error, the Gilbert alluded to in the cable being a
prominent Irish historian—but the others just
allowed the dead-and-buried Academician to die
again without comment.
Although rumour probably errs in sur-
mising that the projected addition of another
judge to N.S.W. Supreme Court Bench will be
for the exclusive purpose of taking on John-
Norton-business, The Bulletin would hardly be
surprised if John should yet require, not merely a
judge to himself, but a whole Full Court to deal
specially with the incidental developments of
John upon the legal war-path.
Present at recent Melb. Old Colonists’
Carnival was Mrs. A. C. Macdonald (wife of the
secretary of the Australian Geographical Society),
who remembers Ross’s visit to Hobart, in
1839, with the Erebus and Terror, on their Ant-
arctic exploration voyage. Mrs. Macdonald’s
father was a West Indian planter, who, having
freed his slaves before emancipation was made
compulsory, afterwards settled in Tasmania.
A well-known Sydney habitual female-
drunk caused much joy to those near enough to
catch her sotto voce comments when she recently
honoured a fashionable Sydney sanctuary with her
presence. “ Gladstone 1 ” she muttered, when the
parson brought on the board the “lamented death
of that great Englishman.” “Humph !—Glad-
stone was my godfather. But he wasn’t an
Englishman. He was a Scotchman—and a d d
mean one at that! ”
Cabled that Gladstone’s last words were
“Our Father which art in Heaven.” Statement
subsequently qualified to the effect that his abso-
lutely last words were “ a disconnected remark in
French.” Cartloads of pious sermons and poems
will be preached and written about the first utter-
ance, but never a word will be said about the “dis-
connected remark in French.” No Englishman is
ever pious in French.
Parkin, an oppressive person who visited
Australia years ago with a view of booming Him-
perial Federation, got his reward among the
latest batch of Birthday “honours.” Not much
reward—only a C.B.— but quite enough for
Parkin. A much more famous person of the same
name is chief “gaffer” in the roadside pub scene
in “Silver King.” “Tell us about the ’Atton
Garden murr-der scene, Mr. Parkin.”
St. Albans Wilson helps his brother, S.R.,
into the Segenhoe Estate, Singleton, N.S.W. ;
tenant-farming is to be a feature of the manage-
ment. This is getting back to the brothers’ old
occupation. Nearly 30 years ago they were millers
in the north of South Aus. ; their place—the first
mill at Pt. Pirie—was burned in ’76. After that
they turned to gold-mining, and so gravitated to
the Barrier. W.R. was manager of the long-since
discarded Day Dream; S.R. of the B.H. Prop.
But, in money-making, W.R.’s talents were far
the greater; and, lately, S. H., broken in health,
gave Westralia best (he had the bad luck to plump
for the Murchison), and now turns to a new
question altogether.
Dear Bulletin. — Re that par. of May 7th.
Rev. J. G. Gell was selected by Arnold, of Rugby,
at Sir John Franklin’s request, as head of a Govt,
college which was to have developed into the first
university in Australasia. He came out in ’39 or
’4O, but "he scheme seems to have fallen through.
The idea was taken up by the Church of England,
and Gell became the first warden of the now dor-
mant Christ’s College. He was, I believe, a con-
temporary at Rugby of J. R. Buckland, who in
’46 became the first head-master of the Hutchins’
School in Hobart. In Stanley’s “ Life of Arnold ”
there are several letters to Gell and Franklin about
this college. Arnold took a lively interest in these
provinces, and “actually owned 200 acres in Maori-
land. ”— Y ello wbush.

THE NEW WOMAN.
DOROTHY (aged, 10) : “ Mother , when shall I have
finished my education? ”
MOTHER: “ When you are a B.A.”
D. : “ What is that? ”
M. : “ A Bachelor of Arts.”
D. : “Don’t you make any mistake, mother — I’m
not going to he a bachelor. I’ll marry some
fellow or another! ”
When Field - Marshal Madden, Adminis-
trator of the Vic. Govt, was under examination by
the Law Commission, it was most noticeable that
his manner towards that enquiring body quite
changed after he returned from luncheon. Previ-
ous to mealing he looked like coming into conflict
with president Higgins who, on the previous day,
had ordered a witness to leave the room. After
the adjournment he actually apologised for being
late, was most affable, and asked permission to
modify several of the statements he had made
when his stomach craved for food. Query: What
does his Waxcellency take for lunch? Probably
pork and lemonade.
Sydney Consecrated Press makes Cardinal
Moran say that Gladstone was once on the
point of becoming R.C. “He does not seem ever
to have had the grace again, but he remained a
champion of the authoritative teaching of the
Church.” The only “authoritative teaching ” the
G.O.M. ever championed was that of W. E. Glad-
stone. Further, there has hardly ever been a
Protestant of distinction of whom it has not been
asserted by the R.C. Church that he was at one
time on the point of entering her fold, only, some-
how —he didn't. And the narrowness of the
escape appears often to have resulted —as in
Gladstone s case —in violent antagonism to the
Pope and all his works.
ON THE QUAY.
MR. HAYSEED: “ She looks ready to go.”
MRS. HAYSEED: “ Yes; she’s right down to the
pencil mark!"
Mrs. Cox, of Avoca, Vic., has won fame as
a gold prospectress. She has been at the game for
years, and got “on to it” now and again in a
small way, but has at last cut something good that
promises to be lasting. She has sunk on her latest
reef, opened out, and knocked down a five-ton
crushing that glitters all over. The working
woman is past 50, and lends flavor to her per-
formance by riding five miles to her work every
morning on a thumping big bay.
Longstaff has completed a striking full-
length portrait of Miss Ethel Gray, daughter of
George Gray, the mining-man who is just now “ a
princely patron of the Turf, ” as horse reporters
have it. The 15-year-old damsel stands in her
riding habit as she lives, a pert little billy-cock
hat well back on the head, and rich golden brown
hair hanging loose on either side of the piquant
young face. The pose of the figure is charmingly
natural, and the picture a masterpiece of quiet
effect.

S.A. Advertiser Bonytl o i’s knighthood
is the latest phase of the Adelaide Hospital
quarrel. Bonython earned Premier Kingston’i
gratitude by sticking to him during the stormy
times of two years ago. The bauble was to have
been thrown to Bonython at Jubilee time last
year, but there was a hitch, which it is stated
caused some heated letters between two notabili-
ties. Bonython has been on the Advertiser from
boyhood. He became part-proprietor in 1879, and
is now sole owner of a paper which is said to yield
about <£20,000 a year.
On Queen’s Birthday, Michael O’Donnell
was released from Yatala (S.A.) labor-prison after
being imprisoned for thirty-two years. O’Donnell,
when a young man of 24, had a row with one
Forrester, a publican, at Gilberton (S.A.), and in
the course of a scuffle the latter was stabbed by
O Donnell with a shoemaker’s knife, and died a
few minutes after. Chief Justice Hanson, who
tried the case, and Crown-Prosecutor Wearing
died years ago ; Strangways, who. defended him,
is now in England ; Boothby was then, and still
is, sheriff, and O’Donnell, now a man 56 years
of age, emerges from prison to re-commence life !
T.D. : “ M‘Elhone was not the only man
whose oratorical outbursts destroyed the peace of
residents near Sydney Parliament House. Crick,
John Haynes, and one or two others can always
be heard on the opposite side of Macquarie-street.
The men one can never hear are ‘ Mudgee ’ Jones,
Sydney Law (Balmain), and Bigg (Newtown) — for
the sufficient reason that they never speak. For-
tunate that the Assembly Chamber is at the end
of the Legislative block furthest from the hospital
—otherwise the jawbone of the Parliamentary ass
would have slain its thousands.”
Vetus : General Miles, U.S. Commander-
in-Chief, seems to be aged, like Winfield Scott,
who was Generalissimo when the civil war began.
The old complaint. Yet Von Moltke was the
same age as the century, thus 64 in the Danish
war, 66 in the Austrian, 70 in the French. How-
ever, it is the young, in general, who pant for
war. German Emperor William shunts out even
the men between 40 and 50, without mercy, and
is all for youth. Yet Moltke was not singular
among the German leaders of 1870-1 for being ad-
vanced in years. In fact, not one really young
German general distinguished himself in the war.
The Emperor may make a huge mistake, accord-
ing to the genius of his nation.
Those N.S.W. Leg. Assembly - members
who go in twos, either because they are brothers
or through mere similarity of name, figure
thus, by latest showing, for and against the Com-
monwealth Bill : Two Campbells (Alex, and Archi-
bald). for ; two Browns (H. H. and T.), against;
two Clarkes (F. and H.), one for and one against;
two Piddingtons (W. H. B. and A. B.), one for
and one against; two Phillips (M. T. and S.), one
for and one against; two Thomsons (D. and J.),
one for and one against ; two Fitzpatricks (T. and
J. C.), one for and one against ; three Jones (R.
and T. and Russell), two against and one on the
rail ; two Smiths (S. and T. R.), against. Another
curiosity of the list is that of the members, sons
of ex-Premiers—one, Farnell, goes for the Bill,
and the other, Parkes, against. Again, of those
who carry the Milesian O—one, O’Sullivan, is for
the Bill, and the other, O’Reilly, against; and
again, of the Mac’s—two, M‘Lean and M'Millan,
are counted for the Bill, while the other two,
M‘Court and M‘Gowan, are against.
Colonel Seratchley, writing from Thursday
Island to his son Victor in 1881, told of an emi-
grant-ship bound for Victoria in 1858 with 300
Chinese on board, having gone ashore and been
seized by aborigines on the Vic. coast. Accord-
ing to Seratchley, the 300 were put in a stock-
ade and eaten by the natives —a few each day,
till all were consumed. Peter Seratchley
would not lie. He said the story was told
to him, and implied that he believed it
down to the last of the 300. He added :
“Things are not so bad now in Aus-
tralia. ”
Dicker Hamilton, a well-known
old-time sport, once put up at a hotel
near Flemington course, and ord-red
luncheon. “ A chop and potato will do,”
said Dicker, “and a small bottle of
Bass.” The waiter brought in a dish,
and Dicker sat down. “Take the cover,”
said he. The man obeyed, and Dicker
beheld —the landlord’s bill for past patron-
age of the kind. Dicker stared, then
laughed, and called for the landlord, who
was outside the door. “My dear boysaid
he, “I always knew you to be a smart chap ;
but I never knew you were so good at cooking
accounts —eh ? ” And Dicker got his lunqli—he
generally did—on the nod, once more.

“ Trotter Bethune ” to The Bulletin :
“Medico” is inclined to be facetious in referring to
the Tichborne claimant. I visited England in ’72, and
heard the Claimant deliver an address toa large audienoe
in a marquee erected in a field near Portsmouth. He was
introduced by Guildford Onslow, and his speech, though
somewhat faulty in the aspirates, was in perfectly correct
English. I cannot believe that his language could have
deteriorated so much in 13years as alleged by “Medico.”
Knowing the fate of the real Tichborne, who was mur-
dered on the Haunted Stream, in Gippsland, manj years
before, I went out of curiosity to see if the Claimant was
really Arthur Orton, whom I had known in Gippsland in
1860, and who was an illegitimate half-brother of Tich-
borne. I came away quite convinced that the Claimant
was not Arthur Orton, but Thomas Castro, another ille-
gitimate half-brother of Tichborne and of the Chilian
herdsman whom Tichborne encountered in his travels
across the South American continent, and who followed
hint to Australia. The Claimant confessed two or three
years ago that he was Arthur Orton, but that was done
because he was paid for it and was in great poverty and
distress. He sought afterwards to retract his confes
sion, and, besides, it is well known that the Orton
family have ever recognised him as one of its members.
I am inclined to think, myself, that Cresswell, now in
Parramatta Lunatic Asylum, is really Arthur Orton. I
think I should know him if I saw him, as, although 38
years have passed since we met, all resemblance to the
Orton of long ago can hardly have been obliterated in the
Cresswell of to-day. One thing, however, is certain, and
that is that the lately-deceased Claimant was neither
Tichborne nor Orton. I maintain that he was Thomas
Castro and no other.

Two Australian Pioneers.
FRIENDS AT LAST.
One of the grand women who helped to make
Australia what she is, and of whom her adopted
country is proud, is (Mrs.) M. A. Bentley, now
residing at 163 Alexandra Parade, N. Fitzroy, and
another Australian pioneer, prominenb as con-
tributing benefit to its adopted country, is War-
ner’s Safe Cure. Mrs. Bentley is now an ardent
believer in and friend to this medicine, and she
tells you why:—“For years I suffered with a
serious complaint brought about by exposure in
the early days of the gold-fields. I arrived in
Geelong in 1850, and when married in 1852 my
husband took me to Ballarat. Afterwards I ac-
companied him to nearly all the ‘rushes’ in the
colony; just roughing it with the others. For
many years past I had suffered severely, but could
get no relief until my son-in-law advised me to
try Warner’s Safe Cure. Though now an old
woman I feel greatly improved in health through
using it, and warmly advise it to ladies." *

Aboriginalities.
A certain N.S. Wales “prudent Federa-
tionist,” an attorney noted alike for the solemnity
of his visage and the prodigious number of maps,
&c., with which he illustrates his oratory, while
stumping the country in the anti-Bill interest,
arrived at a little one-horse Southern town. The
whole local population, awe-stricken in the
presence of the ponderous lawyer with the bundle
of papers, followed him silently to the hall
where he proceeded to let loose his eloquence.
Starting at 10 a.m., he was still dolefully damning
the accursed Bill at 1 p.m., while the simple
country people slept or gazed blankly at him, each
one afraid to leave while so great a man was
wagging his jaw. At last a terrified person in a
front row arose, and silently vanished through
THE OPINION OF AUGUSTUS .
“Your country — haw—’ll look a lot better when the
leaves are out, dontcherknow! ”
the door; and a little later a woman put
her head in and screamed that there was “a
man down the well! ” In two minutes the hall
was empty except for the speaker who still talked
on. When the man was hauled out of the well he
asked if “the bloke was torkin’yet,” and when
told that he was, sprinted off to the scrub fol-
lowed by the rest of the audience, and not one
returned until the aforesaid orator had sorrow-
fully rolled up his maps and departed. Next day,
a meeting was held at which it was unanimously
decided that all hands should support the Federal
Bill.
♦ * * *
Man isn’t the only animal that has to
battle for tucker in the back country. Jim had a
horse that always kept condition, if let alone. If
teamsters were feeding their horses anywhere
handy, or a stable and manger were unguarded,
Bob, with teeth and heels, soon got his nose in.
On one station Bob persistently jumped into the
stud-rams’ paddock, until the boss ordered him to
be hobbled ; but he was there again next morning.
They watched him next night and found out how
he did it. The wire fence was old and had become
unstrained. Bob, as soon as it was dark, got his
neck on the top wire, and bore it and the lower
ones down till he could get his shoulder on to it.
Then, throwing all his weight into the job, he
got his rump on and rolled over—literally into
clover, compared with the paddock he had been in.
• * * *
Wrinkler : “The ordinary red gum from
any eucalypt, taken in bits the size of a pea,
pounded-up and swallowed with water, is death
to diarrhoea and dysentery. The little pink-
flowered dysentery-plant is just as good, but must
be plucked when the flower is on. A hand-full in
a pint of boiling water makes four doses. In the
same way, pennyroyal as a diuretic has long been
used by bushmen. Again, your correspondent re
brown bread gives the straight tip. The pollard
best suited for it is the rough-grained used for
rabbit-poisoning—and it also makes a palatable
porridge (not thickening like oatmeal, however)
for Scotchmen. Moreover, the station poisoner
does not lock it up like flour, and he is easier got
at than the cook.”
* * * *
M.Q. ; “ Have had long experience of
dingoes in the Wimmera, Vic., and, unlike
* C.C.,’ have several times succeeded in poisoning
the real article. In my experience they never
feed off a sheep the night they kill it, but return
some nights after; and, if strychnine be inserted
into the sheep with a long instrument, they will
eat it. Also known them to drink poisoned
water in dry seasons. Once knew a dingo to
enter into a friendship with an old bull on a selec-
tion. He followed the bull every day for a week,
and one morning he would not allow a girl to
drive the bull out of a paddock, so I shot him.”

Dear Bulletin.— Rode to Cunnamulla
from Eulo way last St. Pat’s Day—flies fiendish,
heat hellish. Got off to open the 12-mile gate,
and noticed a big, bilious-looking bubble on the
tip of the horse’s nose. Had a look round him,
and found the flies had eaten his off-eye clean out
of his head, and it had slid down on a stream of
tears to the point of his nose and was just about to
drop.— Dadgaboo. [Cakel]

Jaydee . ‘A rich, ‘near’ squatter was
recently standing outside the station-store, when
a miserable-looking traveller staggered up, with
the usual request for something to eat. ‘ Haven’t
had a morsel for two days, sir ! ’ ‘ Don’t care if
you hadn’t had a bite for a month—you’ll get
nothing from me ! ’ Down on his knees went the
swaggy on the instant. ‘O, Lord, kill this man 1
Make nis wife take to drink, an’ give ’is kids the
diptheery ! Burn ’is ’ouse down to the ground,
an’ send a flamin’ drought to kill all his ’ The
squatter darted into the store, threw a 201 b. bag
of flour at the obsecrator, and beat a hasty retreat
to the house. ”
* * * •
Dear Bulletin. —Why not give a rest to
the bloated capitalist and sweater who prays in
patent-leather boots, and turn your wrath upon
the loathsome agricultural humbug ? This is the
time o’ year when able-bodied new-chums, looking
for work up-country, get an engagement from a
wealthy farmer on a sliding scale—first six weeks
at 2s. per week wages; then a rise to ss. ; and
afterwards —in some unstated future—the hand-
some remuneration of 10s. per (week. On these
terms a greenhorn is worked like a nigger, or like
six niggers, for six weeks. The crop being now
“ in,” the farmer sets his latest recruits to per-
form impossible feats of strength in various
departments of bucolic labor, quarrels with them
if they protest, hurls clods at them if they don’t —
in short, gets rid of them under any circum-
stances. A choice specimen of the wealthy,
sweating farmer has an absolutely Australian
reputation. It is quite a common thing for him
to threaten one of his two-bob-a-week servants
with a hayknife or pitchfork on a Sunday morn-
ing, after which he goes to the local chapel and
prays like a good ’un. Sometimes he has been
handsomely wallopped by a “hand” stronger
than his accursed self, but, in a general way, he
practises his barbarous tricks with impunity. To
“ pull ” him up before the local court jay-pays is
mere vanity and vexation of spirit. The brute is
the fatman of the district, and all attempts to
“get justice on ’im ” have failed miserably.—
Follow-the- Plough.

A M.L. Bulletin correspondent attributes
the recent Maori trouble entirely to the dog-tax.
“ The number of the native tykes,” lie says, “ is
ever increasing, and their united scratch'ability
would make a gold-seeker’s fortune in Klondyke.
Visited a pah once in the Arohenua district. In
our party was a young and well-favored lady who
took a lively interest in the dear dogs, of which
there was a dreadful horde basking in the sun ;
and when she drew out a biscuit the mongrels re-
oiprocated so energetically and unanimously that,
in a few seconds, they had left her almost in the
‘ altogether. ’ (That’s how I know she was well-
favored). She had to go home in a borrowed
Maori mat.”

A Maoriland objector (at the assessment
court): “ I have one cow for our own use, one old
horse besides myself, and one pig and about 14
apple-trees, all my own produoe. The Govt, have
deprived many hundreds of people of a bare sub-
sistence by damaging the colony with the millions
of money of immensely wealthy people in Britain
to the serious injury of widows and orphans.”
* * * *
Ponte : “ Recently saw an old toper drink
a glass of brandy, whisky, rum, gin and beer in
quiok succession. Recalled the old days when
the sons of neighboring squatters used to take
possession of the town. On one occasion the fun
had been fast for two or three days, and every-
thing in the way of larks that ingenuity could
devise had been played. About a dozen of the
boys were in the pub. parlor, and all the liquor
had been sampled, when one of them had a happy
thought and ordered drinks all round —no two
drinks to be alike. When they were brought in
he emptied the lot into a jug and refilled the
glasses with the mixture, which was voted
glorious. Up there any drink concocted of more
than two ingredients is a ‘kimo.’ ” [Really, human
ingenuity is a stereotyped affair. Hugsmans, the
Drench realistio-mystio novelist, makes the blase
hero of 1 ‘ En Route a modern of the moderns,
decadent of the decadents —invent with pride a
kind of drink-organ. He pressed one key, and
absinthe flowed into a goblet standing on top of
the organ ; another—and out came old cognac;
another—vermouth ; and so on. When the deca-
dent felt inspired he would play a symphony on
his organ, and a score of liqueurs mingled in one
ecstatic draught. But the common bush person
with his jug gets there just the same.— Ed. B.]
* * * *
K.K. : “In considering the town wards
gravitation of the country-worker, the gregarious
instinct of mankind is too much overlooked.
Poverty in the country is imprisonment, with
solitary.confinement; in the city, it is imprison-
ment with tho alleviation of company, sympathy,
and exchange of ideas. Nature is all very well,
but in Austral backblocks she is often too austere
to be a pleasing mate, hence the desire to get into
town, and exchange ideas about the best place for
a threepenny feed or the best horse to put a bob
on.”
* • « *
Emu : “Some Westralian blacks have a
tradition that the bottom star in the Southern
Gross forms an emu’s head, the body and legs
being made by smaller stars (easily traced any
moonlight night). The two pointers are blacks
trying to spear said emu ; but as they are not suc-
cessful, emus continue to walk round. My nigger
informants emphatically believed that had the
emu been speared, the bird’s earthly existence
would have ended.”
* * * *
Wallaroo : “ The ‘ struggling cocky ’
isn’t in the same street with the average ‘ big ’
farmer for meanness. Know several of the latter
who prefer to struggle along with a couple of half-
wits rather than pay reasonable wages to good
men. The ‘ balmy ’ one is satisfied with poor
food, poor clothes, poor bacon—and not too much
of ’em. In busy seasons swaggies are taken on at
ss. or 6s. per week and keep, but generally quit at
end of the first week. Up at 4, and work till Bor
9; cornhusking till 12. Tucker, bad salt-junk
and badly-baked bread, with an occasional dish of
vegetables and boiled rice. ”
A PLEASANT DREAM.
Wer. : “Be Barramundi At Eurombar
station, two miles off the Dawson River, is a big
lagoon teeming with these fish, the largest of the
kind known. Though very shy they swim so near
the surface when undisturbed that their backs are
often out of water. In calm weather they bite
fairly well, but the angler must keep still and out
of sight. I was told that a 3ft fish was caught in
this lagoon, and was shown the scales, which
measured over an inch in diameter.”
* * * *
Tho latest N.Q. invention is a divided
billy-can. You can carry the pup in one part and
tea or soup in the other, the heat of the pup keep-
ing the victuals warm, thus saving time and
matches.
The sort of thing which
makes the selector love the
squatter so. The selector has a
creek for one frontage; on the
other side of it is the squatter.
A boundary fence in the bed of
the creek would be useless; it
would certainly be washed away
in the first flood. Consequently,
permission is got by the selector
to put up a “ give and take”
fence—now on one side of the
winding creek, now on the other.
On the selector claiming half-
value, the squatter pleads that,
as the fence does not “mark a
common boundary line,” the ac-
tion does not lie, and on that lie
succeeds.
♦ * *
Some of the joys of sheep-
farming. Writer drove a mob
of ewes, heavy in lamb, from
drafting-yards to a paddock two
miles distant. En route several
dropped, paralysed in their hind-
quarters through crushing. Next
day found half of them dead,
the others with their eyes pecked
out. Lifted up one that had
both eyes out and a hole pecked
in its back, and it walked 100
yds. before it lay down again.
Another had its side torn away
by a dingo. Have seen crows
clinging to the necks of moving
sheep and pecking at their eyes,
and have often noticed them
following the dingo on the war-
path. They’re up to his game,
and know well enough they’ll
come in for the pickings.

E.S.S. : “Droving down
the Maranoa during the heat-
wave of ’95, we saw many scores
of blacks in the broiling sun
with no covering on their heads
or bodies. Anyone whose skull
is properly developed may im-
munise himself to such state by
beginning with hair long enough
to cover his collar, and “shear-
ing ” gradually. Anything’s easy
when you’re used to it—the
trouble is getting used to it.
Apropos , I know two N.S. Wales fencers who
couldn’t work an hour with their shirts on.
Have seen them on the hottest summer days
naked to the belt, and slogging away with mauls
or axes. Their skin was never burnt or blistered,
but each of their chests was covered with a thiok
growth of hair.”

’97 they were -£1,250,000, so he only took off
£BlO,OOO. But Reid’s utter inability to tell the

Artist Longstaff—
as isn’t generally known
—designed the striking
whisky - advt. poster
lately published in Mel-
bourne. Three scarlet-
coated hunting john-
nies sitting round a
table, about to be served
with their liquor by an
aesthetic barmaid in a
mauvey-drabgown. The
central figure appears
to lean back into space,
and altogether the pic-
ture is so strong that
the artist would have
put his name to it if he,
and not the lithograph-
er, had carried it right
through to a conclu-
sion. Longstaff, by the
way, thinks of exhibit-
ing a roomful of his
pictures shortly, when
his “Bush Fire” sub-
ject is completed. This
is already an assured
sensation, if Melbourne
can possibly be moved
by any work of art
nowadays. The “ Bush
Fire” effect, as Long-
staff studied it in
Gippsland a while ago,
is a great, glowing real-
ity on his big canvas.
A smell of smoke and
burning wood comes
from the back-ground,
and to stand in front
of the picture is to feel
warm. It heats the
studio. Also it reminds
one of a certain Argus,
or Age, paragraph sent
along by a Gippsland
correspondent at the
time when this particu-
lar bush fire was doing
its worst. The par.,
after enlarging upon
the terrible destruction
to property in the dis-
trict, stated that Mr.
John Longstaff had
come down from Mel-
bourne to view the
conflagration, and was
“ greatly pleased with
what he had seen.”
One of the standing anti-Billious pafa-
graphs which appear every day decked, with loud
capitals in Sydney Telegraph calls upon the
electors to cast the Federation Bill down into the
deep, damp green sea of oblivion lest its accept-
ance should end in the destruction of Foreign-
trade. It says:—
That freedom from Customs dues has been a boon to
our farmers, pastoralists, miners, to all classes, is certain.
To Sydney it has brought increased trade, and made her
more and' more the intercolonial centre. Compare Syd-
ney with Melbourne since 1893 ; the difference has been
extraordinary.
But the Telegraph wisely stops there and doesn’t
compare them. Here are some figures: Total
trade (import and export) of N.S.W. in 1893,
.£41,028,258; in 1897, £45,347,341; increase,
l 319,083. Victoria, total trade in 1893,
£26.592,365; in 1897, £32,194,152; increase,
£5,601,787. The results are extraordinary, right
enough, but not at all the kind of extraordinary
which the Telegraph implies.
The great bulk of the anti-Billite logic in
N.S.W. is devoted to showing what dreadful
things might happen if the other four provinces
formed a league against “our” province, which
“would then be entirely at their mercy.” The
other provinces have got. the same disease in a
milder form, sundry Victorian anti-Billites in
particular being much troubled about what might
happen if N.S.W. conspired with S.A., Tas-
mania and Westralia against “us.” Yet, for
some 50 years Sydney and Melbourne have been
wholly at the ‘mercy of the country districts,
which might have conspired together at any time
to make them pay all the taxes, and to compel the
expenditure of all the loan money in the country.
And nobody has even realised that the awful
danger existed. Why doesn’t some one get this
horror attended to ? The only way to remove the
danger, either in the Federal or the State Parlia-
ment, or the municipal council, or elsewhere, is to
give every place more representation than all the
other places put together. If this isn’t done every
place is tottering on the verge of a precipice all
the time.
THE MALCONTENT.
O, I don’t care a bloomin’ pin
For Purves’s purryration :
You don’t catch me a-joinin’ in
No song of Federation.
The Guv'ment dances round the Bill,
Yet I distrust their jig-gin’s—
They may express the People’s Will,
But 1 shall cheer for ’Iggins.
Fin sure he loves the workin’-class—
He says so, most emfatie,
And swears that if the Bill can pass
We shan’t be demmycratic.
Toffs toast the “ Cause ” in dry shampine,
I’ve read about their swiggins ;
But their sort ain’t in line with mine,
So I shall cheer for ’Iggins.
He preaches Freedom, ’ot and strong—
O, he’s a clean pertater :
He’s ’Ancock’s pal, and can’t go wrong ;
But Trenwith is a traiter—
Why, if the Party meets him now
They gives him orful wiggin’s,
And when he spouts we drown his row
With ’earty cheers for ’Iggins.
The “ Cause ” that’s got a lot of friends
Arouses my suspishun ;
All blokes wot work for “ noble ends”
Support the Opposishun.
I'iie young men and the old ’uns too,
Wot grafted on the diggin’s
Conspire to teach me wot to do—
That’s why I cheer for ’Iggins.
My blessed bosom shan’t be stirred
By “ gags ” about “ Our Nation,”
And if they launch, on June the 3rd,
The ‘‘Good ship, Federation,”
And all the joyful jossers go
A-swarmin’ up the riggin’s—
Well, I shall merely slink below
To whisper cheers for ’Iggins.

THE TWO GEORGES-WHICH IS TO DIE?
The county cannot stand them both. Either the Federal George or the anti-Federal George must go. This is what must happen after the Federal election.


There is in London a person named Fel-
bermann who runs a greasy weekly rag called
Life. From a recent number of Life, The Bul-
letin clips this awful paragraph :
His Majesty the Emperor-King- of Austria-Hungary,
has been graciously pleased to confer upon Mr. Louis
Felberrnann, the editor and proprietor of this journal,
the Knight Cross of the Order Francis-Josef, in recogni-
tion of the services rendered to Hungary. Soon after the
Imperial decree appeared in the Official Hungarian
Gazette, His Majesty granted an audience to Mr. Louisi
Felberrnann at the Hofburg Vienna, and expressed the
great pleasure that it afforded him to be able to reward
the services rendered by MY. Louis Felberrnann. His
Majesty conversed for a considerable time with Mr. Louis
Felberrnann, and enquired about the position of the
Austro-Hungarian colony in England. Numerous tele-
grams and letters of congratulation reached Mr. Louis
Felberrnann from all parts of England and Hungary,
amongst which should be specially mentioned those of
His Excellency Count Deytn, the Austro-Hungarian
Ambassador to the Court of St. James’s, and several
Cabinet Ministers in Hungary. Mr. Louis Felberrnann
feels proud of the honor of having received these friendly
congratulations, and takes this opportunity to express
his most grateful thanks to these illustrious personages,
and to all his friends who so kindly expressed their good
wishes on this occasion.
Those interested in the wool industry will
be pleased to note that the earlier prognostica-
tions of the success of the Co-Operative Wool and
Produce Co. Ltd. have been fully verified. The
very excellent total of over 13,000 bales of wool
sold shows to what a large extent the co. has
secured the confidence of pastoralists. As the
utmost care and efficiency is placed at the service
of clients, the co.’s rise in public favor should be a
rapid and continued one.*

NEW SYSTEM of HEALING.
The POLYPATHIC INSTITUTES
AND
THEIR REMARKABLE WORK.
WITH ABSOLUTE CERTAINTY OF LOCATING DISEASE, THEY CURE BEYOND REASONABLE
DOUBT IN ALL CASES.
SYDNEY & MELBOURNE.
29 YORK STREET, SYDNEY.
84 COLLINS STREET, MELBOURNE.
ELEVEN YEARS’ WORK
OF THE
Combined Institutions under the Polypathic System.
WHAT OUR SYSTEM HAS ACHIEVED IN ELEVEN YEARS.
The following is a brief synopsis of cases which have been successfully treated by us during the past ELEVEN
YEARS, commencing December 1, 1887, and ending December 1, 1897. This does'not include applicants for treat-
ment who were pronounced by us INCURABLE, or whose cases were taken onlv as patients requested with a view
to their partial relief, but is an accurate RECORD transcribed from our books of CURES ACTUALLY PERFORMED,
guaranteed absolutely and implicitly correct in every particular :
THE DIAGNOSIS OF DISEASE
WITHOUT LOOKING AT THE TONGUE, FEELING THE PULSE, OR ANY
PREVIOUS KNOWLEDGE OF THE CASE.
Here bqgins the very first starting-point of ’vantage with the Polypathic school of practice, for its chief ex-
ponents are men who, from a natural and overpowering sympathy with all forms of disease and suffering, are able to
determine by quickened sense surely, and with determination, the exact cause of all that makes life a burden to
thousands all over the land. The highways of medical practice are strewn with the wrecks of wrong diagnoses, with
the going out of innumerable lives, because of improper location of disease, far more than from the use of remedies
of themselves hurtful. Here Polypathy plants its foot firmly down, and declares that not alone the instinctive, keen
sense of its consulting chief should be taken as a finality, but that what he determines is the real trouble, with all
the causes that led him to the clear conclusion, shall be placed before the full faculty of the Institute, and his
judgment meet their approval.
The grand and. distinguishing first step, then, in this new system is the accurate and absolute determining of
that which is and is not at war with the natural current of your life, and, when that is done, to find the means of
quick and sure removal.
The environment of the several schools has been from the very earliest years down to a recent period the one
insurmountable bar to the strong and sure battle with an enemy that should never have been considered unconquer-
able, because in the light of the wisdom of these later days nearly all forms of disease, if cared for in proper time,
will yield to the scientific and natural treatment of the most advanced scholars, who use with a strong hand all the
agencies that are now happily within our reach.
The Polypaths go into the vast fields of medical properties, and find in each that which in certain conditions
never fails, and they accept and adopt in accordance with this clear principle, and apply with that confidence that
long experience has taught them to be well-nigh infallible. Bound down by no special school of simple theory,
having no special system to maintain, they enter the realm of Polypathy and apply to your special case that which
science and intuitive knowledge have taught with unerring skiil to be specially designed for you. They enter the
field of surgery and rob it of much of its terrors by proclaiming that many cases heretofore piaced under the knife
of the operator now yield to the later discoveries, and are eradicated from the system without shock or danger,
reducing the necessity of using the knife by more than one-half from what was absolutely essential in even recent
years by the old system.
Polypathy claims the freedom of accepting the good and rejecting the bad in all systems ; it has formulated
special treatment for itself in all chronic cases; it prepares its own medicines, and applies them in the light of
nineteenth century progress.

TALES OF THE CONVICT SYSTEM, by
Price Warung; price, 1/-; posted, 1/3.
The fear that the Labor-party will lose ground
in the State Parliament is at the root of a good
deal of the noisiest opposition to the Common-
wealth Bill in N.S. W. But there is no cer-
tainty that it will do anything of the sort. On
the contrary, no party ought, in one respect, to
gain more. The most forcible objection of the
thorough-going Democrat to the Labor-party is
that it is avowedly prepared to play ducks-
and-drakea with the fiscal policy if its pro-
gramme of social reform is thereby advanced,
and as that disturbing element can’t be good
for the country it is accordingly distrusted and
weakened. Further, no matter what it may
profess, it cannot “ sink the fiscal issue ” ; it is
all the time aiding one policy or the other, and
mat ters are not improved even if it is true that
the party does not care which it aids. But, seeing
that three-fourths of its supporters have pre-
ferences one way or the other, the fact that it
sinks Protection for a long time and sustains
Freetrade is pretty certain to arouse suspicion
among those with the Protectionist preferences,
who not unnaturally think that it isn’t the
fiscal issue that the party sinks but only one
particular end of it. And if it sank Freetrade
for a long time there would, no doubt, be the
same suspicion and weakening among those
with Freetrade preferences. So long, then, as
the State Parliament has to settle tariff business
the Labor-party, being willing to go with either
crowd, cannot be more than a third party ; and
for the other reasons it can’t be more than a
constitutionally weak third party either. To
that extent any scheme to remove the fiscal
question out of State politics would be right
into the hands of this party.
♦
The ways of Justice are, indeed, a curious
study. In Queensland, the other day, the
secretary of a local building society, convicted
of having embezzled <£l3oo, received from the
officiating Rhadamanthus a sentence of three
years penal servitude. Immediately after-
wards a man, found guilty of having pur-
loined a watch, went up for four years. True,
the latter had previous convictions to his score,
but it iB extremely doubtful if all his steals put
together came to one-quarter the amount,
or his list of steals was one-quarter as long
as that of an individual who had been de-
liberately embezzling for years. Also, there is
not the slightest comparison as to consequences
to be made between the turpitude of an indi-
vidual whose defalcations bring about (as in
this case) the insolvency of a society, and the
ruin of dozens of small investors—and the
criminality of a thief who only snatches a purse
now and again, or collars a time-piece on occa-
sion. How is that, in Australia, embezzlement
should be regarded as so much less heinous a sin
than petty larceny? . . . But Bundaberg
sentences were always rather ludicrous. Some
time ago, the keeper of a swell brothel in that
odorous sugar-town received a sentence of two
years—and was immediately liberated under
the First Offenders’ Act !
Stipendiary Paytkn, of Newcastle (N.S. W.),
in sending one YVkstcott to gaol for non-com-
pliance with a court-order for the support of
his wife in a lunatic-asylum, puts in operation
a theoretically-useful, but, as administered, an
egregiously indiscriminating law. Westcott’s
plea of being out of work and having two young
children to maintain, availed nothing. He was
bundled off to a cell till the order, amounting
to £3 155., should be liquidated. It is a fact
that a man who can, and won’t, should be made
to support his wife, sane or insane ; and, if sent
to gaol, he should be made work there, and so
contribute to her support. But there are other
relations which the law should cover, as well
as those of husband and wife, and other insti-
tutions as well as insane-asylums. It is a
standing scandal that well-to do sons and
daughters may leave their worn-out fathers in
benevolent-homes in N.S.W. to be wholly pro-
vided for by the general taxpayer; brothers
may so leave brothers ; sisters, sisters. The
wife of a deceased N.S.W. Supreme Court
Judge energetically labored to stem the grow-
ing tide of such abuses a few years ago, but
failed to raise the necessary public interest.
The lady proposed to gazette the names of the
well-to-do relatives of the State-fed and lodged,
but not even this side revenge upon blood skin-
flints was allowed. Westcott and his wife are
the mere fringe of a family relationship, the
whole of which should be under one definite
and equitably-administered law.

POLITICAL

“ Dick ” O’Connor, on the cost of Federa-
tion : “Federation, gentlemen, will cost you 3s.
6d. per head, or Is. more than it costs to register
your dog.”
Tarrangower Times (Vic.) is responsible
for the suggestion that a number of patriotic citi-
zens should step up and guarantee .£lOOO, for dis-
tribution amongst the charities of the constitu-
ency that comes nearest polling its total votes on
the Federal polling-day. Bribery and corruption !
Among all the politicians who denounce
the Federal Constitution on the ground that the
remedy for deadlocks between the two Houses is
insufficient, there isn’t one, so far as this paper
knows, who ever endeavoured to provide his own
province with any remedy for deadlocks, inefficient
or otherwise.
L. F. Heydon, about the bitterest and
most one-eyed anti-Federalist of the N.S.W.
crowd, starts most of his anti-Billious orations by
stating that he is perfectly unbiased. For that
matter, it is probable that the Spanish Inquisition
was convinced that it was quite unbiased as re-
gards the burnt Protestant.
In the last three years, from the Riverina
alone, 384,000 bales of N.S.W. wool have gone to
Melbourne, and an immense quantity more has
gone from Western N.S.W. The allegation that
under Federation the Riverina trade will go to
Melbourne has just one large hole in it—the
Riverina trade has gone there already.
S.A. politicians wonder what the demo-
cratic crowd would do if anything went wrong
with Kingston, the only lawyer on the Liberal
side in the Assembly. Glynn, once with the
Liberals, is now an Oppositionist, and Gordon is
in the Council. S.A. Legislature only harbors six
lawyers in all, and four of them are Tories.
? Gov. Phillip wrote a memo, when leaving
Australia ordering that no part of Sydney should
ever be sold. He recommended that it should be
reserved as the property of the Government for
ever. Had Phillip’s direction been followed,
Sydney’s claim to be the Federal capital might now
more readily be accepted by the other provinces.

The Bulletin is pleased to offer some informa-
tion. Tasmania is the country that is in the con-
dition which Coghlan never heard about-if
Coghlan’s own statistics for 1896 are reliable.
Every day of late
Sydney D.T. has an-
nounced in large let-
ters that “Mr. Reid,
at the Town Hall,
Sydney, put this
straight to the peo-
ple : the capital would
be certain to be in Vic-
toria.” This large, con-
fident assertion strikes
the trusting elector re-
gularly in the eye, and
if he is struck with it
often enough he will
probably believe it. If
Reid had said such a
thing it wouldn’t have
mattered much. But,
as a mere ordinary
fact, what he did say
was, that if he were
Turner which he
isn’t, thank Heaven !
—he would offer to
sell to the Common-
wealth as a Federal
capital that part of
Melbourne where Par-
liament House and the
Treasury Build i n gs
are. And the real
statement is just as
much like the impu-
