ox is like an Ibsen
drama.
The present fore-
cast in S.A. is that) che
Convention Bill will have a 2 to 1 majority,
despite the opposition of Advertiser, Single-
taxers and Labor-party.
Sydney Telegraph : “ Under the Bill,
Newcastle can never hope to add a ton of coal to
her exports to Victoria.” Well, Victoria is already
Newcastle’s biggest customer. If N.S.W. isn’t
to federate unless Victoria, which already buys
twice as much Cornstalk coal as any other cus-
tomer, will promise to buy still more coal, it is a
pretty large order. Yet, supposing Victoria
asked, as a condition of the Federal union, that
N.S.W. should buy some of her coal, the atmos-
phere of the Daily Telegraph would be blue
with blasphemy.
The capture of the Mataura (M.L.) seat
by the Seddon Govt.’s supporter, M'Nab, who
scored a majority of 387, more than neutralised
Duthie’s victory at Wellington. Wellington was
always Tory, and the hard-faced Duthie is probably
the most personally popular rich man in the city,
and, when he won on these two counts, there was a
loud chorus that Digger Dick’s day was over.
But at Wellington the Tories merely retained a
seat they had owned all along. At Mataura the
Liberals captured one from the enemy, so the
honors are with Seddon.
In his Goulburn speech' Premier Reid
denounced the Federation Bill because of the
three-fifths majority needed to decide any question
at a joint sitting of the two Federal Houses, the
power of the senate to reject money bills, and the
equal representation of the provinces in the Senate.
Reid voted for all these things at the Convention.
Which of the two Reids is the public to believe ?
N.S.W. Land Minister Carruthers states
that the West Bogan scrub lands, which were
made habitable by the expenditure of money bor-
rowed at 3 per cent., have been let at rentals
which will produce 8 per cent, on the outlay. But
nothing is said about applying the alleged 5 per
cent, profit towards paying off the debt. That, of
course, goes down to Posterity in the good old
way.
Certain N.S.W. anti-Billites now state
m ysteri ously that large sums of Yio. money are bein g
sent to N.S.W. to promote the acceptance of the
Federal Bill. They say darkly that they know
the persons who are sending this coin, but won’t
mention names till the time is ripe. The time
will possibly be ripe on the morning of polling-
day, when it is too late for the alleged conspirators
to explode the tale.
The Yanks are almost as particular about
their army as the average Australian province is
about its police. Even at the present time, with
recruiting-offices open in every city, no applicants
are accepted who are not single, under 30 and
over 21, not less than sft. 6in. high, and able to
produce testimonials to character. Consequently
only about 10 per cent, of the applicants are
accepted. What an effective device is war for
skimming the cream of a community’s manhood !
Rogers, the poet, was once in com pany
when a newly-fledged duke was introduced and
listened impatiently to the admiring comments
upon the young man’s personal appearance till at
last the “ dear duke ” condescended to smile.
“Ha!” said Rogers, “thank God he has bad
teeth ! ” Which recalls Higgins’ contention that
although the Federation Bill is good in the great
majority of points, it ought to be rejected because
of a few small shortcomings. He snarls and thanks
God venomously that “it has bad teeth.” Rogers
was old and crabbed and dyspeptic. Has Higgins
any such apology ?
Premier Turner admitted last week (what
a good many Victorians have known for a long
time) that it really doesn’t matter much if the
Federal Constitution prevents Victoria carrying
N.S.W. wool at specially low rates, because it is
more than questionable if the business pays. For
the mere glory of doing a big trade at Melbourne
the Vic. railways now carry N.S.W. wool, in some
cases, at one-third the regular rates. Turner
reckons that the glory is all his province gets out
of it, and that Melbourne may be better off when
it only gets the trade which goes its way without
special inducement.
The gorgeous logic of Lyne, M.L.A. :
As time went on, the present unequal conditions of the
States would become intensified. In 40 years he believed
this colony would have eight millions of people, and Tas-
mania 450,000. So the position would be this : That Tas-
mania with 450,000 people would dominate eight millions.
This colony could not afford to put itself into such an un-
alterable situation. (Applause.)
N.B.—N.S.W. is to have 31 members to Tas-
mania’s 11. Will the long member from the
Hume explain how 11 people dominate 31 ? And
why is Tasmania to increase only three-fold in
40 years while N.S.W. increases six-fold, seeing
that Tasmania is actually increasin'? by a much
bigger percentage annually than N.S.W. ?
A MINING ITEM .
“ Crosscutting with Government aid."
L. F. Heydon, stumping the N.S.W.
northern districts with his unreliable tale of woe,
tells the people constantly that N.S.W. has
.£2,000,000 a year land revenue, against Victoria’s
£400,000 and S.A.’s £200,000, and therefore
N.S.W. shouldn’t federate. But as each province
keeps its own land revenue, and the Federal
Government has absolutely nothing to do with it,
the question of which one has the biggest land
revenue is just as irrelevant as the question which
has the biggest feet, or the largest rock-cod, or the
Toriest attorney. He also advances as an Anti-
Bill reason that Sydney is growing faster than
Melbourne, which is just as relevant as it would
be if Turner pointed out that Melbourne has more
boot-factories, and colder winds, and a bigger
corporation ash-tip.
Slattery’s sudden retirement from Fitzroy
(Sydney) eleotion leaves the comedy and rotten-
cabbage element in the contest to centre round
Caulfield and John Norton. As a preliminary
omen, Caulfield has, after much agony and
perspiration, got John fined £l5O, for contempt of
court. Slattery is saving himself up for the elec-
tion in King Division, where Reid will now scoff
at him as “ the gentleman who ran away from
Woolloomooloo.” Norton, the only brainy man
nominated, will no doubt be elected, unless war-
rior Chapman gets all the suffrages of the grateful
admirers of his bacon.
ON THE BENCHES IN
THE PARE .
The chief efforts of the
S.A. session—which opens
23rd June will be concentrated on a Pas-
n an d a Civil Service Reform Bill
Inore than half of the policy foreshadowed
by,Kingston when Parliament met in June, ’96,
will have to stand over for the third year in suc-
cession.
What a commanding influence old Jack
Robertson must have had upon his intimates!
When you take the prominent men of the present
JN.b. W. anti-Bill crowd, you will find that most of
them at one time or another have fallen under the
sway of the old warrior who thought it was like
the darned cheek of the other Australias to set up
governments of their own.
In a moment of unusual vigor, Sydney
Telegraph recently described the Federalists as
blinded Billites.” Which is strongly sug’gestive
of Lawson’s “ Captain of the Push ” :
A dozen ghouls came sloping round the corners of the
blocks.
And each one spat and halted, and the one that came
behind
Spitting fiercely on the pavement called on God to strike
him blind.
Holy Joe Cook, N.S.W. Postmaster-
General, has told the Local Option League that
“ a Parliament will never be formed in N.S.W.
which will permit Sunday-trading.” And yet
there is such a Parliament now, and Sunday-
trading is rampant, and Sunday Brunker has
ceased to crusade against the Sabbath-desecrating
banana, and Joe Cook makes his sinful crust as a
member of a Ministry which is too worldly and
too worldly-wise to enforce the Sunday observance
law.
In his brief reign as Special Commissioner
of Papua, Colonel Scratch ley impressed upon the
British Government the paramount necessity of
retaining in Imperial hands the financial control
of the island. “If,” he wrote, “the expense of
managing New Guinea be borne by the colonies,
I foresee that a deadlock must eventually arise
between the Imperial officer and the Australian
Governments.” The present land-grab business
is likely to prove Scratchley a true prophet, but
if it doesn’t the deadlock is nevertheless sure to
come owing to the financial control being as it is.
Jervois and others, seeing the same danger, urged
that the British Protectorate should be at once
converted into a Crown colony.
Commenting upon the bad blood stirred up
in N.S. Wales by the Constitution Bill, and the
difficulty of hereafter getting together a Cabinet
of men who would so forgive and forget as to pull
well conjointly in the public interest, an ex-Minis-
ter observed : “ It’s not friendship for one another
that makes a good Cabinet. I was member of a
Cabinet every member of which blackguarded the
other behind his back and snarled at him to his
face, and we did as good public work as any Cabi-
net on record. I could make a first-rate Cabinet
at present out of the bitterest of the Convention
enemies. They’d curse one another, no doubt,
going in and out of Cabinet-meeting, but that is
rather better than the reverse for the public.”
The People, published by the Australia®
Socialist League, iB against the Federal Bill
because it provides for an indissoluble union
under the Crown.” The People actually says
that this destroys all possibility of a Republic."
But if these provinces don’t accept the Bill, the
only difference is that they will be indissolubly
disumted under the Crown. The connection with
the blessed old Crown is just as indissoluble, and
the Republic just as impossible (on paper) one wav
as the other.
Edmund Barton’s activity in the cause of
Australia is astonishing when one considers his
established reputation for “that tired feeling.”
The protracted M'Sharry arbitration case, with
its month-long addresses by counsel, is a severe
enough trial of endurance, but Barton adds to
this a Federation speech almost every night, and
the dictation of reams of newspaper and other
correspondence on his pet theme. He is also
known to have helped the movement right loyally
from a monetary point of view.
At last general election the Yic. Tory
party was exceedingly anxious to oust Longmore,
and the plural
voters went forth
to Box Hill, Oak-
leigh and the
other suburban
portions of his
constituency to
plump for the
nonentity, Keys.
By so doing, how-
ever, they were
prevented
from going to
Heidelberg in
the interests
of Robert
Harper, and
the result was
the defeat of
that respect-
able grocer by
a short head.
There are a
large number
o f directors,
bank officials,
trustees, and
building soci-
ety men who
have votes in
every electo-
rate around Melbourne, and they can turn an

generally regarded as safe, and it was known that
a determined effort would just about “ do it ” for
Keys, anyhow.
Premier Reid's glaring cram that N.S.
Wales, under the Federation scheme, because it
may have a revenue from customs alone of £2 per
head per annum, will therefore be the most
heavily customs-taxed country in the world, is
flying, round. Sydney Telegraph keeps this sin-
ful misrepresentation standing in big type, and
L. F. Heydon spreads it as he goes. If these
people don’t know that all the seven provinces
averaged a heavier customs taxation than that till
’92, and that most of them raise more than £2 a
head now without any trouble, then their ignor-
ance is aboriginal in its intensity. But probably
they do know it.
E. M. Clark, M.L.A. (N.S.W.)
The other colonies had separated from this colony some
time ago, and had tried to fashion their own careers.
But now, like the prodigal son, they were trying to come
back. . . . The Federal capital would most certainly be
in Melbourne. That was all practically settled, and he
was informed on credible authority that a large amount
of British capital had been invested in Melbourne on that
understanding.
Isn’t there one anti-Billious person among the
crowd who is manly enough to tell the plain, un-
varnished truth—which is, that every invitation to
federate since 1890 has come from N.S.W., and it
is the prodigal mother who has been shrieking to
her sons to come back ? And, seeing that the
capital is to be entirely taken from the province
which now owns it, and that Victoria certainly
can’t ruin herself by ceding her one big city to the
Federation, will the fearsome Clark say which
lunatic asylum his “ authority ” is in ?
Conybeer and M‘Gregor, M’s.L.A., are
furiously stumping S.A. in the anti-Billious inte-
rest, because —(1) The present Federal scheme will
“ drag S.A. down to the level of N.S.W. and Vic-
toria ”; (2) S.A. is asked to hand over its postal
surplus to help the bankrupt Postal Departments
of these two States ; (3) S.A. has been deprived of
the control of its own river, the Lower Murray ;
(4) the Barrier trade isn’t “secured ” to S.A. ; (5)
S. A., Tasmania, and Westralia can be voted down
by N.S.W. and Victoria (the N.S.W. anti-Billious
are against the Bill because they say it is the other
way about); (6) because the whole thing is a dark
conspiracy of the other provinces to rob S.A. in
every possible way. The anti-Billious is much the
same everywhere, and he has always the same
fixed idea—-that there is no knowing what horrible
thing the other provinces may not do to his pro-
vince unless his province is protected by having
more voting power than all the others combined.
“THE TIRED N.S.W. VOTER.”
My friend, have you heard of the town of Sydnee,
On the banks of the river Slow,
Where blooms the Waitawhile flower fair ;
Where the Sometimeorother scents the air
And the soft Goeasys grow ?
It lies in the valley of Whatstheuse
In the province of Letterslide ;
Thattiredfeeling is native there,
It’s the home of the listless Idon’tcare,
Where the Putitoffs abide.

upon to cast their vote for or against the Bill empowering
the FEDERATION of the AUSTRALIAN COLONIES.
We have already federated air, rubber, fabric and steel
into one complete and perfect whole, and the result has been
that
Dunlo,p Tyres
are now ridden and acknowledged to be the best by millions
of cyclists, who find them simple to handle, perfect for
road riding, and the speediest on the track.
The Dunlop pneumatic Tyre Co* [td.,
and at Melbourne, Adelaide,
Perth, and Christchurch, N.Z.

Johnnie Cornstalk!
You are idle, Johnnie Cornstalk, and you don’t do what
you ought,
And you think too much of cricket and of divers kinds
of sport, *
And to serious things like politics you give too little
thought,
But you’re going to show a different day is dawning 1
There’s no call to “ waken,” Johnnie, no occasion to
“ arise,”
You’re the kind of chap that’s always good at anything
he tries,
And you’re just a-goin’ to look around and open both
your eyes,
And be fairly on the job o’ Friday morning !
You’re a lazy kind o’ covey , Johnnie Cornstalk,
But you’re capable of something like a thrill,
And you’ll show the beggars whether
You can pull yourself together,
When you go for Toby Barton and the Bill!
As you will l
You’re agoin’ to bet your blankets on the Bill!
You are careless, Johnnie Cornstalk ; very careless, too,
my lad,
And in anything but sporting you can easily be had,
But you’re not too blessed stupid, not too feather-brained
begad,
And the knaves who think you are will get a warning !
You are going to take your brothers o’er the border by
the hand,
You’ll make something of Australia but a horse-and-
ericket land,
And for those who still may doubt it, you will make ’em
understand,
When you start out on the warpath Friday morning!
You’re an aggravating fellow, Johnnie Cornstalk,
And in politics too fond of standing still,
But they’ll find you not so snidy
When on Federation Friday
You are straight for Toby Barton and the, Bill!
As vou WILL,
You will bet yer blessed, bluchers on the Bdl !
W. T. GOODGE in ORANGE LEADER.
When The Bulletin went to press on
Tuesday night many of the N.S. W. country re-
'turning-officers had not even received instructions
as to where the local polling-places were to be
situated,; and a large proportion of the baekblocks
population wasn’t in a position to vote, for the
sufficient reason that it really didn't know
where'the vote would be taken. Which is one of
the many ways in which a God-fearing Ministry
can help to stifle the popular voice. The loss of
votes tmthe anti-Billious is nothing, for they have
no hope of getting a majority, but every vote lost
on the. Federal side reduces the chance of reaching
the 80,000 minimum.
“ The Dragging War” is the heading the
Age now sticks up. David Syme evidently re-
cau(.;on of the be llj g erents ; the Age
readers are waiting for good copy.
“If I were a citizen of Victoria, S. Aus-
tra ]j a or Tasmania, I would stump those pro-
vinces trom end to end in favor of the Bill,’ said
p . Reid t Goulburn. If he spoke the truth
the Bill would enable
these provinces to rob N.S.W., and that he would
be the eloquent boss-robber of the gang. But if
Re id was a Tasmanian politician he would proba-
bly be rousing his province with the yell that
wben it had only five members of the Federal Lower
House to N.S.WVs 25, and the three small pro-
vinces all together had only 17 to N.S.W.’s 25,
t hey wou l d be wholly at the mercy of the big
grasping province, and Federation meant suicide.

More curious items about the great Hull
grab of 250,000 acres of Papua at 2s. an acre, with
illimitable other concessions thrown in, keep com-
ing to light. A despatch of Governor M'Gregor,
which has just turned up, says distinctly that
Duncan Gillies, of Victoria, is one of the pro-
moters ; but the good old boomer, Duncan, denies
this. And it is announced in London that the
great scheme was prepared after long consultation
at Government House, Brisbane, which certainly
looks—well, peculiar. It is also stated that,
though the job has only just come to light, the
syndicate has already got started and has grabbed
over 30,000 acres. In one way and another the
great Papuan swindle promises to be the most
smellful steal of this generation. The latest re-
garding it is that Chamberlain, on the protest
f q Q ovt has suggested some alterations in the
*-• (the swindle-agreement.
At present the most sensational cry of the
Sydney paper which lies so magnificently in the
interests of disunion is that Federation will m-
volve an extra cost of about 2s 2d per head per
annum to every Victorian, and about LI 2s. od.
per head per annum to every N.S. Waler. The
man in each province will pay the same taxation,
and the same proportion of the Federal expendi-
turej and be governed by the same laws, so how
a fj tbe disorganised universe this result is to
come about only the great boss liar who fries
eternally down below is likely ever to find out.
And as N.S. W. will have 31 votes to Victoria’s 28,
t i, e rea son why the big province should let the
Slna ]j province play such a swindle on her is past
comprehension. Most of the stories of the dread-
ful things that are to happen to N.S.W. in the
Federal Legislature evidently rest on the assump-
t;on that the 31 N.S.W. representatives will all be
struck with permanent idiocy.

TENHAM STATION
(Outside Tick-Infested Area),
Situated in Gregory South district of Queensland, con-
sisting of 276 square miles of countrj’ (more or less), of
which 190 square miles are Leasehold, having 17 years to
run from the Ist July, 1897, and 86 square miles of country
held under Grazing Right, under “Crown Lands Act of
1884.” Together with
6500 CATTLE (more or less), Fifty (50) HORSES (more
or less), STATION PLANT, STORES and IM-
PROVEMENTS thereon or belonging thereto.
The Country is situated on Cooper’s Creek and Kyabra
Creek, about 40 miles south of Windorah and about 230
miles west of Charleville railway station. It is very rich
fattening country for cattle, consisting of lightly timbered,
open, mulga country, with occasional black-soil plains,
abundance of salt-bush, blue-bush, cotton-bush, and other
edible shrubs. TENHAM is splendidly watered by the
main channel of the Cooper and its many branches, be-
sides springs. The Cattle are quiet, and have been well
■worked. In the herd there are about 1000 head of Bul-
locks and Cows fit to start for market. The Improvements
are sufficient for the efficient working of the station.
Particulars and Conditions of Sale may be had gratis at
the office of Messrs. Macdonald-Paterson and Hawthorn,
Edward-street, Brisbane, Solicitors for the Plaintiff;
Messrs. J. F. Fitzgerald and Power, Queen-street, Brisbane,
Solicitors for the Defendants ; or at the offices of the said
B. D. Morehead & Co., Brisbane; Messrs. Hill, Clark &
Co., Sydney ; Messrs. Ryan, Hammond & Mates, Mel-
bourne ; and Messrs. Barker Bros., Adelaide, and at the
place of sale.
Dated this twenty-fifty day of April, 1898.
(L.S.) W. A. DOUGLAS,
Deputy Registrar.

The bye - election of journalist Ryott-
Maughan for the Burnett (Q.) is another Labor
nail in the Boodlewraith coffin. What exactly
would happen to Thomas Joseph Byrnes and his
crowd if he dared to face the people, instead of
taking and giving office by laying-on of hands, is
a matter for enthusiastic conjecture.
George Dibbs, in his anti-Billious speech
on Monday night, could grow no larger lion to
plant in the path of Federation than that it was
“ a leap in the dark.” Which sounds well. But
then, the man who doesn’t leap in the dark gets
run over by the night express, or knocked down
by the wandering cow. More men have been
killed in the dark through not leaping quickly
enough than from any other cause.
“Austral ” writes :
Dear Bulletin. —Why doesn’t Deakin or somebody Vic.
way get up and demolish Tiilett in his Anti-Federation
flapdoodle, anyway? The writer heard him at the Sunday
meeting recently, and Til'e'i- talked inconclusive twaddle
for haif-an-hour, and n ostly abused the !man who has
worked steadily for thet onimonwealth for years. Tiilett
hasn’t lived long enough m ibis country to know all the
evils of disunion. He asks us to throw away this chance,
but hasn’t been through all the weary struggles that were
necessary before we got it. He’s not a citizen of any
province, and hasn’t got a vote, and if we had to fight he
couldn’t be compelled to take up arms in defence
of our country. All the Anti Federalists in Victoria
worthy of any abuse are importations from Hingland, but
Ben has more brass, or less sense, than the rest. He gets
his ideas from the Melbourne Trades Hall crowd, and its
opinions are guided mostly by its bitter hatred of Tren-
with, and it hates Trenwith because he has risen above
the ruck. Good Democrat as he is, the Trartes Hall
clique would sell its soul and the Commonwealth too to
pull him down. We, who belong to this continent, want
to get our hands all over it now, before any more syndi-
cates swamp it with niggers or any foreign power comes
knocking at our gates, and when we've done that Ben
can come along and help Hancock and Maloney and
Barker to run the concern. But if this chance is lost,
Ben won’t have to struggle to make another one, and if
in the interval Britain were to foreclose its mortgage on
the Northern Territory and lease it to one of Somers
Vine’s nigger and Chinese syndicates (a danger that is
always with us) it would be our disaster—not Ben’s.
Last Tuesday the Sydney Telegraph re-
ported that there were 277,000 names on the N.S.
Wales general roll and 31,000 on the additional
roll, but of the latter about 15,000 are only trans-
fers from one roll to another, therefore there are
about 293,000 names in all —including the dead
and absent. The moral of which is that no elec-
tor can afford to stay away from the poll with the
idea that there are plenty without him. On 18th
November, 1897, when the roll was smaller than
it is now, Attorney-General Want told the N.S.
Wales Legislative Council, as a reason for raising
the minimum to 80,000, that there were “ nearly
350,000 ” electors on the lists (Hansard, page
4801). The argument, of course, was that with
350,000 voters, the original 50,000 minimum was
absurdly small. Plainly, the anti-Billious were
just as unreliable in all their statements then as
they have been ever since.
In the Sydney libel-case of Green v.
Evening News, Justice Stephen ruled that a
newspaper’s reports of preliminary judicial pro-
ceedings were not privileged, and the jury found
themselves obliged to find for plaintiff, vvith a
farthing damages. There was no question of
malice, and the report was a mere statement of
what took place in a court open to the public.
The Judge regretted that the law here was not
the same as in England, where a fair and accurate
report of all judicial proceedings is legal. In
these circumstances the jury probably did the
best it could by giving plaintiff a nominal
verdict, which, The Bulletin presumes, will not
carry costs. If it had taken the more inviting
course of d ning the obsolete law and finding
for defendant, the lawyers would at once have
moved for (and got) a new trial, and the last state
of the News might have been worse than the
first. The case is another illustration of the
shocking inadequacy and stupidity of N.S.W.
libel-law, which is as far behind that of England
as it ought to be in front of it.

Vic. Judge Hodges had a pretty case of
sharp-practice to deal with the other day. Plain-
tiff sued for =£275, advanced to his friend, the
defendant, in 1891, on a verbal contract that the
cash should be repaid, with 6 per cent, interest,
when he could take the money out of his business
—the loan being granted as part of a first deposit
on an outside land transaction. Defendant be-
came fully possessed of the land in question.
Also, his business flourished meanwhile, but he
still stuck to the £275 loan. He paid neither
principal nor interest to his trusting pal, and,
when sued for the debt, he pleaded that the
Statute of Limitations had put it beyond recovery.
Judge Hodges brought what Gilbert’s Lord
Chancellor called “ the subtlety of the legal
mind ”to bear in favor of common justice. “ The
Court had to determine the question whether or
not the cause of action arose within six years of
the bringing of the action. Plaintiff said the
agreement was in effect that the money was to be
paid when defendant could take the money out of
his business. Defendant said that from the date
of the loan to the present time he was always able
to take the money out of his business, in a certain
sense, but, as the court understood him, while he
was in a position to do so at the time of the loan,
in a certain sense it would have been incon-
venient.” And as the intention of the plaintiff,
said Judge Hodges, was not to damage defen-
dant’s business, the cause of action didn’t arise
until 1896, when defendant gave a certain mort-
gage on the land aforesaid. Seeing that 1896
was not six years ago, plaintiff got a judgment for
<£275 principal with costs. Plaintiff’s generous
consideration for the sharp man’s business interests
served to pull him triumphantly through v he
Statute of Limitations.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Perfect weather could not draw more than 5000
people to Tattersall's Club Races at Rand wick last
Saturday. Cyprus won the Hurdle race from the favorite
Orysohte, and Strathroy and Sailor Boy, the two best-
backed in the Flying Handicap, finished in the order
named. Backers then began to go down, and the books
to make up some of their recent run of bad luck. Lilly-
dale at 10 to 1 won Tatt's Stakes; Flodden, at 12 to 1,
beat Glendennon by a head for the Winter Stakes ; and
backers received the most unkind cut of ail when Gaulme,
absolutely unthought of, won the Welter Handicap.
On the hill outside Randwick Course a China-
man makes a book each race-day and does good biz. A
/, betting goes on there and the punter bets
with the Chow “ for luck.” John sings out the odds,
tlee to one Stlathloy,” etc., and is generally a point
longer than the “clistian bookie.”
* * * *
All day long, and nearly all night too, the big
betting-shops in Pitt-st., Sydney, do a thriving trade un-
molested. A Chinaman lately opened a similar shop in
an obscure corner of Redfern. A police-emissary invested
« ’ j n 0n k' B e an( l "’on 9d., and the heathen was
fined £5O and costs by a Sydney magistrate on a recent
Tuesday. On this scale N.S.W. could easily pay its awful
federal expenses by a big raid on the city gambling
• • • •
Rosehill Racing Club holds its winter meeting
on Saturday, June 25, the event of the day being Rosehill
Cup of 200 sovs. with a £5O trophy. Tatte'rsal! has a large
consultation on this race, and seeing that the most im-
portant feature of a race nowadays is that Tatt. should
have a consultation on it, so that the drawer can lav the
owner of the quadruped something to nothing, this is
worth mentioning.
* * * *
The season’s opening run of Sydney Hunt Club
was held last week, and so deplorable an exhibition of
colonial ” horsemanship was never before seen. The
ladies all rode excellently, but the men, mostly military
johnnies, followed a few miles behind and generally fell off
at every fence. Of course there were exceptions which
it would be invidious to name, but they were few and far
between. Those military swells should in future walk to
the hunts and climb carefully through each fence. Or,
perhaps, the ladies will coach them a little.
* a a a
Melbourne backers who went to Flemington and
ou ®®dc on Queen’s Birthday came back from the
V.R.C. meeting full of joy and whiskv, in most cases.
Five events fell to decided first favorites, two of them,
froin the Miller stable, starting at 4to 1 and 9to 4. Ikey
Mo's only consolation was in the poorness of the attend-
ee, as things turned out. The wind was tempered to
the shorn fleecer by the rain that trickled down his neck
and kept a number of his usual customers in town. The
ram did even more than this for Ikey. It sometimes
drove punters to the grand stand for shelter at the very
moment when they were making up their minds to back
the next winner.
* • * .
The Millers have another likely Grand National
horse in the five-year-old Nilus. In'the two-mile steeple
at Flemington, last week, Nilus carried 12st. successfully
through the mud after giving the top of the stone wall a
clout that- nearly brought him to grief. He hadn’t much
to beat at the finish, but in the meantime he had pegged
along comfortably whilst five of the others were falling
down. B

Sydney trotter St. Louis cracked a hoof prior
to his first M.L. engagement, and hackers would not look
at him in the Mile Dash, 14 starters. Winner’s div.,
£26 6s. Dr. Slate, who went over to see his pair trot’
had no luck on the first day, at any rate. His pony,
Narragansett, shaped badly at the wrong end in the pony
trot, won by the Sydney-bred Aileen, who returned
backers just enough to buy drinks with.
Sunshine, stable-mate of Fritz, possessor of un-
deniable speed, broke up and distanced a field of eight in
the Maiden, albeit Sunbeam’s daughter trotted un-
steadily at the outset. She was such a hot- cert, that the
tote return was only £1 Bs. Mr. Buckland had a lucky
turn with Sunshine later on. The largeness of the field
in the Electric Trot, and smallness of the tote, necessi-
tated the “ bracketing” of Sunshine and Awahuri. The
latter materialised, paying £6 45., and, being bracketed
with him, Sunshine’s backers scooped that div. though
she didn’t get a place.

• • * •
Obit. : William Douglas, Maoriland’s best-
known and staunchest turf-supporter, aged 63. A ster-
ling sport; large breeder, and “straight” owner. He
held pronounced ideas on racing and breeding, and made
hone and substance of superior importance even to
pedigree when buying stud stock. Result—innumerable
strongly-con4itutioned racers who went on winning
long after higher-class, higher-priced, spider-legged flut-
terers succumbed to the exigencies of training. The “all
black ” was successful in a sackful of races during the last
25 years. Douglas preferred a steeple to a cup, and won
M.L. Grand National three times. He was a colonist of
48 years’ standing, and carried on sheep and cattle raising,
largely, since the sixties.
• * * •
“ Newhaven’s rush in the City and Suburban
fairly corpsed the onlookers,” writes The Bulletin’s
London correspondent. “ Amongst the last three to get
away a quarter of a mile from home, Rickaby could not
have dreamt his mount had ‘ a possible,’ and, as there is
no ‘all horses to be ridden out’ regulation here, did not
seem to be making an effort. Suddenly the Australian
spread his legs, and, in giant strides, overlapped every-
thing, too late to catch Bay Ronald, for the judge’s eye
was fifty to a hundred yards too adjacent. The horse’s
form was raw-nakedly exposed, however, and his handlers
for their clumsiness deserve all the weight the handi-
cappers are sure to give them now. It was the chance of
a splendid coup hopelessly bungled, and it is doubtful
whether Cooper will ever look upon its like again—with
this crack, anyhow.”
Concerning which a local commentator :
The second defeat of Newhaven by Bay Ronald (for
the Epsom Cup) indicates—as was here suggested at
the time—that B.R. won the City and Sub. Handicap
with a lot in hand, and Newhaven, when he rushed into
second place on that occasion, was passing two or three,
or more, horses which had already been slightly eased.
Newhaven’s two defeats by the same animal in lj and 1 j
mile races don’t count much against his chance of win-
ning over longer courses at Ascot. He is entered for the
8-miles Alexandra Plate. But this seoond failure seems
to knock the stuffing out of a lot of English newspaper
pars, lately to hand. The horsey writers were quite cer-
tain that Newhaven should have won the City and Sub.
If this wonderful rush had started a little earlier, the re-
sult would have been different, &c., &e. The Epsom
Cup race last week was J mile longer than the other, but
Bay Ronald wag again oue too many for the Australian
champion.
• * * *
Joe Thompson, in a letter to Australia, says
he saved Newhaven in the City and Suburban for
his book, and stood to win £20,000. Joe’s version
is that the field jumped away without Newhaven,
who was 100yds. to the bad before a quarter mile was
covered. Turning into the straight the Australian seemed
out of she hunt, but he made a marvellous run, passing
everything but Bay Ronald. “ Had it been another
100yd*.," Thompson said, “ Newhaven must have won.”
But it looks as if the race was truly run.

* » • *
What is to be will be. On April 14th, 4200
guineas were paid for the English Derby colt Dunlop,
and he failed to run into a place. A Mr. Larnach bought
a mare called Pilgrimage for 160 guineas, unaware that
she was in foal. She foaled, and the offspring, Jeddah,
started at IUO to 1 and won this year’s Derby. ’
* * • *
Australian horses are going down like ninepins
at ’Ome. Galway started second favorite for a Steeple-
chase at Manchester and pulled up beaten ; Maluma
started favourite in a race at Newmarket and finished
sixth ; whilst Newhaven’stwo beatings must have cleared
out the followers of the Australians in England. Next
time they ought to give Newhaven a chance of winning
races after his Australian fashion—from end to end at a
solid pace of his own making.
* * * •
A suggestion from ’Frisco, that the best man
available in Australia be imported into the States to show
the locals how to start horses with the barrier, is a season-
able one. Here is a chance of a good trip and dollars
for Moorefield “ Jack ” Hagerty.
* • * *
At a recent up-country bazaar a young blood
with more money than brains bought a rocking-horse,
took it home and played an old trick-raffled the horse
for £2O in aid of the good cause. But, as luck would have
it, the winner didn’t care a rap for the cause and raised
Cain when he found the animal was dead timber. That
young sport had to shell out another £2O to square
matters, and now goes about damning the Good Cause.
* ♦ * *
A certain Northern racing committee having
been “ducked” through the rider of the second horse
pulling the committee’s certainty, could not form a
quorum to wipe out their fancy. The necessary 4th man
(a local J.P.) was found “ tight as Adam,” but it was
absolutely necessary to have him, and he was hauled out,
and promptly voiced the committee's wishes by not only
wiping out the seoond, but added—“ the rider has been
guilty of the biggest bit of larrifeinism I’ve ever met with
in my experience as a magistrate. He is sintenced to
seven days' without the option of a fine.”
* * * *
A rampageous bookmaker writes from Maori-
land to inform The Bulletin that all its tote arguments
are “ rotten as a pear," and he doesn’t care a bad word
for its asterisk bully-ragging. Pleasant to get glad
tidings from distant friends.
» » • *
Experiments in speying, as the operation is
called in connection with cows, are being tried with several
fillies in America, and if successful will no doubt come in
vogue this side. A word will have to be coined to
describe the unsexed mare in the racing world, and the
question will arise as to whether the speyed mare will be
allowed a gelding’s concession in weight.
* * * .
There was nothing brilliant in Sydney football
last Saturday. Brisbane PastGrainmarSehoolsplaved Wal-
laroos on Sydney Cricket Ground, and although the sides
were evenly matched, as evidenced by the score—B points
each—the play was poor. University and Paddington then
met and played a one-sided match, University running
over its opponents by 19 to nil.- On the Agricultural
Ground, Pirates and Sydneys played the best game of the
day.. At half-time the score stood Sydney 6, Pirates nil,
but in the second half Pirates ran over the new club,
winning by 14 to 6. Casualties—a broken head and a
couple of laid-outs.
* * * *
When Rugby football was first introduced to
Charters Towers many of the players, no matter how
ignorant of the game, were ready to argue a point with
the umpire, wherefor one referee always carried the rules
in his pocket, and the play would frequently stop while
an excited group listened to the law on the point at issue.
Backing out of a scrimmage one day, an irate player,
carpenter by trade, who had been badly hacked, rushed
the referee. “ Here,” he bellowed, “ let’s have a look at
them blanky specifications 1 ”
» * * »
When Mt. Morgan football-team visited Charters
Towers, the “ unco guid,” headed by a few clergymen,
wired the Chief-Secretary that a charge was to be made
for entrance to a public reserve on Sunday, and, as a
result, the superintendent of police was instructed to
interfere. John Marsland, president of the local
union, then advertised “admission by ticket only," ob-
tainable beforehand, and, on the appointed afternoon,
when the police superintendent made his objections,
Marsland said, “I have invited a number of my friends,
sir, to witness a football-game on my own ground. This
reserve was pegged out by me as a mining area, and ap-
plication for the lease lodged with the warden on Satur-
day morning.” Under the Act, the reserve was thus as
much Marsland’s property as a goldfields homestead area,
for the 30 days which would have to elapse before objec-
tions could be entertained.
* * • *
A London weekly, The Man of the World,
shrieks “The manner in which the English batsmen
pottered about the block-hole caused roars of laughter to
the free-hitting Australians. If we are to regain our
supremacy in cricket, a bolder style of play must he
adopted. Playing for averages has spoilt English
cricket.” This sort of stuff must be very consoling to
poor old Stoddart. His team didn't suffer much by com-
parison with the Australians as regards hitting'. The
trouble was that the bowling didn’t allow them to potter
a sufficiently grand total in four out of five test-matches.
They played on good wickets for the averages which they
had achieved in England on wickets not so good—and,
as a rule, didn’t approach them.
* * * •
Westralian cricket item : “ Mount Benson met
the Lady Mary team on the ground of the former, but
their innings proved an ignominious failure, the whole
eleven being disposed of for U runs. When the Lady Mary
■went to the wickets the first hit made was a fiver, so the
match was won. The innings closed for 4fi runs.”
* ♦ * *
Albert Edward, the latest “Royal cyclist,’
took third money (and probably won some' “ place ” bets)
for the Derby with Dunlop, by Ayrshire. The Australian
Ayrshire, singularly enough, is by Dunlop, who got his
name long before pneumatic bicycle tyres were thought
of.
• * * •
P.Q. : At a recent Melb. Amateur Cyclists’
banquet, a Mr. Jones, in a fine, outspoken speech
of four lines, said that the present advance in the pro-
gress of amateurism was due to their striving to keep the
sport pure and above suspicion, like Ciesar’s eggs. The
sport! Save the mark. They meet the ladies every Satur-
day afternoon, and exhibit themselves with buglers and
captains and bells along the suburban streets, to journey
(that’s the expression) to a hotel a little way out, where
they get duly photographed. Then they feed sumptu-
ously. Afterwards the Father of Cycling, and kindred
popular characters, whose careers are sketched each week
in the cycling papers, pass the butter to one another, and
the opportunity is seized of handing over the attendance-
medals and making presentations to popular Billies—
mostly subscribed for by the ladies. “Harmony then
prevails,” and the heroes ride home in the moonlight,
vowing that they have never enjoyed a more pleasant
outing, &c., &c.
* • » *
Lacrosse is still determined to assert itself in
Melb. New clubs, and vigorous attempts at enthusiasm
every Saturday, and regular practice during the week.
The game is understood to be a sort of silken bond be-
tween Canada and this country. Hands lacrosse the sea!
3rd June, 1898.—In Hope.
From the cane-brakes of the northlands to the wheat-
fields of the south,
From the pillared gates of Sydney to the home of flood
and drouth,
Out west to the Never Never where the red sun sinks
to rest,
They march in tens of thousands strong to greet the
people’s guest.
They come to crown our lady, the fa : rest of the fair
Young daughters of the radiant earth, with laurels in
her hair,
They come in tens of thousands strong to bid a nation
wake
Prepared to live and fight and die, if need be, for her
sake.
With hands across the rivers and with hearts that beat in
tune,
We’ll brothers be with south and west upon the third of
June;
No border lines shall stand between, no microscopic
soul
Shall hiss its words of paltry hate where Murray’s waters
roll.
We’ll crown her with a laurel wreath, with ferns will
strew her way,
We’ll travel leagues of weary miles and loyal homage pay,
And north and south and east and west, where’er her
temples be,
We’ll sign her charter full and fair from Darwin to the
sea.
And millions in the future days shall bless the men who
gave
To fair Australia greeting kind and homage true ar;c!
brave,
The men who set her feet upon the pathway bright of
fame,
And throned her ’mid the nations with a heritage and
name.
MIRANI.

The following remarks, from Melbourne
Liberator, seem almost to suggest that Jos.
Symesis trying to be unfriendly—or possibly it is
only his way of disguising his emotion :
It is very clear that the Sydney Bulletin has got into
the hands of the Jesuits ; and the sooner people come to
understand that the better for then). Instead of the old
joyous, jolly, rude, laughter-provoking journal it once
was [Ah, were we ever joyous ?], it is now cunning,
morose, abusive, pessimistic and diabolical. Over the
Federal Bill it has completely lost its head, and pours out
incoherent nonsense and lying abuse against all who do
not support its fad or worse. It is clear that the owners
of The Bulletin have much to gain—or to hope for —in
the success of the Bill ; and they compel their pen-slaves
to scatter the most outrageous falsehoods and misrepre-
sentations abroad. Still, there is comfort for good and
honest people in all this —the Jesuit organ could never
support anything good, nor abstain from cracking up the
most absolutely worst. The fury of The Bulletin shows
that its Jesuit owners expect the'Bill to be defeated.
And, only a week ago, the Sydney Holy Homan
organ called this paper a poisonous pink infidel,
and a crocodile, and something in the Iscariot
line, and said that any Christian would prefer to
see a snake on the table. Will some good Hebrew
now kindly arise and throw things ?
The ex-convict-transport Lady Franklin,
built by convict labor at Port Arthur, Tas., in
1814, is now being broken up at Hobart. She was
employed carrying passengers and stores toNorfolk
Island from Hobart continuously till ’54. In Dec.
’53, on the 17th day out from Hobart, her convict
complement rose in revolt and took possession of
the ship. Locking the guard and crew below they
made free with the stores and otherwise enjoyed
themselves for several days. Then they took to
the boats. They left one boy free, and him they
posted at the mast-head with the key of the
prison and instructions not to release the confined
ones till a certain time had elapsed. The boy
obeyed his orders to the letter and pursuit was
thus rendered futile. The vessel at once headed back
for port arriving 25th Jan., ’54. Capt. Willett,
the master, received severe injuries during the
fracas, having his shoulder broken. Some of
the escapees were afterwards recaptured on
an island where they had taken refuge.
It was said that a woman on board aided the
mutineers by hocussing the guard, but the general
opinion was that the soldiers sided with the con-
victs, and made but a half-hearted opposition to
the rising. In 1855, Mr. Downing, a merchant of
Hobart, bought the ship for ,£2050, and fitted her
for whaling, re-naming her the Emily Downing.
She was added to Alick M'Gregor’s whaling fleet
in ’64, and long sailed under his “ gridiron ” flag,
making many profitable voyages till the whaling
industry languished. In ’B5 Tas. Govt, bought
her and turned her into a powder-hulk, which
position she filled till a few months ago. Her first
christening was in honor of Lady Franklin, wife
of the then Governor, and a figure allegedly repre-
senting this lady has ever since adorned her bows.

LEGAL MANAGER :
VVM. ROBERTSON, F.F.1.A., Licensed Auditor, &c.
The Great Victoria Colliery Company No Liability,
which will be the means of creating a coaling port within
the colony’s own borders, has after a large expenditure
developed and brought to a producing stage a very valu-
a le coal mine, easy of access to shipping, and now pro-
poses issuing the balance of Reserved Shares in hand for
the purpose of providing a Wages Fund to cover all
expenses and costs of working up to the time the coal is
placed on the market and the Mine itself revenue pro-
ducing—being the object for which such shares were
originally' reserved.
To epitomise.—After conscientious work, extending
over three years, and the expenditure of ±BB3O 16s. lOd.
to 30th April, 1898, the Co. has proved and made avail-
able for actual Coal winning a very large area of high-
class black coal, estimated by reliable experts to contain
in one seam alone upwards Of 11,000,000 tens of Market-
able Coal, and has almost completed the construction of
a line of Railway from the pit’s mouth to the port of ship-
ment— 7J miles long—in itself a work of considerable
magnitude—to enable it to market its coal.
The purchase of all the necessary railway iron from the
Railway department has been arranged, of which delivery
is now being taken, a substantial sum having been paid
on account, and a powerful locomotive and 38 Railway
Waggons in first-class order have been secured and paid
for. The mine workings are efficiently opened up, fully
equipped and capable of an immediate output of 100 tons
best black coal per day, which can be largely increased in
the near future without new works, its quality being
attested by consumers of unquestionable standing in the
commercial community.
The work yet to be done to enable the company to
make itself felt as an important factor in the Coal Supply
of the Australian colonies (and in the present troubled
times a matter of grave concern to British statesmen as
an element in our Naval Supremacy, vide the Argus
Leader, 25th April, 1898), is comparatively trifling in re-
spect to that already carried out —it being estimated that
a little over £lOOO will suffice, and nearly this amount is
already accruing to the Company on shares recently sub-
scribed by present shareholders, so that the fresh capital
now sought will be actually invested in improving and
extending the operations of an extremely valuable and
progressive property, free from the drag of old liabilities
or losses.
In conclusion, therefore, the directors have every con-
fidence in placing this Prospectus before the public and
in recommending the venture as an investment, possess-
ing all the elements of success.
Applications for shares may be made to the Manager or
the Company’s Brokers, from whom full prospectus may
be obtained."
WM. ROBERTSON, F.F.I.A.
Manager.
Prell’s-buildings, 70 Queens-street,
Melbourne, 14t,h May, 1898.

THE
Rortb Queensland ghillagoe
Exploration Company
NO LIABILITY.
Capital: £12,800, in 2300 Shares of £3 each.
This Company has acquired about 500 acres of valuable
mineral lands in the Chillagoe district, comprised in five
or six leases, ranging from 40 to 200 acres.
The Company’s representative having been on the field
since February has had the opportunity of selecting only
good claims, after a thorough prospecting, and, conse-
quently, the Company has every reason to believe the
leases taken up will prove productive from the first.
Assays of samples taken from a large lode from Copper
Hill, area about 200 acres, 12 miles westerly from Mont-
albion— range, from 20 to 45 per cent, copper, from 2 to
14 07. silver, with 2dwts. of gold. The Company also
owns 175 acres at Mt. Garnet, 40 of which are adjoining
Mr. Moffat’s well-known claim, besides other leases con-
taining large formations in adjacent localities.
All information pertaining to the Company may be
obtained from the Brokers, Messrs. Menzies and Moule,
406 Collins-street, Melbourne ; or from Messrs. Rollason,
Brandon and Co., Secretaries, 39 Queen-st., Melbourne.

Vic. Full Court has received a painful
smack in the eye from the Privy Council in the
everlasting matter of the Alliance Contracting
co. (London) v. Russell, who represents a French
syndicate in Melb. Said Alliance co. claims to
have bought a Westralian mining property from
said syndicate through its erstwhile agent, one
Cayron. Two years ago, when plaintiff 00.
applied for an order to compel specific perform-
ance of the disputed sale contracts, Judge Hodges
gave a qualified verdict for the defendant syndi-
cate. The case was then carried to the Full
Court (Chief Justice Madden and Justices Wil-
liams and Holroyd) on appeal. The appeal was
dismissed, but their Honors gave judgment for
plaintiffs for ,£l2OO, which had been paid on
account of the contracts and retained by defend-
ant. The desire of the Full Court to
put a stop to further proceedings was
somewhat marked. Plaintiff co., not satisfied
to take back the ,£l2OO and retire from the fray,
got leave to appeal to the Privy Council, giving
security in £5OO for the prosecution of the appeal.
Six months later, defendant applied to a Judge
in chambers for a decision that aforesaid appeal
had been abandoned. This question was referred
to the Full Court (Chief-Jus, Madden, Justice
Hodges, and Justice Holroyd), and the learned
triumvirate ruled in defendant’s favor— i.e. , they
took upon themselves to say that the appeal to the
British Court had been abandoned (although
plaintiff said it hadn’t), and practically took pos-
session of the .£5OO lodged as guarantee of plain-
tiff’s good faith, A gain the desire to stop further
proceedings Was distinctly apparent, and Melb.
AgE, commenting on the Full Court’s ruling, sug-
gested that it was absurdly unsound, Plaintiff
therl appealed direct to the Privy Council for
leave tb appeal, and the P.C. last week granted
the leaVe which the Vic. Full Cduft had pre-
viously cancelled. Also, the P.C. has restrained
Vic. Full Court from distributing the ,£5OO
security. Victorian justice is on its trial just now.

The Anglomurkan National
Anthem.
(’Apropos de V entente cor diale.}
[For The Bulletin.]
God save thee, O my country,
The greatest upon airth—■
Victorious and glorious
And happy, and so forth !
God save our noble Jonathan,
God bless our gracious Bull!
(Excuse these muddled epithets—
My swelling breast is full!)
Chorus:
Hurrah for Bullanjonathan /
Three cheers for Johnansam /
The A nglomurkannation
Is bound to lick creation ;
Geewhillikins I Tarnation /
Goldarn ! and likewise Damn t
We fear no Dons and devils,
Or treachery of Spain ;
Our noble Maine they scuttled—
We’ll sweep the Spanish Main >
Beware th’ EagliOn’s talons,
The Lioneagle’s paw !
Shout “ God Save Yankee Doodle
And “Dewey et mon droit!”
Chorus : Hurrah, dec.
Who says we are but traders ?
Why, that is all my eye !
We don’t sell wooden nutmegs,
We make the Spanish fly !
For islands rent and riven
The ghost of Philip pines;
We’ll blow our mild Havana
’Mid smoke of forts and mines !
Chorus : Hurrah, dec.
They said we ran for shelter —
Confound their knavish tricks *
We merely kept on programme .
“We start to shoot at six ; ”
At nine we stop for breakfast
(Qui mal pense, honi soit!)
And then we give them gruel
With Dewey et mon Droit!
Chorus : Hurrah, dec.
Ye Royal Red Republicans,
Twist not the Lion’s tail!
Fling out the Union-Jonathan,
Whose Stars shall never fail.
St. George for merry Yankland !
Break fetters from the slaves,
Free sons of Columbanglia—
Brityankia rules the waves !
Chorus : Hurrah, dec.
M.L.
J. LIDDELL KELLY.
Users of “anti-fats” will probably be
badly scared by the medical pronouncement that
the Empress of Germany’s recent break-down in
health was due to her persistent use of one of these
nostrums.
“Basher’s Hurricane.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Astonishment is a word which faintly expresses
the feeling of the ship’s company when we learnt
that Capt. Basher had married. And when we
saw the lady the puzzle grew harder than ever.
She had been a Mrs. M'Cluskie —and looked it.
Tall, bony, red, and freckled, with eyes the color
of galvanised iron, and teeth which would not
have disgraced a small elephant—she was the
finest sample of the unadulterated female porridge-
eater that I ever clapped eyes on. She was to sail
with us, too. We trembled.
It turned out that the captain had married her
for the sake of the late M‘01uskie’s money, and
during the first half of that voyage he earned
every penny of it. The ex-widow ruled the ship
from the first moment she put her prunella-shod
foot aboard us. Basher, the rollicking dare-devil,
was tamed to the demeanor of a mouse. His wife
was as pious as she was prudish, and she was as
prudish as a prayer-meeting full of old maids.
Basher shivered in his complaisance. He cuffed
an ordinary seaman for unwittingly applying its
proper name to a certain portion of the topsail brace-
block. The coppered part of the vessel was always
alluded to as “ the underneath part ” in the lady’s
presence, and the belly of a sail was spoken of
apologetically, with bated breath. On Mrs. B.’s
washing-days, it was sacrilege to look over the
counter, for the more essentially feminine of her
garments were hung to dry from a sort of “ parshee-
boom,” rigged from the stern ports, and secure
from the irreverent gaze of the rude sailor-man.
Basher lived two lives. With Mrs. B. below, he
was his own profane self; with Mrs. B. on deck, it
was “ Mr. Brown, kindly step for’ed, and ascertain
if the fore-to’-gallant-mast is sufficiently stayed,”
or “ Bos’n, please inform the hands that to-mor-
row, being the Sabbath, Divine service will be
held in the saloon, as usual ” —ye gods ! The
strain on Basher must have been awful.
We were somewhere about the 50th parallel of
latitude, having had to go farther to the south-
ward than is usual to look for wind, when Capt.
Basher rose in revolt. “ This is too d d fine a
passage, Mr. Brown,” he explained confidentially
as he arranged his plan of campaign; “an’ I
guess, if we don’t assist the blanky course of
Nature, I’ll never live to see port. So —to-mor-
row night! ”
To-morrow night came, starlit and beautiful.
The weather being decidedly cold, Mrs. Basher
had retired early to her bunk. There was a big
swell; and the vessel was brought beam-on to it.
The dead-lights, previously loosened by the car-'
penter, were removed from the deck above the.
cabin in which the lady slumbered, and a length
of hose was led to reach from the “ Downton ”
pump, at which four vigorous seamen waited
eagerly for the order to “shake her up.” A couple
of hands were also stationed on the half-round,
with draw-buckets.
The ship was rolling terribly, scooping it up
over both rails, and threatening to sling the
“sticks” out of her. Captain Basher arrived on
deck in a hurry, having locked his lady-love in her
berth.
“ Now, then, my lads, let her have it! ” he said,
and we did. That pump was never worked as it
worked that night, and the water poured in at the
deck whilst the men with the draw-buckets kept
up a terrific broadside of cold salt-water through
the open ports. The rest of the watch were em-
ployed with handspikes in thundering on the deck
above Mrs. B.’s head. Basher at intervals bel-
lowed orders of a heroic nature, whilst an
apprentice and I kept the little brass signal-gun
going till she got too hot to use.
Every few minutes our gallant captain would
rush below to the door of the cabin. “ Be brave,
Jessie, my love, and remain where you are ! We
may yet weather this awful storm,” he would roar
through the keyhole ; or, “ Do not abandon hope,
my dear beloved wife ! the hurricane is now on
our weather bow; but trust in God and your
William, and remember everything depends on
your keeping cool.” (Poor Mrs. B. was in all con-
science cool enough.) Then, deaf to her screams
to be liberated, Basher would rush on deck again
to exhort us to further exertion.
We kept it up for two hours, when the captain
gave orders to “ ease up handsomely,” and on
things being made shipshape, the vessel was
brought to her course again. Basher, having
donned his oilskin and a life-belt, was pumped on
to clinch the realism, and went below to his
affrighted spouse, who was by this time more dead
than alive.
* * * *
We made the Cape ten days afterwards, and
Mrs. B. did not continue the voyage with us, pre-
ferring to take steam to Plymouth. That was
ten years ago, and the lady has never forgotten
the horrors of that awful night. Captain Basher
is living on her money somewhere Deptford way,
secure from hurricanes and similar perils, for his
wife will not hear of him going to sea again, with
or without he r . 12
Maoriland.

A Black Dress.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ He counted the weeks he had lain there —ten of
them, and seemingly no nearer the end than at
the beginning. He wondered idly how much
longer he would live. They must be getting im-
patient—it took so long—so long to die. And yet
the time was not altogether tedious, the pain was
not so great now. He wondered if she would
regret much. What a young widow ! —after only
18 months’ married life !
Alice would have been sorry. Why had she
left him so soon with but a frail babe as a remem-
brance. Something dropped in the next room and
disturbed him ; they had been busy there the last
few days.
The door opened, and a little girl with Alice’s
eyes and mouth came in.
“ Do you want anything, father ? ”
“No ; are you going to stay with me ? ”
“ Mother wants me ; I am helping her.’
“ What is she doing ? ”
“ Making a dress —a black one,” she says ; “ and
I’m to have one too, soon.”
“ Are you ? Run |iway, then."
So she was impatient—poor Jenny ; and he had
kept her waiting two whole months —almost a
third.
Presently Jenny came in. He found himself
thinking how well she would look in black, and
he almost wished he could be there to see k her.
She would need more color, though ; but that
would come —afterwards !
One —two —three days went by. On the fourth,
he called her to him. “You can wear your black
dress to-morrow, Jenny,” he said.
And she did.
ALBIN DE MIRIAN.
An Adelaide paper swears to the truth of
this story, and, if it isn’t true, it certainly ought
to be. A stout German lady and her daughter
came in from the country and put up at a hotel.
They were leaving by an early train in the morn*
ing, and had to rise before daybreak in conse-
quence. Mother was large and masterful, and the
fraulein was a subdued girl, evidently in an excel*
lent state of discipline. In the grey dusk of the
morning, the German mother, cold and embittered
by early rising, strode into her daughter’s room and
demanded why she wasn’t up, and, to her amaze*
ment, her carefully-trained miss replied curtly
that the interfering old woman had better get out.
She was ordered to rise immediately and repent
of her sins while dressing, and a sleepy voice from
the bed replied with an unheard-of expression,
“Rats!” Then the infuriated German lady
resolved that her daughter was possessed by
an evil spirit, and, whisking the clothes off the
bed, she got hold of the sinner, and laid into the
soft part of her with a shoe until the shouting and
objurgating victim was a bright blush color
from concussion. When it was too late
she discovered that she had been attacking
the barmaid and that her own daughter was
up and dressed in the adjacent room. The ques-
tion of compensation was settled privately, when
the German mother recovered from her hysterics.

The Ports of the Open Sea.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Down here where the ships loom large in
The gloom when the sea-storms veer,
Down here on the south-west margin
Of the western hemisphere,
Where the might of a world-wide ocean
Round a foreign land rolls free—
Storm bound from the world’s commotion,
Lie the Ports of the Open Sea.
Ly the bluff where the grey sand reaches
To the kerb of the spray-swept street,
By the sweep of the black sand beaches
From the main-road travellers’ feet,
By the heights like a work Titanic,
Begun ere the gods’ work ceased,
By a bluff-lined coast volcanic
Lie the ports of the wild south-east.
By the steeps of the snoft-capped ranges,
By the scarped and terraced hills—
Far away from the swift life-changes,
From the wear of the strife that kills—
Where the land in the Spring seems younger
Than a land of the earth might be—
Oh ! the hearts of the rivers hunger
For the Ports of the Open Sea.
But the captains watch and hearken
For a sign of the South Sea wrath—
Let the face of the south-east darken,
And they turn to the ocean path.
Ay, the sea-boats dare not linger,
Whatever the cargo be ;
When the south-east lifts a finger
They make for the open sea.
South by the bleak Bluff faring,
North where the Three Kings’ wait,
South-east through the tempest, daring
Their flight through the storm-tossed strait;
But away from the grey-fog, leeward,—
From the graves of the “Fatal T.,”—
Where the black-ribbed reefs run seaward
By the Ports of the Open Sea.
1 or the south-east lands are dread lands
To the sea-dogs in the shrouds,
Where the low clouds loom like headlands,
And the black bluffs blur like clouds.
When the breakers rage to windward
And the lights are masked a-lee,
And the sunken rocks run inward
To a Port of the Open Sea.
Yonder a white-winged roamer
Struck where the rollers roar—
Where the great green froth-flaked comber
T alls crash on a black-ribbed shore.
Ah ! the coaster’s captain brave is
Who sails when the white bars moan—
Over yonder a lonely grave is
Marked with the word “ Unknown.”
But oh ! for the South-East weather—•
The sweep of the three-days’ gale—
When, far through the flax and heather,
The spindrift drives like hail.
Glory to man’s creations
That drive where the gale grows gruff,
When the homes of the sea-coast stations
Flash white from the dark’ning bluff.
When the swell of the south-east rouses
The wrath of the Maori sprite,
And the brown folk flee their houses
And crouch in the flax by night,
And wait as they long have waited
In fear as the brown folk be
The wave of destruction fated
For the Ports of the Open Sea.
Grey cloud to the mountain bases,
Wild boughs that rush and sweep;
On the rounded hills the tussocks
Like flocks of flying sheep ;
A lonely storm-bird soaring
O’er tussock fern and tree ;
And the boulder beaches roaring
The Hymn of the Open Sea.
M.L., Sept., ’97.
HENRY LAWSON.
Lovely Woman is Peculiar.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE LADIES’ COMPARTMENT.
“Yes,” said the stationmaster, meditatively;
it s peculiar, ain’t it ? Here you see the ladies’
compartment—northern mails, western mails,
southern mails—go empty every day; and the
blessed women crowding into the rest of the train
like sheep into trucks in the middle of the season.
Why ? I don’t know. Here’s a compartment
specially set apart for them ; no man allowed in—
and only this evening I saw two come up: ‘l’ll
get in here,’ says one. ‘ Oh, no,’ says the other,
‘ that’s ladies l 1 ‘So it is,’ says the first one; ‘I
didn’t notice.’ And then she went and crowded
into a mixed compartment with five gents-
blessed if she didn’t—and going all the way to
Bourke. The ladies’ compartment went off empty
—it does seven times in ten. You’d think they’d
like a carriage to themselves, wouldn’t you -goina-
alone on a long journey ? But they don’t.
Second-class don’t seem to object so much •
nrst-class pretty nearly always object, so far as I
see. Berhaps it’s the babies—women with babies
do travel in the ladies’' sometimes ; and the toffs
hate babies like poison. But as often as not
you 11 see the woman and the babies in a ‘ mixed ’
tumbling over the gents, and an empty compart-
ment next door. At Easter, when the trains
were packed, two gents came along late one night,
.there was a lavatory compartment with six men
in, a lady, and a little boy—and the ‘ first ladies’ ’
empty, as usual, I went and asked the lady
civilly if shed move out-told her she’d have
more room, and so on. ‘Oh, no,’ she says ‘I
prefer to remain here ! ’ and the two gents had to
take seats in different carriages. They wanted to
go into the empty ‘ladies,’ but we’re not allowed
to do that, bo it goes empty, as I say.

and often she did; but now we can put 'em out if

1 forget exactly where—a long night ride, any-
way ; and she came and fixed herself in the
smoker bright and early. Up comes eight com-

something at the time about being recommended,
but I didn t quite hear. She got on verv well ”

°f a needle makes her yell. You say you didn’t

look in mouth. ‘ Bad tooth. Open a little, please,
A very bad tooth—open a little more, please. Ah,

near her mouth : ‘ no, 1 I say ; ‘too large.’ Then
as if speaking to myself, I add: ‘ And yet this
easily went into Lady Jersey’s mouth. 1 After a

remark. Let me see—l’ve fully a hundred of
these instruments; but to get 'a small enough
one . Oh, yes, Lady Carington’s is the one. I
took a tooth from her years ago, and haven’t used
the instrument since, 1 My patient sits as quiet as.
a lamb all this time. I pick up a baby tool that,
you could get into the mouth of a bird and
flourish it about. Then I slip the large double-
toother into her mouth, and away comes the two-
inch molar.”

CORSAIR.
A MATTER OF SEX.
“A girl never willingly marries her doctor,”
said Mrs. John Manley to me over a cup of after-
noon tea. We had been discussing social pheno-
mena. Mrs. John Manley is a sort of society
Pythoness, and the depth of her knowledge is
Atlantic-like. The remark seemed to indicate a
finger-post at the cross-roads of sexual philosophy,
and I waited patiently for developments.
. The sexes are diametrically opposed on that
point, continued Madame. “ A man frequently
marries his narse. Indeed, the fact that she has
been his nurse mostly predisposes him to marry
Ch* the other hand, to a girl of any sensi-
bility, the idea is usually repugnant.”
i 0 ou so ar as to say, now,” I put in,
that a woman doesn’t willingly marry the man
she has nursed ? ”
Mrs. John Manley sipped her tea before reply-
ing. “My sex is not without its share of incon-
sistency,” she finally remarked, without a smile.
‘‘ Put you adhere to your original proposition ? ”
With a few exceptions. They prove the rule.
My sister’s case is only one of several I have met
with in a large experience of—undeveloped en-
gagements. Minnie was in love with a very nice
young man, a young doctor who had just obtained
his diploma. I am convinced that she was very
fond of him ; I have no doubt of that whatever.
They had been engaged about a year when
Minnie, who bikes, fractured her knee-cap.
Richard ”
“ The nice young man ? ”
4 Exactly; pleaded hard to be allowed to attend
“ er - .If was thought that she would prefer to be
in his charge than in that of a stranger, and he
was called in. He proved a devoted physician, of
course, and, I am sure, paid more attention to
Minnie than he did to any of his other patients.
Perhaps they weren’t, numerous ; at any rate, I
believe he came to our house at least three times a
day. Minnie progressed rapidly, and, in time,
recovered completely. But, after that episode,
her feelings towards Richard changed unmis-
takably. In fact, she speedily became averse to
his very presence. She confessed to me one day
that since he had attended her as a doctor the
idea of marrying him had become repugnant to
her. And, as a matter of fact, the engagement
was broken off a month later.”
Mrs. John Manley concluded triumphantly, and
I drank weak tea in profound silence.
Can you suggest any explanation ? ” I pre-
sently inquired, tentatively.
“A woman, my dear sir,” observed Mrs. John
Manley, “objects to being anticipated.”

Is the Cause of very many of the Ids to which Flesh is Heir.
Ayer’s Sarsaparilla purifies, vitalizes and enriches the Blood, as testified
to by Mr. W. J. Pickering, Storekeeper, Breeza, New South Wales,
who sends his portrait and tells the following interesting story;

“In the year 1890 my ankle broke out, and I went to a doctor who told
me I was suffering from blood poisoning. He prescribed for me but did me
no good, and I tried several other physicians without benefit. I went into
St. Vincent’s Hospital, Sydney, and after a month’s treatment there came
out worse than ever, feeling greatly discouraged. I suffered terribly for
four years, until my wife, hearing of your Sarsaparilla, got me a bottle.

AN EXTRACT.
“ The first night I had slept a little from sheer exhaustion. The
next was terrible. I vomited incessantly; I was chilled to the bone, and
could not warm myself. I suffered horrible pain in the head, and through-
out my whole body. Next day, of course, I was a wreck. They told me I
had been delirious half the night. I remember nothing of the day; but
next night I got a couple of hours’ sleep, and was rather better in conse-
quence. But vomiting went on steadily, and the fourth night I was again
delirious. The next day I was as bad as ever; but, thanks to a good
dose of Beecliam’s Fills, i had an excellent
night, and I truly believe that they saved my life. I knew well where I
picked up this fever—in the marshes of Mwenzo, and near Kituta—and the
killing thing was being unable to stop to cure it; the ten days on the lake
especially—often without food for twenty-four hours—nearly finished me.
I felt certain at the time that I was done for; and I very nearly was.”— See
“ Three Years in Savage Africa ,” by Lionel Decle—Methuen & Go., London.
Mr. Lionel Decle covered a distance of over 7000 miles at one
stretch, between Capetown on the South and Mombassa on the East coast of
Africa. It is worth noting that this “unique journey ” would probably
have been cut short; and, to quote the introduction by Mr. H. M. Stanley,
M.P., “ its vivid record ” would never have been penned had not Mr.
Lionel Decle provided himself with a supply of
BCECNAM’S PILLS.
